THE
PARLIAMENTARY DEBATES
(OFFICIAL REPORT)
IN THE
FIFTH SESSION OF THE FIFTH PARLIAMENT OF NORTHERN IRELAND
APPOINTED TO MEET AT STORMONT THE THIRD DAY OF FEBRUARY, IN THE
SIXTH YEAR OF THE REIGN OF
HIS MAJESTY KING
THE SENATE.
GEORGE VI.
VOLUME XXV.

---

Tuesday, 3rd February, 1942.
The Fifth Session of the Fifth Parliament of Northern Ireland appointed to
meet on 3rd February, 1942, in the Sixth
year of the Reign of King George VI, was
opened by the Governor (His Grace the
Duke of Abercorn, K.G., K.P.) in
person.
The House met at Five Minutes before
Twelve of the Clock noon, Mr. SPEAKER
(The Right Hon. Viscount Bangor,
O.B.E., D.L.) in the Chair.
KING'S SPEECH.
His Grace the Governor, being seated
on the Throne, commanded the acting
Gentleman Usher of the Black Rod
(Brigadier-General H. G. Young, C.I.E.,
D.S.O.) to let the Commons know "It
is His Grace's pleasure that they attend
him immediately in this House."
The Commons being at the Bar with
their Speaker, his Grace delivered the
following speech from the Throne:-
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
The war has entered upon its third
year, and the people of Northern
Ireland, in common with their fellow
citizens in Great Britain and the
Empire, face the tasks and ordeals of
the future with resolution, confidence
and courage.
On the recent arrival of American
troops in Northern Ireland my Government extended a cordial welcome to all
ranks. I see in this historic event a
new demonstration of unity of purpose
between the British Empire and the
United States of America, and I am
confident that the result will be a
further strengthening of those ties of
kinship and those common ideals which
mean so much for the future of the
world.
I have learned with deep satisfaction
of the close co-operation existing
between the Civil Government and all
branches of His Majesty's Forces.
This cordial and harmonious relationship, invaluable in time of peace, is
indispensable in dealing with the special
problems created by a state of war.
Splendid and self-sacrificing help was
given to the authorities and the civil
population during and after the savage
air attacks on part of our area last
spring. This noble service, rendered so
ardently and in such abundant measure,
won unstinted admiration and praise.
The behaviour of the Civil Defence
Services under most arduous war conditions was exemplary, and the moral
of the people, unbroken by trial and
suffering, illuminated a dark and tragic
period in our history. I am glad to
know that further measures have been
developed for the defence and protection of our citizens.
Whilst thousands of our men and
women have volunteered for service in
A
King's Speech
King's Speech
THE SENATE
His Majesty's Forces, it is my fervent
hope that the demand for more recruits
for all branches of the fighting services
will be met in ever increasing numbers,
and that those whose duties are indispensable to war production will join
either the Ulster Home Guard or the
Civil Defence organisations, which are
vitally necessary for the maintenance of
a strong Home Front.
The Ulster Home Guard and Special
Constabulary continue to perfect their
organisation and training under military direction, and I am confident that
all ranks will be ready to meet any
emergency which may arise.
Our farmers have made a striking
contribution to the demand for increased food production, and approximately five hundred and thirty
thousand acres of old grassland have
been ploughed up since the commencement of hostilities, involving the
payment to farmers of over one million
pounds. I am glad to note the continued success of the scheme for the
improvement of agricultural holdings
and to learn that during the past year
more than thirteen thousand applications for financial assistance were
approved and over one hundred and six
thousand pounds were paid by way of
grants. A gratifying response has also
been made to the appeal for an increase
in the acreage under flax, and I trust
that the agricultural community,
realising the extent to which the
fighting services are dependent upon
flax for essential parts of their equipment, will redouble their efforts as a
public duty and make a still greater
contribution in the present year.
My Ministers have given unremitting
attention to the problem of the
mechanisation of farming. Since the
outbreak of war over two thousand
eight hundred agricultural tractors and
flax pullers have been made available,
and, through the machinery of the
Government Hire Purchase Scheme,
equipment to the value of two hundred
and fifty thousand pounds has been
distributed to farmers and tillage contractors. Over one million pounds
worth of machinery has been purchased
for use on our farms in Northern Ireland since the war began.
Steps are being taken to ensure that,
as far as possible, an adequate number
of workers will be available to assist our
farmers in harvesting the crops during
the coming year.
The Reports of the Drainage Commission were laid before you last year
and you will be invited to consider, at
an early date, proposals based on the
recommendations of the Commission for
bringing additional land under
cultivation.
I am gratified by the extent to which
the industrial capacity of Northern
Ireland is being increasingly utilised on
war work, and also by the reduction in
the number of unemployed persons
during the year ended December last
from sixty-nine thousand six hundred
and forty-three to twenty-eight
thousand one hundred and sixty-four.
My Government will continue to investigate fully every means by which
the energy and skill of our people can
be put to still greater use in the
national effort.
All practicable steps will continue to
be taken, in co-operation with the local
authorities, for the health and wellbeing of our people. Arrangements are
being made for the maintenance of
hospital and medical services under
emergency conditions, and I am pleased
to learn that it has been found possible,
through a revision of the derating
grants in rural areas, to give increased
financial assistance to local authorities,
which will enable them to provide
essential services on an improved
standard, particularly in relation to
public health matters.
The question of juvenile delinquency
under war-time conditions is receiving
attention, and I trust that the steps
which it is proposed to take to deal with
this problem will have beneficial results.
The War Savings Campaign, promoted and
and inspired by so many
enthusiastic voluntary workers, continues to receive a large and growing
measure of support. More than thirtyfour million pounds have been invested
and lent to the Imperial Government
since November, 1939.
Among the measures which will be
laid before you will be Bills providing
for the extinction of the outstanding
debt of the Unemployment Fund
amounting to over two million pounds,
and also for a substantial reduction,
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
3 FEBRUARY 1942
during the period of the war, in the
contributions payable by local authorities towards the cost of unemployment
assistance.
You will be invited to consider proposals for extending the civil bill
jurisdiction of the County Courts from
fifty to one hundred pounds.
Members of the House of Commons.
The Estimates for the Public Services
will be laid before you in due course.
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
My Government continues to give
close attention to the welfare of our
workpeople employed in industry, particularly in undertakings engaged upon
war work. It is gratifying to know
that an increasing number of firms are
establishing canteens for the provision
of hot meals at reasonable charges.
The extensive evacuation of women
and children which took place last year
placed heavy demands on the numerous
organisations concerned with their
welfare, and the self-sacrifice, cheerfulness and adaptability displayed under
abnormal and trying conditions deserve
the highest praise. Notwithstanding
many difficulties, no effort is being
spared to ensure the education of all
school children, and the teachers, as a
body, have responded readily to all the
calls made upon them. The provision
of free milk to expectant mothers and
young children has proved most
beneficial.
Our schools continue to discharge
their functions with efficiency and to
give all possible assistance to the war
effort. The secondary and technical
schools have formed units of the Air
Training Corps, and many members
of the Services have joined evening
instructional classes. I am glad also to
find that numbers of our young men
and women have taken up University
training courses with a view to entering
the Armed Forces or specialised
branches of war industry.
It is a source of much satisfaction to
me that my Government has, for some
consideration
time, given
to the
question of post-war reconstruction,
particularly in relation to housing,
town and country planning, sewage
disposal and the provision of water
supplies throughout Northern Ireland.
The services of an expert have been
secured to make a preliminary investigation into the problems arising out of
the replanning of Belfast and the
surrounding district, and a Technical
Committee has been appointed to advise
the Government on the provision of
adequate water and sewerage facilities.
I pray that the blessing of Almighty
God may rest upon your deliberations.
Then his Grace was pleased to retire
and the Commons withdrew.
House adjourned during pleasure.
House resumed.
Papers Presented to Parliament.
Ministry of Commerce:
Report of the Industrial Assurance
Commissioner for Northern Ireland for
the year ended 31st December, 1941.
Ministry of Finance:
Accounts of Capital Receipts and Payments and of Earnings on Capital
Advances or Investments for the year
ended 31st March, 1941.
Ministry of Labour:
Unemployment Insurance (Emergency
Powers) (Amendment) Regulations (Northern Ireland), -942.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Address in Reply to His Majesty's Most
Gracious Speech.
Mr. SPEAKER having read the
King's Speech,
Mr. STEVENSON (in morning dress):
I beg to move, That an Humble Address be
presented to His Grace in the following
words:
Your Grace,
We, His Majesty's dutiful and loyal
subjects the Senate of the Parliament
of Northern Ireland beg leave to offer
our humble thanks to your Grace for
the Speech which your Grace has
addressed to both Houses of Parliament.
In being entrusted by the Government
to undertake this important task I realise
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
THE SENATE
[Mr. Stevenson]
that an honour has been conferred on my
native County Tyrone-an honour which
I fully appreciate. The opening of this
new Session finds our country still at war,
and unlike the southern portion of Ireland
known as Eire, we here in Northern
Ireland are all in it. We have taken our
stand with the other outposts of the
Empire and are fully determined to see it
through. I am glad to be able to say
that the people of this area are playing
their part in factories, workshops and
farms, as well as in the fighting and
auxiliary services, and are contributing
in no small way to the national effort.
Many thousands of men are working
from dawn to dusk, and are also serving
on the Home Guard and other branches
of Civil Defence Forces.
Thousands of women are serving in His
Majesty's Auxiliary Forces and those who
must stay at home are giving all their
spare time to first-aid work, canteen work
and entertaining the soldiers quartered
amongst us. They are all deserving of
the highest praise.
It is a matter of great satisfaction to
know that American troops have landed
in Northern Ireland. They will be a very
welcome addition to our fighting forces,
and it goes without saying that as our
visitors they will be treated by all with
courtesy, kindness and will receive that
hospitality for which Ireland is famed.
As a member of the Senate I wholeheartedly approve of the stand taken by
the Prime Minister and the Government
of Northern Ireland on conscription, and
hold that its non-application to Northern
Ireland is placing us in a very unfavourable position with our friends in Great
Britain. I hope the time is not far
distant when this reproach will be removed
and we shall be privileged to take our
place with the other members of the
British Empire.
As British citizens we claim all the
rights of that citizenship and are prepared to accept all its responsibilities.
There ought to be, and I hope will be, a
full and determined effort made by everyone who is anxious to see right triumph
over might, and thus hasten that happy
day when peace, based on justice and
liberty, shall be firmly established
amongst all nations.
I am glad to see by the Gracious Speech
that the number of our unemployed has
been materially reduced. I feel, however, that not one person should be unemployed while there is so much national
work to be done, and I hope the Ministry
of Labour will continue to use the manpower and women-power available to push
on with the national effort wherever this
can be used to the best advantage.
Reference has also been made in the
Gracious Speech to the great part that
agriculture has played and is playing in
the struggle. Every demand that has
been made on the agricultural community
has been responded to nobly, and this in
spite of the great difficulties with which
the farmers have been faced through
adverse weather conditions and shortage
of agricultural labour and the necessary
machinery. I would urge on the Government the necessity of making full provision for sufficient labour and machinery
to be available in the coming season. This
Our
is a matter that brooks no delay.
food front is a most important one and
no effort must be spared to see that
farmers' requirements are fully catered
for.
As one whose interests are bound up
in
the linen trade I would like to see a large
increase in the acreage under flax. Owing
to the war now in the East and increasing
transport shortage from the West, this
fibre is getting more and more necessary
to the war effort, and, in addition, it
would keep our great industry moving
and lessen unemployment. I trust the
Government with the whole-hearted cooperation of the industry, which should
be forthcoming, will make an appeal at
once to the farmers, at the same time
guaranteeing them a fair price and the
necessary seed in good time for sowing.
Regarding potatoes and other root
crops and cereals, subsidies and prices
should in all justice to the farmer be
announced at once-(hear, hear)-and
thus give good time for the necessary seed
and fertilisers to be procured. The
Gracious Speech refers to the reports of
the Drainage Commission and it is hoped.
that steps will be taken quickly to deal
with unnecessary flooding. Apart altogether from main drainage schemes, it is
obvious to everyone going through the
country that thousands of acres are
rendered useless through the open
King's Speech---
Address in Reply

ech
has

an

•
drains on the farms not being systematically cleared. County councils should
be urged to put the powers which they
already possess in this connection into
full operation.
The measures outlined in the Gracious
Speech for the extinction of the outstanding debt of the Unemployment Fund and
for the reduction of the contributions
payable by local authorities towards the
cost of unemployment assistance will be
heartily welcomed. The Bill for extending the civil bill jurisdiction of the county
court meets a much-needed reform and
has the approval of the legal profession,
chambers of commerce, public bodies and
the public generally. I feel that the programme outlined by the Government
should eventually lead to that era of peace
and prosperity which we all long for and
which every individual should hasten to
attain. I beg to move.
Mr. WHALEY (in morning dress): I
rise to second this Address of Thanks for
the Gracious Speech from the Throne.
I do so with diffidence, seeing that it is
the first time I have spoken in the Senate
Chamber. I sincerely thank the learned
Leader of the House for this privilege,
and I take it, besides being a compliment
to myself, as a compliment to the County
Fermanagh, from which I and another
hon. Senator come. It is gratifying to
think that, although we are at the circumference, we are not forgotten at the
centre.
The subject matter of the Address,
which forecasts many needed reforms, has
been fully and exhaustively dealt with by
the hon. Senator, the mover, who has
left very little for me to say.
I should
like, however, to endorse all he has said
about the warmth of feeling that exists
here and all over Northern Ireland at the
landing of the American troops on our
shores.
I understand that many of them
have family ties with the Province. They
will find by experience, before they are
long in our midst, something of Ulster's
proverbial hospitality. I should say in
passing that this friendly feeling exists
in a very marked degree between the
civil population and our own home forces.
As has been pointed out by Senator
Stevenson, our two great democracies,
with others similarly minded, are engaged
in a fight for the freedom of the world,
and it is reassuring that in the days of
peace that will follow, their co-operation
will be a sure guarantee of peace in the
days to come.
The farming community is also referred
to in the Gracious Speech. As farming is
our principal industry, it is satisfactory to
know that the acreage under crops last
year had substantially increased, and
that with grants-in-aid and mechanisation farming appears to have turned the
corner. It is to be hoped that the
Government will see that farmers in
future are fully rewarded for their
labours. The output of our farms wa
never more urgently needed than it is at
the present time and will be in the
future.
A reference is also made in the Gracious
Speech to youthful delinquency. This
lawlessness which has arisen at an alarming rate will doubtless receive most careful consideration by the Government, as
it has an important bearing on the future
of our Province.
We are passing through anxious times,
and it behoves every one of us to do his
or her part, no matter how small, in
assisting the Government in the war
effort. It is satisfactory to see that
although conscription, which, after all,
is the fairest way of recruiting, is not in
force in Northern Ireland, the voluntary
system, which is continually growing, has
called many thousands of our young men
to the colours. Those in authority,
especially the War Cabinet, are called
upon to make
decisions. Let us
grave
hope and pray they will be guided aright,
and lead us all with stout hearts and
strong faith in Almighty God, and in the
justice of
cause, so that we shall
emerge victorious from the conflict. As
the war cannot be carried on without
money, let us lend to the Government
and go on assisting the different funds at
present in operation, and when we get
our income-tax notices let us
promptly and without a frown.
our
pay
Mr. ROBB: I am sure I am voicing
the opinion of all the Members of the
Senate when I say we have listened to two
very interesting speeches, well conceived,
well constructed, full of matter, and
admirably delivered. I must confess
there seemed to be little pabulum in the
King's Speech, but the move and
seconder have developed their themes.
King's Speech---
Address in Reply
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
from small beginnings to eloquent conclusions. There are, in fact, apart from
the immediate subject of the war, very
few topics mentioned in the King's
Speech. The mover and seconder have
endorsed the hearty welcome given in this.
country to the American troops, and they
have emphasised the good work that has
been done voluntarily by all branches of
His Majesty's Services in this area.
have made one or two comments which I
shall be glad to pass on to the appropriate
department.
They
The mover was emphatic on the necessity for further development of flax in
our area. None of us can appeal too
strongly to the farmers to do even more
than they have done in the past, but no
one can disguise or conceal from himself the
difficulties under which the farmers last
year found themselves, especially those
farmers who had sown flax. We hope
nature will be more propitious in the
coming season. In that hope the Government, and everyone interested in the
progress of our war effort, will join in
urging the farmers to do all they can to
help, no matter how far their previous
results have been disappointing. The
mover also made a point which I will
convey to the appropriate authority, that
it would be a great advantage if the
farm produce
prices for
produce could
be
announced early. Having lived all my
life in the town I am not, of course, quite
so familiar with the necessity for that as
other people may be, but I can see the
importance of it and I shall have it conveyed to the proper authority.
With regard to the other subjects mentioned in the Gracious Speech, may I just
say a word about juvenile delinquency.
There has been a spate of talk about
this matter, and it has been alleged there
has been a great growth of juvenile
delinquency since the war began. That
is partly true and partly not true. The
figures for the last two years of persons
under the age of 18 charged and convicted
were lower than they were in the year
1937, but although the total figures were
lower there are two aspects of the matter
which are serious. The number of indictable offences has proportionately increased,
and the number of charges against girls
has greatly increased. These are disquieting facts. People write to the ne 78papers and suggest this and that as the
cause.
So far as I personally am concerned there are two things I would like
to say.
First of all, it has to be remembered
that the activities of the police in the
detection of crime are progressively increasing. I cannot give the exact figures,
but during the period of, say, the last
twenty years I think it will be found that
of the total number of cases where crime
has been committed a larger proportion
than formerly have been cases where some
person has been made amenable. Whereas
the number of cases in which persons,
both adult and juvenile, have been made
amenable has grown, so we find that that
tendency has been reflected in the case of
juveniles, and we have had more juveniles
prosecuted, but not more in proportion to
the general average of cases in which
persons have been made amenable.
As to the root cause of this trouble,
people have different ideas, but I think
all of us will agree that in recent years
the exercise of parental authority has
diminished, and that diminution has been
reflected in lapses from honesty by
children. I do not know what the
Government can do to help that. It is a
matter for the people themselves. Some
people might possibly say it is a direct
charge on the churches, but until
greater measure of parental authority is
restored I am satisfied we shall not have
any great improvement in the numbers of
juveniles made amenable for offences
against the law.
on
a
now
With regard to the Bills that are to
come before Parliament in the present
Session, there are only three mentioned
in the Gracious Speech. The first is the
Bill to extinguish the outstanding debt
the Unemployment Fund,
amounting to over £2,000,000. In the
year 1926 the outstanding debt upon the
Unemployment Insurance Fund was
carried to Suspense Account, and it then
stood at some £2,400,000. Bit by bit
something has been paid off that until at
present the debt stands at something
round about £2,000,000. Moneys having
become available it is proposed to wipe
out, with the sanction of the Imperial
Treasury, that debt, and to get rid of it
once and for all.
Another Measure will provide for a
substantial reduction during the period
of the war in the contribution payable
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
3 FEBRUARY 1942
as
by local authorities towards the cost of
Unemployment Assistance. In 1937, by
an Act of our Parliament, the contribution to be made by local authorities
towards Unemployment Assistance.
distinguished from Unemployment Insurance was fixed at a sum of roughly
£52,000 a year. The consent of the
Imperial Treasury has been obtained for
a modification of the arrangement then
made, with the result that the contribution will be lessened by about £40,000 a
year, a very great relief. At the same
time I must point out that it is a relief
only for the period of the war. Employment has increased and consequently
Unemployment Assistance has diminished
and as employment increased people were
transferred from Unemployment Assistance to Unemployment Insurance.
The third Bill is one that has been
welcomed by the mover of the Motion for
the Address. It provides for an extension of the civil bill jurisdiction of the
county court. That is a question that
has been agitating the legal profession,
chambers of commerce and other bodies
for a long time. After all, the county
court is the people's court, and since the
limited jurisdiction was fixed in 1877 the
value of money has greatly changed. It
is, therefore, now proposed that the civil
bill jurisdiction of the county courts
should be increased from £50 to £100.
That will certainly meet a popular demand
and I hope that, on the whole, it will be
beneficial to the people generally, however
the legal profession may regard it.
The Speech also refers to what happened
after the great evacuation from the raided
areas. It praises the efforts of all those
who had to take charge of any branch of
the welfare scheme. There is one phrase
used which perhaps might lead to a little
misunderstanding, which I would like to
correct. It says:
Notwithstanding many difficulties, no
effort is being spared to ensure the
education of all school-children, and the
teachers, as a body, have responded
readily to all the calls made upon them.
It was pointed out to me that the expression "teachers, as a body," might
mean that there were some teachers who
did not respond. That was not the intention. It means teachers one and all.
As regards the question of post-war
reconstruction, the Government has had
Nobody yet
that under consideration.
knows how our population will be distributed after the war, but if there is to be
any dispersal of population throughout the
country areas one thing absolutely necessary is that there should be a proper
water supply and sanitation, and the
Government is getting advice about the
possibilities regarding that matter. As
to whether it will be possible or advisable
I am not yet in a position to give an
opinion, but I know there is a large body
of opinion which thinks it is desirable
that the concentration of industries in
Belfast should be dispersed. You cannot
have that dispersal in any large measure
unless you are assured of a proper water
supply. That is one of the questions upon
which the Government hopes to get expert
advice, and upon which the Government
hopes to bring forward proposals when the
time is ripe.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I would like to
associate myself with the expression of
sympathy voiced by the Leader of the
House in having called upon the mover
and seconder of the Address to commend
a skeleton and futile document to the
people of Northern Ireland. I have said
on previous occasions that I have had
great sympathy with those Members who
have been called upon to move and
second the Address in reply to former
Speeches from the Throne. On this
occasion I have even greater sympathy
with the mover and seconder, because I
think this is the most futile and impotent
Speech that has yet been delivered to
this House.
However, while sympathising with the
mover and seconder, I join gladly in congratulating them on so well doing the
only thing they could do, namely, entertaining us to very beautifully phrased
speeches. They have been called upon to
undertake what I think is the most
difficult task a public representative can
be called upon to perform, and that is to
take the floor and make a speech upon
nothing. They have adequately carried
out that duty. Having said that, I
whole-heartedly condemn this Speech,
and I say there should have been a very
constructive Speech placed before Parliament. There is no justification or excuse
for that skeleton we are asked to accept
to-day.
Reference has been made to the decrease
in unemployment. I do not say the
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
Leader of the House would deliberately
misrepresent anything, but I do say that
the alleged reduction in unemployment is
a misrepresentation of the real position.
in Northern Ireland to-day. We are
told that unemployment has been reduced
from, I think, 60,000 to 29,000. In
theory that may be true, but in fact it
is not a correct statement of the position.
We have now approximately 29,000 unemployed in Northern Ireland after
almost two and a half years of war.
We
have another 30,000 men, women and
children in Northern Ireland driven from
their homes, expelled from their native
towns and country and compelled-in
fact conscripted to go to England for
employment there-breaking up their
homes in Northern Ireland.
I have no hesitation in saying that that
state of affairs would not have existed if
we had had an active, efficient, capable,
or even half-competent Government, in
Northern Ireland during the past twenty
years. If we had had a Government in
power performing its functions as any
good Government should; if it had consulted opinion outside Parliament instead
of trying to address itself to political
propaganda, I have no hesitation in saying that those 29,000 unemployed people
in Northern Ireland to-day would be in remunerative employment, and those 30,000
men and women who have been driven
from their homes and country would be
earning their living in Northern Ireland
to-day.

wonder?
Mr. McALLISTER: I could not help
remarking that the mover and seconder
of the Motion for the Address said they
represented Fermanagh and Tyrone.
When the Government are, so to speak,
in a difficulty about getting a Speech
commended to this House they go to those
two partitioned counties with their overwhelming Nationalist majorities to get
them out of their quagmire and difficulty.
Surely there was never a greater exposure
of the fallacy and injustice of gerrymandering than the fact that the two
Nationalist counties of the Six Counties,
with overwhelming Nationalist majorities,
are called upon to be the spokesmen of
the Northern Government.

I have little to say with regard to the
speech of the Leader of the House.
has given us a very interesting lecture,
because he had nothing else to deal with,
on the question of juvenile delinquency.
It has been very interesting and very
entertaining, but he did not deal with
unemployment in Northern Ireland. The
thought has occurred to me that surely
no responsible citizen in this country, be
he public representative or otherwise, can
view otherwise than with dismay what is
the possibility in these Six Counties when
peace comes and when this war is over.
I think we are all agreed there is bound
to be absolute disorganisation as an aftermath of this war. A great many of our
workers have been in munition factories,
and munition factories come to an end when
peace comes. How are these thousands of
thousands of munition workers to be
absorbed into profitable employment?
Again, numbers have joined the fighting
forces. How are they to be absorbed into
profitable employment? I have no hesitation in saying that it was the first and
most elementary duty of any responsible
Government to take steps to deal with
that horrible outlook which faces us, not
only in this country but in other countries
after this war. Yet in the Speech there
is only a passing reference.
After twenty years experience of the
Northern Government, I have no hesitation in saying that they themselves cannot
engineer a means whereby this tragic state
of affairs is to be met and remedied, but
they can invite assistance from those outside Parliament or in Parliament, wherever
they can get them, to assist in considering
how this terrible, tragic situation is to be
effectively met. There is only a passing
reference in the King's Speech to the
future tragedy of this country if this state.
of affairs is allowed by the Government to
continue.
On the larger constitutional question
which has been referred to, I personally
have always taken the view that as a
public representative I should not voice
my individual view. I should have the
resolution or the finding of the organisation, under whose authority I am elected
as a public representative, before expressing any private views upon large national
and constitutional issues. Therefore,
holding these views all my public life, I
intend at the moment not to make any
reference to the speeches that have been

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
would like to compliment the proposer
and seconder of the Address of Thanks
to the Governor for the Gracious Speech.
I really must admit that the speeches were
very well considered, very fine, and very
well delivered. I do not think I have
heard much better in this House, and for
my Friend, Senator McAllister, to offer
sympathy-I think he must be bubbling
over with sympathy when he extended it
in a case like this. It is very kind of
him, but I think it is misplaced. I would
like to see him offering it in cases where it
is much more deserved.
To come to the Speech, I feel it is a
very lengthy one and covers a great deal
of matter that deserves careful consideration. It is a war-time Speech, and it is
making reference to the war all through,
urging and encouraging the citizens to do
all they possibly can to prosecute the war
to a successful conclusion, and at the same
time promising legislation to provide for
and rectify the disasters that have
occurred as a result of the war in this
particular part of the country, that is,
Belfast, in housing and town planning.
I certainly think there is ample provision.
for post-war work, and I am glad to see
the Government are taking the matter up
early and endeavouring to do their best
to see that something is done in that
direction. I am particularly pleased to
see reference made in the House again to
United States troops coming to Northern
Ireland. It is a wonderful thing. It is
splendid, and I was very pleased when I
found the Prime Minister had taken
prompt action to let the world at large
know that so far as the majority of the
citizens of Northern Ireland are concerned
they were truly welcome. We were very
pleased to have them amongst us. They
are related probably to our own ancestors
who went out there and did a wonderful
work in the United States of America.
Very many Presbyterians left Northern
Ireland and left their mark on the United
States of America.
Mr. MCALLISTER: And got independence.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: That
is so.
At the same time we are all inclined to be just as independent to-day.
We are not intending to be enslaved by
Hitler or any other person.
We are
resolved to defend ourselves and to keep
ourselves free. I was very glad that the
prompt action of the Prime Minister let
the world at large see how we make them
welcome. It is really good in a country
like ours probably threatened with
invasion. Up in this part of the country
we are not afraid to say whom we are
afraid of. In one part of the country
they say they are going to take steps to
prevent anyone invading them whether
the British Government or the German
people. But we in this part of the country
are concerned only with invasion of this
part of Ireland by the German hordes,
and we are very glad to have the American
troops here to assist us against invasion.
I further think we are very thankful
to have such a great man ruling the destinies of the British Commonwealth as Mr.
Winston Churchill, and it is very fortunate we have a man as President of the
United States like President Roosevelt.
We feel thankful that the President has
sent troops here to relieve ours to go
somewhere else. I hope they will be
treated well and that every opportunity
will be taken by the people of Northern
Ireland to show them how welcome they
are.
There are several matters in the King's
Speech I would like to refer to, although
it has been said there is nothing in it.
All practicable steps will continue to
be taken in co-operation with the local
authorities for the health and wellbeing of our people.
That is very important. Only recently
specialists held an inquiry into the affairs
of Belfast, and what was pointed out was
that there is a considerable amount of
overlapping. The Corporation are dealing with a great amount of health services
and doing a great deal of good work for
maternity and child welfare. All these
things are very important for workingclass people. But it is divided between
Belfast and the poor-law guardians.
think if the Government would do something in the way of removing the overlapping and straighten things out and
I
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
THE SENATE
[Mr. Joseph Cunningham]
place them under one body they would
then be able to take a greater interest in
providing such services as are necessary
for the well-being of the people of Belfast.
There is nothing more important than
providing for child welfare. I hope the
Government will be a little more liberal
in providing funds for reducing infantile
mortality.
I notice that everything is being done.
for the industrial workers and naturally
so, because while the man at the front is
very important and the man at sea is very
important and the man in the air is probably more important, those making
munitions are also very important in a
war such as this. It is an engineers' war,
dependent to a very considerable extent
on the skilled men in the workshops. I
wonder if the Government have not taken
some notice of the fact that transport in
Belfast is very bad, and not the fault of
the Corporation. Unfortunately the
Corporation were taken at a disadvantage.
They were intending to replace the tram
system by up-to-date buses when the war
broke out. Traffic is so great that it is
impossible to deal with it. A tremendous
amount of traffic is passing to and fro.
But there is one matter that the Government could have done something about,
that is, with reference to a ferry service
across the river. It has been promised for
a considerable time and nothing has been
done. Yet in the winter season a considerable number of men have been compelled to walk round from one side of the
river to the other. For the Government
to claim they are doing all they can for
the workers-well, they are hardly doing
just all they can. I advise them to take
immediate steps to see that a proper
service across the river is established to
relieve the trams and bus services and
enable the men to get much more easily
and more comfortably to work.
There is also reference made here, as I
said before, to dealing with the post-war
position. The Government have already,
I understand, brought in an expert to
consider town planning. I have always
been very much concerned about the
housing of the working-class people. I
remember many years ago being one of a
deputation to visit Dublin to meet the
Chief Secretary of that time, Mr. Macpherson, on the housing question. At
that time they had rather got the view of
so many houses to the acre and so on,
things that were impossible. I must say
the Corporation has taken the matter up
and dealt with it during peace times.
Unfortunately the war and the effect of
air raids have left the citizens of Belfast
in a deplorable condition. It is just
terrible. I would like to see the Government taking a very interested view of this
and deciding what class of house they
would recommend in future. Is it to be
a slated house again, as we have had in
the past? When incendiary bombs
dropped they went through and set fire
to it.
I notice that in Coventry they are
building houses with a good concrete
roof. Up to the present a good concrete
roof has prevented any damage when |
incendiary bombs have fallen. The
Government should look into this matter |
carefully and consider what they should
recommend, because it would be very
foolish to erect new houses with slated
roofs that are so easily penetrated by
incendiary bombs. Even if the war was
over to-morrow or in a month's time
there is no telling when more trouble
might break out, and it is a very good
thing to be prepared for the worst.
Town planning is, no doubt, very
necessary. Belfast is now in a very bad
state, not only as regards the workingclass houses but as regards business houses
in the city, a considerable number of
which have been demolished. With a
good town planning scheme it is possible.
that there might be a new and better
Belfast built. It is unfortunate that we
have been put to all this great expense,
but we might take advantage of this
opportunity by introducing a good town
planning scheme that will make the
Belfast of the future a city we shall all
be proud of.
Having been born in Belfast I have
always had a great pride in it, and I do
hope the Government will take steps to
put into operation a good town planning
scheme that will result in a more healthy
and better Belfast from every point of
view, with a better class of houses for
the workers. I sincerely hope the
Government is in earnest so far as housing and town planning are concerned, and
I have great pleasure in supporting the
Motion for the Address.

Mr. LESLIE: I listened with great
interest to the speeches delivered by the
mover and seconder of the Motion for the
Address, and I trust I may congratulate
them on the efficient manner in which
they discharged their task. We have
not heard their voices very often before
in this House. In one case we were informed it was a maiden speech, and we
can only express the hope that we shall
hear their voices more frequently in the
future.
Turning to the Speech itself, it has been
said there is nothing in it, but is there
not an undercurrent of suggestion that
this is no time for parochialism; that
Northern Ireland ought to put its full
weight into the war, and not split hairs
as to the branch of war service into which
that weight is being thrown. That is to
say, if you are able to reduce your unemployment figures in one year by
approximately 40,000-which is the figure
mentioned in the Gracious Speech-that
is in itself no mean achievement, and it
is unnecessary to inquire whether that
unemployment has been reduced wholly
owing to employment in Northern Ireland, or whether to some extent it was
reduced by employment across the water.
In either case the unemployment was
reduced with an effect which will help
the prosecution of the war. That, I
think, is the sort of thing which the
Gracious Speech invites us to consider.
I
There are one or two things mentioned
in the Speech to which I should like to
draw attention. We are told it is proposed to extinguish the debt on the Unemployment Fund, amounting at present,
according to the right hon. Gentleman,
the Leader of the House, to approximately £2,000,000. That is rather an
old godchild of my own. More than once
have suggested we should take
advantage of any good times—good
times being a reduction in unemployment
--to get rid of that debt. If I remember
rightly the arrangement we made was a
contribution of £40,000 a year.
thousand pounds a year is a considerable
sum, but it takes a good many £40,000
to extinguish £2,000,000. If that debt
is got rid of the way will be cleared for
enabling us to launch out after the war
if we do find ourselves in the lamentable
position of having to provide for a considerable increase in unemployment, as
was suggested by the hon. Senator on my
Forty
left (Mr. McAllister).
That reduction in the unemployment
debt is not the only reduction we have
recently effected. I do not know how
many of us saw the White Paper issued
a few days ago dealing with the position.
of the Loans Guarantee Fund. If I
remember rightly, under the Loans
Guarantee Acts we have advanced something over twenty-two and a half
millions, and the Loans Guarantee Acts
have not been a going concern for any
great length of time. But of that twentytwo and a half million pounds, according
to the White Paper, we have now less
than two and a half millions outstanding. That is a very great tribute to the
success which has attended the courage of
those responsible financial authorities who
advised the Government that that risk
might be suitably incurred and that it
would not cost the country very much
money. I think I am correct in saying
that the loss sustained has been well under
£40,000 and most of us in my own county
know where the bulk of that went to.
That is a matter for distinct congratulation.
I should like to support what was said
as regards the necessity for the early
announcement of prices by the Ministry
of Agriculture. Living as I do in a
country district, where there are many
small farmers, I know the clamour is
constant "Tell us what you are going to
pay for flax next year. Tell us what you
are going to pay for potatoes. A £10
subsidy on potatoes is no use to us." Some
of them want £50, but I do not think
they open their mouths quite as wide as
that, because they do not expect to get
£50. But they want to know whether
they are going to get £15 or £20.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Or nothing.
Mr. LESLIE Or nothing, although I
cannot agree that that is the case. But
we do want that announcement of prices.
Something has been said about the extension of civil bill jurisdiction. That again
is a godchild of mine. I put a question
last year on this subject, but the right
hon. Gentleman did not tell us whether
the proposal is that the extent of the
jurisdiction should be permanently increased, or whether it is merely a wartime Measure.
Mr. ROBB: Permanently.

Mr. LESLIE: It will be remembered
that the original petition put forward by
the Incorporated Law Society was that
the increase should have effect for the
duration of the war, and I am sure the
members of that society will be only too
glad to hear that it is proposed to make

was said about town planning, and the
question was raised-I think by the

would be necessary to disperse to some

the problems arising from that
referred to incidentally by the hon.

left (Mr. Joseph
my
Cunningham) when he spoke about rebuilding houses damaged by air raids. It
is obvious that if you disperse your
Belfast industries you will not want so
many houses in Belfast to house the
workers, and that is a matter that will

was
Again, on the question of dispersal,
the water supply for domestic use is
mentioned. But there are two aspects of
the question of water supply for an industrial area. One is water for domestic
use, and the other is water you require
as a water-way. It is very little use,
even in these days, trying to establish a
large industrial centre when you have not
got access to the sea. We all remember
the story of the citizens of London when
they rather disagreed with one of the
Stuart Kings. He was going into all
sorts of things, but the Lord Mayor said,
"Your Majesty can do this and that, but
you cannot deprive us of the river." Even
in these days the proximity of a river is of
very great importance, and I imagine
that is a point that will not be lost sight
of when any question of dispersal of facilities for industrial areas comes to be considered.
Something was said about the question
of juvenile delinquency. The right hon.
Gentleman told us that the increase had
not been very heavy, but he drew attention to the fact that the class of offences
had gone from bad to worse; that there
was a rise in the number of indictable
offences; that class of offence which is
more serious and requires the attention
of a jury and at least a county court
judge. Various suggestions have been
made, as the right hon. Gentleman said,
He pointed out that some people thought
it was purely the affair of the churches,
and he rather suggested-and I was sorry
to hear him say it as Minister of Education that an improvement in parental
authority might be of some service.
Perhaps it might, but I do not know
that I want to see any great increase in
parental authority.
What I do want to see-and I believe
the right hon. Gentleman does too—is an
increase and an improvement in parental
influence. I think we must look to the
education which the right hon. Gentleman administers to show the child
that it is influence rather than direct
authority that
that is required for his
proper upbringing. I am sure it is the
more suitable line for the parent to take.
The days of the old tawse behind the
door are, I hope, gone, and I do not
want to see them come back. At the
same time those views have more or less
held the field for a great many years,
and now you are faced with this question
of juvenile delinquency, so that if it is
necessary to proceed on those lines you
cannot say it has met with any marked
success, and it is time to try something
else.
The House is aware that the matter
was inquired into by a Committee of both
Houses of Parliament, and I very much
regret that our hon. and gallant Friend
who took so much interest in that and is
at present serving His Majesty outside
Northern Ireland is unable to be here
this afternoon to join in this discussion
on the line he thought should be taken.
I think there is nothing else in the
Speech on which I can presume to detain
the House. I need scarcely say that I
support the Motion for the Address which
has been most admirably moved and
seconded.

Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in

KING'S SPEECH.
Motion for an Address.
Order read for resuming adjourned
debate on Question (3rd February):
"That an humble Address be presented to His Grace in the following
words:
"Your Grace,
We, His Majesty's most dutiful and
loyal subjects, the Senate of the Parliament of Northern Ireland, beg leave to
offer our humble thanks to Your Grace
for the Speech which Your Grace has
addressed to both Houses of Parliament."(Mr. Stevenson.)
Question again proposed.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN : In the first
place I am sorry that the mover and
seconder of the resolution are not present, because I should like to congratulate
them on the way they proposed and
seconded the Address of Thanks for the
Speech. But I would suggest to the
Government that the proposer and
seconder of the vote of thanks do not
speak for the counties of Tyrone and
Fermanagh. In County Tyrone there
are almost 71,000 Nationalists against
57,000 Unionists, and in County Fermanagh there are 30,500 against 24,000.
But mark you, owing to the Government's gerrymandering the Unionists,
although in a minority in both counties,
are in a majority in the county councils
and public bodies.
Dealing with the Speech, in general,
I agree with all my Friend, Senator
McAllister, has stated. There is one
point I would like again to bring before.
the Government and that is the question
of the potato subsidy for the coming year.
It is over six weeks since I put a question
in this House asking the Government if
they would announce as soon as possible
the subsidy to be paid for the coming
year for potato growing. It is most
important that the farmers should know
as soon as possible whether they are going
to get the subsidy or not, because in my
experience as an auctioneer going round
the country the farmers will not take land
for manuring until the Government
announce whether they are going to give
the subsidy or not.
The result is that owing to the delay
in the Government making the announcement the farmers are not making provision
for manuring the land for the coming
season. At least a certain percentage
of them are not doing so. Those who are
compelled to do so will do so, but if the
Government announce the subsidy in a
reasonable time those who had manure or
could get manure would make the land
ready if the subsidy is to be available
for the coming season. I pointed out at
the time that it is important the Government should subsidise the farmer for
manuring his land because we are faced
with a very serious difficulty at the
present moment. I might also say that I
am whole-heartedly with the Government
in this campaign for food production. We
must have as much food produced as possible, particularly during the coming
season. And the Government should keep
in mind that during the past couple of
years what they call virgin soil has been
cut and crops have been produced, but
that land during the coming years-at
least for four or five years-will not be
able to produce as good crops as it did the
first year.
And if the manure is not put
on this land we may look forward in
three or four years and find there will not
be 50 per cent. of the production of food
we have at the present time. That is a
serious position and as one with experience
in going about the country I put it to the
Government to give it serious consideration.
Coming to the question of improvement
and drainage of land there is no doubt
that the 75 per cent. grant offered to the
farmer is a great inducement to him to
improve his farm and also to see after the
drainage. But I am afraid the Government do not realise the seriousness of that
King's Speech--
Address in Reply
5 FEBRUARY 1942
position, because I understand that as far
as drainage applications are concerned at
great many of them are being very slowly
dealt with, and this is an important question. I understand in some counties at
least 75 per cent. of the applications to
have land drained, and to come in under
the grants scheme, have been delayed so
long that up to now some applications
have not been replied to. The result may
be that the farmer will have to let this
season go by and leave his land without
the drainage having been cleared because
coming up to the cropping season he will
be too busy engaged with the crops.
I have had personal experience of the
reclamation of land on some farms. There
was one farm on which there was a field
of some seven acres practically overgrown
with thorn bushes and whins. I got the
inspector to come out and see that field,
and I must say this in speaking of the
area inspectors and the officials of the
County Armagh Agricultural Committee,
that I cannot praise them too highly.
They go to the very utmost of their
power to do everything they can to help
the farmer, not alone in reclaiming his
land but in advising him what crops to
put in. They are most reasonable and
very courteous to the farmers, and, as I
say, I cannot speak too highly of these
officials of County Armagh.
In this instance the inspector estimated
that the reclaiming of that field could be
done in three weeks by five men. It is
very hard to get labour, but eventually
I managed to get eight men started on
that field. They have now been seven
weeks at that work, and they have not
finished yet. Before I could proceed with
the work I had to point out to the area
inspector that that was the state of affairs,
and I asked him if there would be any
difficulty in getting the grant. He came
out and inspected the work, and he
agreed that the men could not proceed
more quickly than they were doing. In the
case of another scheme we have been
allowed one man for three weeks. Five
men have been started on that work this
week, and I question whether they will
be finished in a fortnight. Therefore, I
suggest to the Government that where a
farmer can be relied upon, and the Ministry of Agriculture see he is doing useful
work in reclaiming his land and making
it ready for the crops, he should be given
every possible help. The Government
should be as generous as possible and not
curtail the 75 per cent. grant to the time
and labour they allot, but should allow
the farmer 75 per cent. of his outlay on
wages to labour.
There is another matter I would
strongly urge upon the Government, and
that is the question of flax production.
Going around the country and coming
into contact with the farmers every day
in the week I am in a position to say
that the farmers are not inclined to grow
flax this season. I say emphatically that
unless the Government are up and doing,
and unless they encourage the farmer, not
by speeches but by substantial help, the
flax produced during the coming year will
not be more than 50 per cent. of that
produced last year. In a great many instances that is due to the fact that the
farmer cannot get labour to pull his flax.
During the past season, when he did get
the necessary labour, it cost him too
much. I know that in one poor district
of County Armagh, one of the poorest
townlands in the county, labourers
charged a shilling a stook for pulling flax.
It cost some of the poor farmers 30s a day
per man, with the result that they lost
money on their crop of flax. That cannot
continue, and I put it to the Government
that they should do everything possible
to see that ample labour is forthcoming
to the farmer for his flax crop, and also
for his other crop production during the
coming season.
Coming to the question of unemployment, it is a well-known fact that Roman
Catholics and Nationalists are boycotted
and victimised on account of their opinions
and beliefs. Ministers of the Government
have advised their supporters not to employ Roman Catholics. The Prime
Minister was asked in another place if it
was true he had stated from an Orange
platform that out of 33 attendants in the
Houses of Parliament only one was a
Roman Catholic, and he was only temporary. When accused of attacking
people for their religious beliefs, and because of their religion, he answered, "I
have never done that." When further
asked if he made the statement, he replied: "I made the statement, but it was
only a statement of fact in connection
with one branch of one department of the
Government, and it was only a statement
of fact." A fine boast for the Prime
Minister to make from an Orange plat-
King's Speech---
Address in Reply
THE SENATE
[Mr. McLaughlin]
form to his followers at a twelfth of July
celebration.
Now we have the fact that in at least
one department of the Government, out
of 33 attendants there is only one Roman
Catholic, and he is only temporary.
What must the other departments of
this Government be like? Does not this
statement of fact show clearly that
even in the distribution of employment
by the Government Roman Catholics
are boycotted. Not alone are they boycotted, but on account of their religion
they are refused employment in large industrial firms spread over the Six
Counties, for we cannot forget the fact
that even other Ministers of the Government have advised their followers not to
employ Roman Catholics. The result is
that these people are forced on the dole,
and after a short time they are transported to Great Britain to work in
dangerous areas and at dangerous work.
Should they decline to go they are refused
relief and allowed to starve. As I have
already stated in this House, at least 90
per cent. of these people are Roman
Catholics, and I defy that statement to be
contradicted.
We are all anxious for the welfare of
this part of the country in which we live.
We are all anxious that we should live
in peace and harmony with each other,
but I do suggest to the Government that
they should give a little bit more consideration to the minority under their
control. If there was more fair play
and more toleration shown there would be
a better spirit prevailing throughout the
Six Counties.

reply.

McLaughlin to make the statement he has

he is very wrong in his other statement.

At the present time it is practically impossible to get a single replacement for
any farm machinery; in fact, even a

Another very important matter-I wish
it had been stressed in the King's Speech
-is the provision of good seed, especially
of oats and barley. Remember,
we
farmers have had, many of us, a terrible
harvest. A lot of our grain has been
heated in the stack and shed. That
heated grain does not prevent it being
of use to the miller or being of use for
consumption, human and otherwise, but
it does prevent it being of any real use
as seed, because its germination qualities
have almost been entirely lost.
I am
sure that all farmers, at any rate in my
part of the country, would be very well
advised to sell all their grain, oats and
barley and buy new seed from places
where the harvesting has been carried out
in fine weather. Seed-time is coming
pretty soon, and I trust that the Ministry of Agriculture will see that there
is seed to be had and that that seed will
be had at a reasonable price. I do not
want to emphasise the question of price.
The really important thing is that
farmers will be able to get the seed.
Another point touching on almost the
same thing is that we are often told
such a named pedigree seed would just
suit our land. We ask for it, and we
are met with smiles-it is, of course,
unobtainable. Cannot the Ministry of
Agriculture see that seed recommended by
their own experts should be available for
the farmer, and while they are doing so.
see that seed, as far as possible, of early
ripening sorts can be obtained.
With regard to the question of post-war
reconstruction, I trust that a real effort
will be made to incorporate new open
spaces, preferably green ones, in the new
lay-out in the heart of Belfast to ensure
that future generations will have something approaching what is called a garden
city. These open spaces will be invalu-
A fervent hope is expressed in the

but would it not be possible even at this

A conscript has this benefit; cannot we

charity but of right.
The Gracious Speech extends a cordial
welcome to the American troops to Northern Ireland. I am sure that welcome is
echoed in the hearts of us all. (Hon.
Members: Hear, hear.) My great-greatgreat-great grandfather was a Governor of
North Carolina, who died two or three
years before the start of the American war
of independence. I can picture how
terribly distressed he would have been if
he had lived to have found North
Carolina and the other 12 States fighting
with us on the question of independence
for themselves. I can imagine that if he
could look down on us now how very
happy he would be to know that those
American cousins of ours are now not
fighting against us, but fighting shoulder
to shoulder with us for the independence
of the whole world.

The
farmers say they could not get labour,
and when they did get it it would not
pull its weight. That is the trouble. We
passed a Bill some time ago fixing standard wages and hours for the farm
labourers. I supported that Bill. I was
glad it was brought in because I knew it
was going to improve the conditions of the
farm labourer to a certain extent, but I
expected the farm labourer to pull his
weight when the time came.
A farmer told me coming up in the
train that he knew another farmer who
had a lot of hay to cut. He applied to
the employment exchange for labour, and
he was sent five men. At half-past five
o'clock when they had one rick of hay
half up the men threw down their rakes
and forks while the man on the stack
jumped down. They said their time was
up.
The farmer offered to pay them
overtime if they would stop for one halfhour until the stack was topped, but they
would not do so. He said it was going
to rain. It did rain, and that stack and
all the hay that farmer had that had not
been put up was lost because it rained
practically for a fortnight.
That is one instance, and here is
another. Last week a man told me of
the case of a widow whose husband died
early last spring. She applied to the
employment exchange for a man to carry
out manure to put in her potatoes. A
man was sent. He came at eight o'clock
in the morning. He smoked a cigarette
and waited until half-past eight before
he put the horse into the cart. Then he
had an hour or half an hour for his
dinner. He carted out some manure that
day, and at half-past five he had the cart
half full of manure.
course, in the cart.

and the widow had to take the horse out

The great reduction in unemployment
was mentioned in the Gracious Speech. I
agree with what Senator McAllister said.
Both he and I would like to know what
is going to be done after the war. All
positions are filled at the present time,
but after the war a big company of men
and women will come back. What are
we going to do with them? That is what
I am puzzled about. I would like somebody to explain that to me. It has been
said that we will have to open up new
industries, but where will the raw
material come from? Where will a
market be found for the products of those
new industries?
There are a great many other things
coming before my mind which I would
like explained, but I do not know to
whom to apply for the information. When
people make complaints to me I simply
ask them have they any concrete suggestions to put before the Government
whereby industries can be established,
and raw materials and markets for their
products found. We must remember
that we have no raw materials
here in Northern Ireland. They have all
to be shipped in. The bringing in of the
raw material and the shipping out of the
products add to the cost of production.
That means that it costs us more to produce those articles than it does other
countries which work longer hours and
pay less wages. Capital is not going to
invest its money just for the pleasure of
giving employment without getting any
return.
Mr. ROBB: I wish I could reply to the
problems Senator Barclay has just put
before the House. I do not know that
anyone can do so.
The wisest minds,
amongst whom I do not reckon myself,
are applying themselves to these problems,
and even they confess they do not know
where they are. Nobody knows, but if,
as he suggests, anybody has any concrete
suggestion and will put it forward, I am
quite certain that not only here but elsewhere very serious consideration will be
given to anything practicable.

to be

Questions to Minister.
SUBSIDY FOR POTATOES.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he can explain the cause of the delay in announcing whether the subsidy granted for 1941
on manure ground grown in potatoes will
be continued in 1942?
I put down a similar question last
December, and my object in putting
down the question again is to get some
encouragement for the farmer to manure
his ground and put as much as possible
in potatoes. I am quite satisfied that if
this subsidy of £10 an acre is renewed
there will be a big increase in the potato
acreage this
I am surprised
there has been such a delay in announcing the subsidy. I know the Ministry of
Agriculture has to await instructions
from the Imperial authorities, but I
think it should be pointed out to them
that they are not helping the food production campaign by holding up this
announcement.
season.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The acreage payment for potatoes is not
a subsidy. It is an integral part of the
price guarantee. In order to ensure that
there would be no increase in the price of
potatoes to the consumer in respect of
the 1941 crop, part of the price to the
producer was made in the form of an
acreage payment. It was announced on
February 21st that the system of acreage
payment would be continued for the 1942
crop and publicity was given to this fact
in the Press and even on the wireless..
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
24 MARCH 1942
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: That is the first
intimation I have received that the
subsidy was to be continued this year.
I did not see any announcement on the
subject.
Mr. LESLIE: May I take it that the
subsidy for potatoes is to be £10 per
acre, as it was last year?
Mr. ROBB: I would assume so from
the answer that has been supplied to me.
LABOUR FOR FLAX PULLING.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if the Government will assure all farmers growing
flax this season that sufficient labour
will be forthcoming for the pulling of
the crop; and give an assurance that
no labourers will be allowed to increase
the cost of their labour for pulling flax
above the standard wages for agricultural labour; and that no contracts
for pulling flax will be allowed which
would work out at more than standard
wages.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Perhaps my
question has not been framed in
in the
form.
proper
I can quite understand
that you could not expect skilled flax
pullers to pull flax at the same rate as
an ordinary agricultural labourer, and
such a man should be given some inducement to pull as much flax as possible, and
his rate of pay might be increased a little.
The only thing I am anxious about is
that the farmer should not be exploited
again in regard to the pulling of his flax
as he was last season. Last season in
County Armagh a number of the flax
pullers pooled themselves together and
would not pull flax at less than a shilling
a stook. They actually went so far as
to make it a penny a beat, with the result
that a good many of them earned 30s a
day for pulling flax. That is not fair
to the farmer, who should really know
where he is. There should be some control over these rings of flax pullers, and
their daily wage should be controlled in
such a way that the farmer will not be
victimised. This victimisation actually
took place in the poorest district of
County Armagh where the soil is very
poor and flax was a poor crop. There
the farmers had to pay 30s a day to flax
pullers, with the result that they lost
money instead of making it. Such
farmers will not be encouraged to grow
flax in future.
Sir EMERSON HERDMAN: I am in
complete agreement with the hon. Member who has asked this question. There
was a flax-pullers' ring in County Tyrone,
where they charged £4 an acre, and you
could hardly get them to pull it at £6
an acre. Every word Senator McLaughlin
has spoken is quite true, and there is no
doubt that in many cases farmers, instead
of making money, lost money last season
on their flax crop.
Mr. BARCLAY: I would like to mention one case of a farmer who grew flax
last season. When he reckoned up what
it would cost him to pull his flax, he came
to the conclusion it would be better to let
the flax lie on the ground. He did so,
and it is on the ground to-day. The flax
pullers did not know what to charge.
They did not charge a shilling a stook, but
fourteen pence a stook. As a result, this
particular farmer lost his crop of flax, and
it is lost to the country. There should be
some control over these charges.
Mr. ROBB: While everything possible
will be done to secure labour for harvesting the flax crop this season, it is quite
impossible for the Government to give
anything in the nature of a positive
guarantee that labour will be available in
every case. The availability of labour
will depend, amongst other things, upon
weather conditions.
A minimum piece rate and a minimum
time rate were in existence during last
harvest, and either party can raise the
question of a revision of those minimum
rates.
The hon. Senator will appreciate, however, that the Government can give no
assurance that individual farmers will not,
of their own accord, pay more than the
minimum rates, and we cannot say that no
contract for pulling flax at more than
the minimum rates will be allowed.
Mechanical flax pullers in greater numbers
than last year will be available, and these
should help very considerably in the harvesting of this valuable crop..
The remarks of hon. Members about
the difficulty some farmers have through
Business of the House
Business of the House
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
having to pay high wages for pulling flax,
I will bring to the notice of the Ministry.
Mr. STEVENSON: I have every sympathy with the hon. Member's question,
but with the present price of flax, I think
farmers will really have to make up their
minds that they must pay considerably
more for the pulling of the crop. I have
in my possession figures showing where 2s
a stone on the finished product was paid
for the pulling of the flax alone, and yet
a very good profit was made on the complete crop.
I cannot really see that the
Government can interfere in the matter in
any way. The only suggestion I can
make is that the farmers should look very
far ahead and make their arrangements
for the pulling of their crop at a reasonable figure.

May I say that there is an urgency about

week, because should any amendments be

in order that the Royal Assent may be
given on Tuesday.
Mr. LESLIE: Really I think some
better explanation for this Motion than
that just given by the right hon. Gentleman should be given to this House. The
right hon. Gentleman talks about the
closing of the financial year.
So far as
I know the financial year has concluded
on the same day of the month for many
years past. It is fairly obvious to everybody that the 31st day of March marks
the conclusion of the financial year.
It
is almost as obvious as the fact that
Christmas falls on 25th December. It is
also the case that this House has not been
called together since 5th February, a
period of seven weeks, and we are now
asked to take the first and second readings
of no fewer than five Bills. I think the
number is without precedent.
We have on previous occasions been very
tolerant and done all we could to meet
the wishes-I was going to say of the
Government, but I am not quite sure it is
the Government that is concerned. But
if we are to consent to this Motion to-day,
we cannot do it without recording our
dissatisfaction at the arrangement, or
want of arrangement, that has required.
this sacrifice of us. It is not a matter of
the sacrifice of our personal feelings or
convenience; it is a question of the sacrifice of doing what we imagine to be our
duty. We exist, I have always been given
to understand, for the purpose of examining Bills sent to us before they reach the
Statute Book. I think the public of
Northern Ireland expects that we will
examine those Bills, and if the public
expects that of us the public is reasonable
enough to admit that we must be given
proper time.
The five Bills are of various degrees of

well stand over. The end of the financial
year has nothing whatever to do with the
Housing (Temporary Accommodation)
Bill or the Fire Services Bill. Neither
seems to depend on the end of the financial
year. The other three Bills are measures
which this House, if asked, would I am
sure be very willing to expedite; but I do
not think we ought to be required to
assent to the Motion as it stands.
As regards sitting three days this week,
it means that if anybody is going to have

be taken to-morrow-he must either have

Gentleman is going to say in moving the

rash man. No man can possibly sit down
and
prepare considered Amendments on a
Second Reading speech in time for consideration to-morrow, and even if he did,
hon. Members of this House could not
get the Amendments in time. They can
appear on the Paper, which will be posted
to country Members and perhaps received

Mr. MCALLISTER: I rise to support
the protest made by Senator Leslie, and
I adopt his arguments against this Motion.
I have often in this House voiced the
opinion that we are too ready to delegate
and sacrifice the duties entrusted to us.
Time and again I have protested against
passing by Regulations certain legislation
which is detrimental to the entire community, and against authorising an
official in an office to issue Regulations far
more extensive, far more drastic, and
more far-reaching than any Bill brought
before the House. We have to accept
responsibility before the public for these
measures. Now we are asked not only to
pass Bills after delegating our powers,
surrendering our rights, and sacrificing
the interests of the people, but we are
asked at a moment's notice to pass Bills
delegating our authority to other people
without one word of explanation from the
Leader of the House.
I suggest that the Leader of the House
has made no case for rushing these Bills
through this week. The Government must
have known for some time that they intended to try to rush these Bills through
this House this week, and they must have
known they were asking us to do that
without any opportunity of considering
them. Why were we not informed by
some means that it was the intention of
the Government to foist these three Bills
on the House. It is impossible for us as
public representatives to give proper consideration to them so as to do our duty to
the public. I suggest that for the Leader
of the House, representing the Government, to come in here to-day and ask us
to accept a Motion of this character is
asking too much. If we are prepared
from time to time to accept this kind of
treatment without protest we are certainly
not maintaining the dignity of the Senate,
our own assembly.
We are sacrificing our duty if we are
to be a mere rubber stamp for any
department of the Government, which
wishes to dictate to us, and which, without even a day's notice, insists on ramming Bills down our throats without
giving us an opportunity of considering
what we are going to do. If an ordinary
business man entered into wide contracts
and launched out on big new avenues of
commerce without considering how long
would they last,
they last, what would the
public think? Yet here is the Government asking us to accept a principle
which no small huckster's shop would
attempt to enforce. What is the urgency
for this Education Bill. What is the
urgency for the Housing Bill? I submit that as a matter of courtesy to the
House, if for no other reason, the Leader
of the House should give a fuller explanation for the Motion he has moved.

Certain of the Clauses in this Billnotably Clauses 3 and 4-relate respectively to the question of grants towards
