Housing (Temporary Accommodation)
Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time."
This is a war measure limited in its
operation to 31st December, 1943, unless
Parliament otherwise determines.
Following upon the air attacks in
Northern Ireland, a number of local
authorities were approached by persons
whose houses were destroyed for permission to erect temporary buildings for
human habitation, in many cases on the
sites of the former houses.
In the circumstances it was considered
that existing local authorities would be
justified in permitting temporary buildings to be erected, on condition that these
should be removable by the local authority
as and when circumstances justified such
a course.
As the law stands at present, however,
Section 43 (1) of the Planning and Housing Act (N.I.), 1931, has imposed on
local authorities the duty of making and
enforcing building by-laws, and as these
by-laws do not provide for the erection
of temporary buildings of the character
indicated it is necessary to legalise any
action already taken or to be taken in
the future to enable approval to the
erection of these buildings to be given.
The Bill, therefore, empowers a local
authority, subject to such directions as
may from time to time be given by the
Ministry of Home Affairs, to consent to
the erection and use for human habitation
Housing Bill—
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
of buildings, the method of construction
of which does not comply with the building by-laws in force in their area.
In giving their consent, however, a
local authority may, if they think fit,
attach conditions with regard to the situation, sanitary arrangements, and protection against fire of any building.
local authority may also fix, and from
time to time extend the period during
which a building shall be allowed to stand.
The
An Amendment made in another place
has added a provision that there must be
agreement between urban and rural
authorities and the Ministry of Home
Affairs in the cases arising in a rural
district within a radius of two miles from
the boundary of an adjoining urban area
and in cases arising in an urban area
within half-a-mile from the adjoining
rural area.
Some of the towns said, "We do not
like the idea of all sorts of queer buildings
being put up near our boundary." Other
people said, "We do not want you to put
up any sort of pig-sty near our boundary"
and accordingly this Amendment was
made.
The Bill also provides penalties for the
breaking of any condition attached to the
consent and for failure to remove any
building within the time allowed. There
is also a retrospective clause in the Bill
whereby buildings that have been erected
in contravention of building by-laws since
the 1st April, 1941, and before the passing of this Bill are covered, and also a
provision enabling a local authority to
attach to such buildings the same conditions as if the buildings had been erected
after the passing of this Bill.
an
Mr. LESLIE: This is the Bill which
the right hon. Gentleman alluded to as
the one Bill of the five in which
amendment had been made in another
place. I think the amendment has
spoiled the Bill. If it had not been for
that amendment I should not have
troubled the House this afternoon. The
amendment is to sub-clause (5) of
Clause 1, and as it has been put in in
another place I do not suggest it should
be taken out. But I shall have to
suggest that it requires two amendments
itself, and, secondly, an amendment to
sub-clause (7). Let us examine what the
effect of this sub-clause is going to be.
The sub-clause was moved for reasons
which, no doubt, awaken our sympathy.
The right hon. Gentleman summed it up
by saying that an urban authority might
object to buildings being erected near its
boundary. But two miles is not near the
boundary. If you are to condone the
erection of buildings for the war-time
necessities of people who have been
bombed out of their houses and have to
rush to the nearest shelter in the country
for temporary shelter, and it is two miles
out, it will be a long way back to their
work in the morning. I do not think
that is a very good provision. Two miles
is too much. Looking at some of our
smaller urban districts, if you do not
allow houses of a certain character within a radius of two miles of the boundary
there is not going to be very much left of
that urban district where you can put
them up.
There is also the question of time. If
it should unfortunately again become
necessary to run up these temporary
shelters to meet a certain emergency it
will take some time for the two local
authorities to come to an agreement between themselves and with the Ministry
of Home Affairs. I would much rather
not have had this sub-clause in the Bill,
but as it is in we must get rid of this
expression "within a radius of two miles
of the boundary." You must have a
focus which must be a particular point
in the boundary.
Otherwise it is not
sense.
This sub-clause also gets you into
trouble with sub-clause (7), the whitewashing sub-clause. It deals with a local
authority that has consented to the nonobservance of its by-laws. Admittedly they
had not got the power to consent, but
they have done it, and we are whitewashing them. It may be they have
consented to the erection of some building within two miles of the boundary.
We have got to get over that, but it only
means the insertion of a few words, and
I have put on the Paper something
which I think will do all that is necesssary. It drops the words about the two
miles radius, and adds a few words in
sub-clause (7). Otherwise I support the
Bill, and but for this unfortunate subclause I should not have found it necessary to trouble the House.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Fire Services Bill-

24 MARCH 1942

Fire Services (Emergency Provisions)
Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: This Bill is intended to
combine the fire-fighting services of
Northern Ireland into one Force with a
view, both to unifying the methods of the
many local units and to making the force
not only more easily controllable but much
more effective for the tasks which lie before
it. To this end it is intended to transfer
the control of all fire brigades and their
auxiliary fire service units from the local
authorities who are at present the responsible bodies, and to place this control in
the hands of the Ministry of Public
Security.
Prior to the outbreak of war the firefighting resources of Northern Ireland
were on a rather scanty scale. In the
cities of Belfast and Londonderry wholetime brigades equipped with modern
appliances were in operation, but elsewhere there were part-time brigades, that
is, brigades which, while they may have
had in some cases modern appliances, were
manned only by retained firemen, who had
not the opportunities of having the concentrated training in fire-fighting that
were available to their full-time counterparts in the cities.
It was
These peace-time services, nevertheless,
are courageous and efficient, and their
knowledge of the fire risks in their own
localities has been most valuable.
with these "retained" brigades as a
nucleus that the present large Auxiliary
Fire Service was developed. New pumps.
and equipment were obtained and issued
to the different scheme-making authorities,
who recruited large numbers of men to
operate the appliances. Training was
carried out enthusiastically, and the
effectiveness of the fire services was shown
in the heavy raids of April and May last.
While due tribute must be paid to the
keenness of the men of the fire services
and to their ability, and while the Government gratefully acknowledges the really
good work carried out by the local authorities in forming the present fire services, it
cannot be said that the present system of
control, of liaison, of co-ordination, is
sufficient to allow this vitally important
defence arm to give of its very best in an
emergency, and only that very best is good
enough when a city is fighting the flames
during and after a heavy air raid.
Until a few years before the outbreak
of the war, no fire-fighting organisation
had had practical experience of the tactics
which must be adopted to meet heavy
incendiary attacks from the air, and, in
fact, experience of real value was not
available until after the heavy fire bomb
raids on the cities of Great Britain which
followed the Battle of Britain in 1940.
The experience then gained showed that,
to meet such attacks, the fire services must
be organised as one force of a highly
mobile and specialised nature, and operating under a single command which must
be in a position to call up large reinforcements at a moment's notice. In short,
the fire services, like each of the fighting
services, must be remoulded into a single
body capable of co-operating with other
defence bodies, and not merely remain a group of independently
operating units. To achieve this
object the essential features required
are, unified command, unified control, and
a unified system of training and operation.
Local authorities, however willing, cannot
give this centralised direction, and the
remedy lies in a national system of control,
and it is a national fire service precisely on
these lines that is contemplated by this
Bill.
.
The provisions of the Bill are designed
so that the transition may be made by
stages. A good deal of the preliminary
work has been carried out already with the
willing co-operation of the local authorities,
and the Government is confident that this
generous co-operation will be extended to
the new service when the time comes for
it to stand on its own feet. Indeed, the
valuable local facilities at the disposal of
the present fire services could not well be
done without.
It is the intention that the whole of
Northern Ireland shall comprise one fire
service area. Our requirements in
appliances, transport and other equipment have been calculated on
a scale
which allows not only for the fire risk
existing in the Province, but for the fact
that reinforcement here is a much more
difficult problem than it is in Great
Britain. Our supplies of this equipment
are well up to the standards available in
similar areas in Great Britain, and as the
Fire Services Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
supply position is getting easier it is
hoped that shortly we shall receive the
balance of our orders. A recruiting drive
will be undertaken to provide the necessary man and woman power which will be
required to provide sufficient personnel.
to man all the appliances, provide relief
crews and to staff the control rooms, fire
service offices and other establishments
necessary to the existence of a large disciplined body.
It is intended that as from the 1st
April, 1942, a National Fire Service shall
be established and maintained in Northern Ireland at the expense of the
Exchequer, but subject to a small contribution from each local authority. The
estimated annual cost of the National
Fire Service is a substantial sum, and the
contribution which the local authorities
will be required to make will represent
only a small proportion of the full cost.
The contributions from local authorities
will be the produce of a 2d rate from
each of the county boroughs, of a 1d rate
from each borough or urban district,
and of a d rate from each rural district.
In the case of the county boroughs there
will be a considerable saving in the rates,
while in the case of the boroughs and
urban districts there should be, generally
speaking, some small relief obtained. In
the case of rural districts, however, the
proposals will in almost all cases impose
a slight additional charge. These arrangements were discussed with the associations
of all the local authorities concerned and
were willingly agreed to, an agreement
which, I may say, was no small satisfaction to the Government, particularly in
the case of the rural authorities who cannot hope to receive as great a measure of
protection against fire as the towns. They
have appreciated the fact that whatever
can be done in their interests will be
done, and they have unselfishly agreed to
co-operate by contributing to the cost of
providing fire protection for the Province
as a whole. Here I may stress the point
that where previously it has been the
custom for fire authorities to make
charges against owners of property for
attendance at fires the Government proposes, subject to the contributions just
outlined, to provide a free fire service for
the Province.
The provisions of this Bill are, of course,
intended to cover the present emergency
only; we are not at present concerned
with what may or may not happen after
the war. The Government believes that
the nationalisation of the fire services
during the period of hostilities is a
measure which will materially improve
their efficiency in combating air-raid fires,
and I hope, therefore, that you will
support this measure.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."

a volunteer fire brigade, and have recently been trying to work it up a bit.
I live just outside that area, and the
brigade has been to see me on several
occasions, not officially but for practice.
purposes, as I have some water available.
But they do come and test their
machines and train themselves. They do
try, but the fact remains that you are
going to improve their equipment during
the war.
What is going to happen after the
war? For the moment I am omitting
Belfast and Londonderry, because their
brigades will still be there after the war
and will be available for the purposes of
their own areas and the immediately
adjacent areas, just as in times past. If
there is a fire at Carrickfergus the Belfast
Fire Brigade is sent. On the other
hand, suppose we have a big fire in
Belfast, it would not be quite so easy, if
there were an epidemic of fires on the
same night, for Carrickfergus to send its
fire brigade, if it has one, to Belfast.
That is one of your troubles. Whether
you can get over that by unification I
do not know.
On the question of co-ordination, you
cannot co-ordinate that which does not
exist. The small villages and towns have
nothing to co-ordinate and cannot come
in yet, but meantime you are going to
ask them to pay a halfpenny in the
pound. I imagine I am right in saying
that the halfpenny rate is only to be
charged on un-derated hereditaments. It
is very important to know that. That
also applies in the towns and county
boroughs, because there are industrial
hereditaments that are partially derated.
For the moment I will proceed on the
assumption that I am right in thinking
it is only un-derated hereditaments that
will pay the halfpenny. If that is so a
man in the rural districts will have to
have a valuation of £480 a year before
he has to pay a pound, and that will not
break him.
Mr. ROBB: The rate will be levied on
the net annual value, less the derating.
That is on un-derated hereditaments.
Mr. LESLIE: Then it is only the
un-derated man who will have to pay,
and if he has a valuation of £500 he will
have to pay £1 Os 10d. That in itself is
negligible so long as it stays at a halfpenny, but what I am afraid of is that
after the war is over some of these places
will be left with equipment which they
have not the men to maintain, and they
will have a much higher expense to keep
them up. Briefly, I think that in this
matter the Ministry of Public Security
is trying to run before it can walk. It
has not yet exhibited to us any great
extra super-efficiency in the management.
of what fire appliances it has got.
It is true that it has not had very much
chance since the two serious occasions.
Since then improvements have been made
in the means of fire fighting, quite apart
from the fire brigade proper, but there is
no evidence I know of which will suggest
that the Ministry of Public Security will
manage the business better in the future
than the local authorities have managed
it in the past.
During the Belfast raids, fire brigades
came from every place that had a volunteer engine to send, and I think they all
negotiated the roads in safety and, I
believe, did some good. They will do that
again, but they did that under their own
management. We have not got anything.
to build on like the Fire Brigades Act of
1938, on which the statute of Great
Britain of 1941 was based. They had it
Fire Services Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
there three years before they passed their
present Act, and in that Act, if I remember rightly, it was provided that these
charges for extra-boundary services should
cease to be payable. That was done by
statute in England. It is not in this Bill
before us. I think it ought to be. There
is a general Clause in the English Fire
Brigades Act of 1938, and I would like to
see it incorporated in this Bill.
Then, again, there is the question of
finance. The cost is to be borne by the
Exchequer, which means, I presume, that
the bulk of the money we spend will be
recouped to us, directly or indirectly, out
of our Imperial contribution. But that
will only be so long as we conform to the
old Colwyn terms. If we embark on new
services, we will have to pay the cost ourselves. Part of the proposals of this Bill
is the provision of additional fire services,
and that will be where the villages will
get their fire services from. The intention
is that additional fire services should be
provided, and somebody has got to pay for
them. Is that coming out of our
Exchequer also, and indirectly from the
Imperial Exchequer? The Imperial Act
of 1941 does not contain any reference to
the provision of additional fire services,
and I am rather frightened about that,
too. It is another thing about which I
should have liked additional information
if we could put off the Committee stage.
I do not know that there is anything else
on which I need detain the House at the
moment. I do not like to oppose the
Second Reading, and I would much rather
move that the Bill be read a Second time
this day nine months, because I think the
Bill would gain if it was put off and more
time was given to examining its details.
Nothing has been done so far as extradepartmental, and you cannot rush to the
assumption that what has been done in
Great Britain is necessarily suitable in
Northern Ireland, which has more or less
remote districts. You can quite eeasily
make arrangements to cover Belfast and
Derry, but when you get to bringing in
Strabane, I do not know what provision.
they have got for fire prevention.
The MAYOR OF LONDONDERRY:
It is served by the Londonderry Fire
Brigade.
Mr. LESLIE: And the distance from
Derry to Strabane is?
The MAYOR OF LONDONDERRY:
About 12 miles.
Mr. LESLIE: It is a considerable
distance to travel in the black-out,
especially if you have two or three fires
raging in Strabane and Derry or Enniskillen. I am afraid I do not believe you
can do very much. I would prefer to see
the area confined to such rural districts
as might be designated by the Ministry,
or until it had been co-ordinated. That
would extend your method of proceeding
by steps, while if you try to do it all at
once you are only paving the way for
muddle and confusion.
The Schedule is rather in places confusing. Clause 9 gives the Ministry
power to make regulations therefor.
similar paragraph appears in the Schedule to the English Act, but it would
seem to give power to the Ministry to
make regulations prescribing death penalties and fines of £20,000. That cannot
be the intention. You have mopped up
the Bill of Rights, so perhaps the object is
to rely on the conventional understanding
of the word "offence" as some trivial
matter. I think paragraphs 9 and 10 of
the Schedule will want looking at, but if
we take the Committee stage to-morrow
I do not look forward with satisfaction to
It is a
a very highly desirable result.
great pity there should be anything
wrong with the Bill, because it is so well
meant, but I do think it ought to undergo
a little more examination.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
think the air raids we experienced last
year found us wanting in many respects,
but in no respect more so than the fire
services, which were practically useless.
We had no arrangements made in Belfast
to deal with the tremendous fires that
took place at the one time. Whether this
Bill will effect any improvement it would
be hard to say.
The Belfast fire brigade
has been considerably extended since then
and with the auxiliary fire service they
have the necessary pumps and other
equipment situated in different parts of
the city. To co-ordinate the whole fire
services of Northern Ireland is rather a
peculiar and a risky thing to do.
If an
attack were made on Derry and all the
fire services were rushed to that city,
and at the same time a similar state of
things occurred in Belfast the citizens of
Belfast would certainly complain very
Fire Services Bill-
Second Reading
24 MARCH 1942
bitterly if they were left without their fire
services.
Even now they do not like the thought
of the fire services being taken out of their
control. They think they have a very
efficient fire brigade, and with the
auxiliary fire service they think they
would be able to deal with any emergency. No one knows where the next
blow may be struck, and to rush fire
brigades into any particular district and
leave the other parts without protection
will be a very serious matter for industrial centres. The citizens of Belfast
would be seriously alarmed if the fire
brigade was removed from Belfast. The
Bill requires a great deal of consideration
and it is hard to make up one's mind as
to the best thing to do. The Ministry
of Public Security think co-ordination
and unification might be of benefit to the
Province, but it is a question.
Mr. ROBB: There seems to me to have
been far too much talk about co-ordination. The title of the Bill is:
"To provide for the reorganisation and
improvement of fire services in
Northern Ireland, and for purposes connected with the said
matters, and the first Clause
reads:
The Ministry of Public Security (in
this Act referred to as "the Ministry")
may make regulations providing for the
co-ordination, for the period of the present emergency, of all or any of the fire
services provided by local authorities
or for the unification, in whole or in
part, under the control of the Ministry,
of all or any of those services for that
period, for the provision of additional
fire services, and for any other matters
which appear to the Ministry to be
necessary or expedient to be provided
for with a view to improving the
arrangements for fighting fires or for
carrying this Act into effect, and such
regulations may in particular (but without prejudice to the generality of the
foregoing words) make provision for all
or any of the matters set out in the
Schedule to this Act.
There are 24 fire brigades in Northern
Ireland, apart from Belfast and Londonderry. All this is to be done by regulations, and the Act provides that the
regulations shall be laid before Parliament, and Parliament can reject them.
Mr. MCALLISTER: In dummy form,
whatever that means. I have never,
after fifteen years, been able to find out
what it means.
Mr. ROBB: The regulations are there,
and any person can read them.
Mr. MCALLISTER: They are
dummy form. What is dummy form?
in
Mr. ROBB: I have never seen regulations in dummy form. These regulations must be laid before Parliament.
Mr. LESLIE : I believe they have
been laid in lithograph form.
Mr. ROBB: There is no objection to
that if they are there. Under the Bill
itself they are liable to be disapproved
of by Parliament. The object of the
whole Bill is to try to improve the fire
fighting services. To suggest that the
object is to take the fire brigade from
Belfast to Strabane is beyond my comprehension. Presumably you are going to
have an intelligent person directing this
service, and the resources of the whole
service are to be available for any particular area if they are needed.
We hope
they will not be needed in all the places
at the same time, but there will be a
unified control that will send sufficient of
them, and, if necessary, the whole of the
equipment, to particular places where
they are needed. It is not a mere question of co-ordinating the existing services,
but trying to set up a new service with
all the appliances and staff that experience has shown are
necessary and
suitable for the purpose.
With regard to what is to happen after
the war, if nothing happens except that
this Bill lapses you have the Government
of Northern Ireland in possession of and
responsible for the payment of the whole
of this service. The experience gained
during the war will probably have lessons
for the Government and the people as to
what is best to be done at the end of the
war; whether these things shall be put into
cold storage for another war or maintained on a reduced footing all over the
Province. I would hope that intelligence
will be applied to that problem as
arises, but in the meantime the Government has to finance this scheme, subject
to a contribution from the whole of the
country, and that will come out of our

Question to Minister
Business of the House
THE SENATE
THE SENATE.

Thursday, 26th March, 1942.
The Senate met at a Quarter before
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Question to Minister.
SUBSIDY FOR PLOUGHING OLD
GRASSLAND.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he is in a
position to make any statement regarding
the continuance of the grant of £2 per
acre for the ploughing of old grassland
after the 31st March next.
I wish to express my thanks to the
Leader of the House and the Clerk of the
Parliaments for affording me facilities for
putting this question to-day. It deals
with a very urgent matter. The 31st
March, by which date old grassland must
be ploughed to qualify for the subsidy of
£2 per acre, is fast approaching, and if it
is intended to extend the qualifying
period, as was done last year, I think
immediate notice should be given to the
farmers so that they can make their
In
arrangements accordingly.
many
parts of the country farmers have been
unable to get their old grassland ploughed
owing to the late season and harvesting
and threshing operations, which
carried on until a few weeks ago, so that
tractor owners had not an opportunity of
doing ploughing work.
were
Take my own case. I have some grassland for ploughing, and I have not been
able to get it done yet, although in
November last I arranged with the man
who was doing the threshing to get this
land ploughed by 31st March. He has been
held up by threshing operations and has
not been able to do the ploughing, and I
am sure there are similar cases all over the
Six Counties. In some areas there is a
great shortage of tractors for ploughing
work, while in other areas there is probably a surplus. I respectfully suggest to
the Ministry of Agriculture that they
should get in touch with their inspectors
in the different areas and ask for a report
as to how each area is situated as regards
the supply of tractors. If there is a
shortage in a particular area tractors could
be brought from other areas where there
is surplus. The Ministry can get that
information from their agricultural inspectors. I hope the Ministry will extend
the time, as they did last year, when it
was extended until practically the end of
May, and the Ministry gave early notice of
their intention to do so.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
am glad to be able to tell the hon. Senator
that the grant of £2 per acre for ploughing old grassland will be continued for a
further year from March 31st next. I
shall bring the point with regard to tractors before the notice of the Ministry.
Mr. MAGUIRE: The right hon.
Gentleman says the ploughing subsidy will
be extended for another year.
Mr. ROBB: From 31st March next.
Mr. MAGUIRE: That does not mean
any extension of the period within which
the land must be ploughed up in order to
obtain the subsidy. It must be ploughed
before 31st March in order to gain the
subsidy for this season. Am I to take it
that this qualifying period will be extended to 31st May? There must be a
time limit within which the land must be
ploughed up.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Last year the
closing date for the subsidy was 31st
March, and it was extended to 31st May.
The farmers are a great deal behind in
their ploughing, because the land was too
wet for ploughing. In a great many districts farmers have been unable to plough
owing to the dampness of the land.
Mr. ROBB: I am informed that the
answer to the hon. Member's question is
"Yes."

for this day's sitting for the purpose of

Bill, and the Fire Services (Emergency

at this meeting of the Senate as regards

as the Londonderry to Belfast line. I do
not know whether the Leader of the
House has given any further consideration to the matter.
Mr. ROBB: As I said on the last day,

of the Members. The Government do

the other.

Questions to Minister.
EXPORT LICENCES TO EIRE.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin:
are
To ask the Leader of the House whether
the Government are aware that merchants
exporting certain goods to Eire
obliged to apply to the Imperial authorities in London for a licence or permit to
export these goods, which in most cases
means a delay of at least 10 to 14 days;
whether the Government will make representations to the Imperial authorities
requesting them to arrange for such
licences or permits (to export goods to
Eire) to be issued from one of the
Government offices in Belfast, or by the
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
12 MAY 1942
local inland revenue or customs official
and thus save merchants time and risk of
losing valuable Eire trade.
Mr. MCLAUGHLIN : I put this
question down in the interests of the
business men in Northern Ireland. At
the present time it is necessary to get a
permit to send to Eire a number of
articles that are not on the Control List.
I can quite understand any articles that
are needed for war purposes being controlled so that high authority should have
something to say in regard to their disposal, but it is a great disadvantage to
the Northern Ireland business man
to be obliged to send to London to
get a licence when he requires to send
certain articles to Eire. If the interests
of business men in Northern Ireland are
considered there should be an office in
Belfast where they can apply for such a
permit. It would be a very simple
matter for the business man in Northern
Ireland to go to the local customs office
or the inland revenue office and there
apply for a licence.
Let us consider the position of the
London or Glasgow business man if, when
he wanted to send goods from London to
Glasgow or from Glasgow to London, he
was compelled to send to Belfast for a
permit. That is not a business-like
arrangement, and there is very little consideration being shown to the business
man of Northern Ireland when he is
compelled to send to London for a
licence. Therefore I respectfully request
that the Northern Government should
make representations to the Imperial
authorities to allow these licences to be
issued by some of the Government offices
in Belfast, or through the inland revenue
or customs offices, and thereby facilitate
the Northern Ireland business man in his
trading with Eire. If the present
arrangement is to be continued it will
mean that the Englishman will have preference over the Northern Ireland
business man as far as doing business
with Eire is concerned, because he is
more easily and more quickly able to get
his licence in London. At the present
time it takes the Northern Ireland
business man at least 14 to 16 days to get
the necessary licence from London.
know of business men who have applied
to London for a licence and at the end of
16 days they have had no reply.
I
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
Government control of exports is a necessary part of war-time economy, and is
intended to ensure among other thingsthat exports are confined to goods that
are surplus to United Kingdom requirements for the war effort or for essential
civilian needs. The Export Licensing
Department of the Board of Trade is the
controlling authority, and its main function is to keep under review the supply
and demand position in the United Kingdom of a wide range of goods and
commodities. This function must manifestly be exercised at one focal point.
seems, therefore, that the general measure
of decentralisation suggested by the hon.
Senator would impair the efficiency of the
export control and would not be acceptable to the Board of Trade.
It
On questions of export licensing affecting Northern Ireland, the Ministry of
Commerce keeps in close touch with the
British Departments concerned, and if
the hon. Senator will furnish particulars
of delayed applications, or if he will put
his informants in touch with the Ministry,
every effort will be made to secure
improvement.

DAMAGED HOUSES-PAYMENT OF
ACCOUNTS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Joseph
Cunningham:
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he is aware that the repair of
damaged dwelling-houses in the city is
considerably delayed owing to the
accounts of the builders being so long
overdue before settlement is made by
the Government Department; whether
action will be taken to expedite payments so that evacuees can be restored
to their homes as quickly as possible.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
think it is no longer a secret that the
city of Belfast suffered in a very severe
air raid about a year ago, and that the
northern part of the city was most disastrously affected in regard to the number
of dwellings destroyed. A considerable
number of business premises were also
wiped out. Up to the present time no
statement has been published of the
approximate amount of damage done to
property or the number of lives lost
during that raid. What I am concerned
about is the plight of the homeless, those
people who were driven from their former
homes and had to find shelter for themselves and their families in country districts and other places all over Northern
Ireland. In some cases they were very
poorly accommodated. I am glad to see
that any houses that can be repaired are
being repaired.
If they
The Corporation do not condemn any
house that it is possible to repair. Everything depends on how quickly the work
can be done, and I regret very much to
say that the workmen upon whom these
poor people are dependent, the plasterers
and other building trade workers, do not
seem to exhibit very much sympathy with
those who are in such distress.
had any sympathy they would work a
little harder, and not show SO much
signs of shirking and malingering as they
do. I am sorry to have to say that, because one would think that the workers
in that particular trade would be very
sympathetic towards the families of
workers in other trades. I spoke to a responsible official in the City Hall and
suggested that he should introduce a contract or a piecework system in order to
try to induce these men to do a fair day's
work for a fair day's pay, but up to the
present this has not been done.
I am concerned with those who are
homeless. There are a number of small
contractors who have undertaken the renovation and repair of damaged houses.
The Corporation are doing a great
amount of work through contractors, and
they are responsible for the payment of
these contractors. I think the Corporation are doing their best to pay these
accounts pretty expeditiously so that the
builders in their turn can pay their
accounts as quickly as possible. I am
particularly concerned with the small
builders who are not under the control
of the Corporation. These men are doing
what they can to repair houses, but when
they have the repairs carried out they
find it very difficult to get their bills
paid. A representative of the Government Department concerned whom I
approached said that, unfortunately, he
was unable to get professional men
examine this work so that the bills could
be passed for payment. Surely a
thing should not be allowed to cause
such serious delay and dislocation. The
Government should find some way of paying the bills when they become due. If
that were done the builders could pay the
merchants for the materials supplied as
well as the wages of the men they employ. I understand that if these builders
have to go to the bank for an overdraft
they have to pay interest at 5 per cent.
This is a prohibitive amount for many
of these small builders.
to
There are also a number of master
painters in a small way of business who
are willing to paint and decorate these
working-class houses after the plasterers
have made such a great mess, but they
have not the necessary capital to undertake the work. A number of them
generously started out to assist in this
work, but they had to stop because they
could not get their accounts paid and
were thus unable to pay for the materials
they obtained and the wages of their
employees. This is a very serious matter
and surely the Government should exert
themselves to the utmost in order to see
that these poor people are restored to
their homes.
You have to go through the back
streets of the city to see the real amount
of destruction that has been caused; you
do not see the destruction if you go only
through the principal streets of the city.
If you go up the Oldpark Road and
through the Duncairn Gardens district
you will find that a tremendous number
of good class houses have been either
completely demolished or badly damaged.

Mr. ROBB: With regard to the complaints of slackness on the part of workers
that point, perhaps, hardly arises on this
question, but I think it is relevant to
one part of the answer.
As no doubt the hon. Senator is aware,
the administration of the War Damage
Act, 1941, is in the hands of the Imperial
authorities. At the same time we are all
naturally anxious to ensure that no unnecessary impediment is placed in the
way of those people who wish to return
to their homes.
The desirability of expediting payment
is fully appreciated, and I am informed
that action has already been taken in
this direction. Any delay that there may
have been up to now has been mainly due
to shortage of staff, particularly technical
staff. This difficulty has, to some extent,
now been overcome.
I am advised that the War Damage
Commission has already decided that payments on account should be made as a
matter of routine. This action has been
prompted mainly by the desire to meet
the requirements of builders and contractors engaged on war damage repairs.
I am informed that the Commission will
always be willing to try to help in any
case if the facts are brought to the notice
of the local regional office.

Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That

On Question, Motion agreed to.
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Unemployment Assistance (Finance) Bill, to
which they desire the concurrence of this
House.
Unemployment Assistance (Finance)
Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."(Sir Joseph Davison.)
The
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time."
object of this Bill is to provide substantial
relief to those local authorities who were
the subject of a levy under the Unemployment Assistance (Finance) Act of 1937.
As a result of the provisions of the Bill
the total contribution of the local
authorities will be reduced by four-fifths
from £51,990 to £11,355. In particular
the contribution of the Belfast Corporation is reduced from £45,000 to £10,000.
Hon. Members will recall that the
principle of a contribution by the local
authorities in respect of Unemployment
Assistance was first established by the
Unemployment Assistance Act of 1934,
but owing to the postponement of the
"appointed day" as regards the unemployment assistance scheme this Act never
came into force. In the 1937 Act the
contributions to be made by the local
authorities were fixed at definite amounts
for the five-year period ending the 31st
March, 1942, and the Act provided that
after that date Parliament should
determine the amount of subsequent
contributions.
On
When in March, 1942, the matter came
up for reconsideration the Government
was faced with a difficult situation.
the one hand the local authorities in Great
Britain were paying contributions at the
same rates as formerly, though the cost
to the Exchequer of unemployment
assistance had become almost negligible.
On the other hand, the Belfast Corporation represented that improved conditions
of employment here warranted the total
abolition of the contributions. In favour
of this contention they adduced the argument that, had Belfast been situated in
Great Britain in 1937, it is probable a
more favourable result would have been
obtained under the Block Grant System
which operated in Great Britain, because
the basis of distribution adopted there
pays special consideration to cities with
heavy unemployment.
In accordance with the principle that
there should be a parity of social services
between Great Britain and Northern
Ireland, the British Treasury was
approached and agreed that, while the
principle of a contribution by the local
authorities must be maintained, special
conditions obtained in Northern Ireland
to justify a reduction in the existing contribution. Thus it was possible to introduce the Bill which is at present before
the House-a Bill which I think hon.
Members will agree affords a very considerable measure of relief to the local
authorities.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That the Bill be now Committed.'
""
On Question, Motion negatived.
Ordered, That the Bill be read the
Third time on Tuesday next.
Drainage Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: Hon. Senators will recall
that the Drainage Commission was set up
in consequence of a recommendation by
the Select Committee on Unemployment.
But the chief consideration that has influenced the Government in introducing
this Bill is the making available of more
89 Unemployment Assistance Bill-
12 MAY 1942

Second Reading
land for food production during wartime.
I need not enumerate the recommendations of the Commission, but shall refer
briefly to some of the main points in the
Bill.
Owing to the very real lack of suitable
engineers the Commission found that it
would not be possible to carry out an
arterial drainage survey as proposed in
their terms of appointment, and were
reluctantly compelled to confine their
activities in the main to an examination
of ten complete arterial drainage schemes,
involving channel improvement, which
the Government
prepared by
were
engineers in pre-war years, and to the
consideration of some rivers where the
main cause of channel deficiency is a weir
or dam.
The ten schemes referred to above are
set out in the First Schedule to the Bill
and in the appendix to the reports.
In regard to the estimated cost of these
schemes, the Commission state that they
were for the most part prepared between
1930 and 1933, and that taking into
consideration the rise which has since
occurred in wages, cost of plant,
materials and consumable stores, approximately fifty per cent. should be added to
the estimates.
The Commission were satisfied that any
of these schemes, with the exception of
that in respect of the Newtownards
River, would bestow considerable and
early benefit on the affected lands and
recommended their early execution. In
addition to the ten schemes prepared by
the Government engineers, schemes in
respect of other rivers were prepared for
drainage authorities by private firms of
consulting engineers and by county surveyors,
Commission
and the
mended that such schemes should also be
considered as well as work on any further
rivers which the county councils might
put forward as emergency schemes.
recom-
They were also of opinion that additional powers should be given to drainage
authorities to deal with certain partial
schemes consisting of the removal
alteration of weirs and the execution of
remedial works.
or
But the most important recommendation of the Commission and the most important provision in the Bill are those
which relate to finance. The introduction of an Exchequer grant to county
councils for arterial drainage schemes
constitutes the greatest departure from
the earlier Drainage Acts, and it is
believed will give to drainage authorities
the necessary incentive to initiate this
important work. It is provided that the
amount of such grant may vary according to the cost of the schemes and the
degree of benefit they will confer, but
that it shall in no case exceed seventyfive per cent. of the total outlay.
In one respect the Bill departs from
the recommendation of the Commission.
This recommendation laid the whole
burden of maintenance
on the beneficiaries, whereas the Bill stipulates that
it shall be borne by the county councils
and the beneficiaries in the same proportions as between these two bodies as they
bear their share of the cost of execution.
It is felt that this change is a logical one,
and that it will give the county councils
a more direct interest in this vital maintenance work and a greater incentive to
see that it is properly and efficiently
carried out. It will also simplify assessment in the future by relating costs to
the actual expenditure incurred each
year.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
Question proposed.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I do not intend to
vote against this Bill because, while it is
perfectly clear that it can confer no
advantages at all on the agricultural community of Northern Ireland, unlike
many other Bills that pass here it cannot
do any harm. Therefore I do not intend
to vote against it. The Leader of the
House has laboured and relaboured the
question of emergency. I understood that
an emergency meant haste, expedition
and hurry, but what do we find is the
machinery that is to operate this Bill?
Under this measure, before a spade or a
shovel can be put on the land, any applicant for a grant has to go before four
separate bodies. His application has first
to be examined and passed by the county
council. Then it has to be examined and
approved by the Ministry of Home
Affairs. Then it has to be examined and
investigated by the Ministry of Finance,
and afterwards further examined and investigated by the Ministry of Agriculture.
The only Ministry that is left out of the
Drainage Bill-

THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
Bill, as far as I can see, is the one from

Mr. ROBB: It is drained dry.
Mr. MCALLISTER: These piecemeal
drainage schemes will be of no advantage.
whatever to the people of Northern Ireland. Agriculture is the staple industry
of this Province, and the drainage and
reclamation of land must be a major part
of agriculture. It can be carried out only
by some central authority with power to
Ideal with the whole of the Six Counties.
Let me quote a concrete case that may
arise under this Bill when it becomes an
Act. Supposing one county council says
that land adjoining land belonging to
another county council can with advantage be drained and improved and made
fit for producing food as an emergency
measure, and supposing that the first
county council is more active and enterprising than the adjoining county council,
but finds that the scheme can only be
made workable and practicable and
efficient by co-operating with the adjoining county council, if the adjoining
county council refuses to contribute its
quota of the cost and declines to cooperate with the other county council, the
scheme cannot be carried out.
That is an illustration of my point that
without a central authority administering
them these schemes can be of no advantage whatever to the community. Reference has been made to the Drainage
Commission. I am sure we are all sorry
we have not the final findings of that
Commission before us, and I am inclined
to think the reason we have not got those
findings before us is because they might
show that this Bill is not a real attempt
to carry them out. I am not going to
pass judgment on the Drainage Commission until I have had an opportunity of
reading its final Report; but we have
heard from the Leader of the House that
the Commission had not the information
and technical assistance necessary to go
into the question of drainage in Northern
Ireland as it should be gone into. If the
Commission had not that necessary
knowledge it was not its fault-it was the
fault of the Government-and if it had
not that knowledge its findings are bound
to be more or less worthless.
This question of drainage is a big one.
In my opinion there is no country that
has so much land capable of being turned
into good agricultural land as Northern
Ireland. That land is at present lying
waste through lack of drainage and
reclamation. We have been told that
this is an emergency Bill. It is only my
opinion, but I can visualise that an acre
of land drained immediately after the
war will be ten times more valuable in
the far distant future. I think the House
will agree that this is not a Bill that can
contribute seriously to the reclamation of
the waste lands of Northern Ireland. This
is a matter in which we are all vitally
concerned, and we are all anxious to
facilitate the Government in every way
we can; but I do suggest to the House
that by introducing a measure of this
description the Government have shown
an entire lack of capacity to understand
and measure and treat in a proper way
the reclamation and drainage of lands in
Northern Ireland, if we are to be a
prosperous agricultural community.

Clauses 7 and 8 are instances of what
I call novel procedure. That is the
authority to remove a dam, which is dealt
with in Clause 7, and Clause 8, which is
to give the county councils quite a new
power. That is the power to require
people in control of dams and sluices to
keep them open or closed in accordance
with the wishes of the county councils.
Although Clause 8 conforms to the recommendations of the Drainage Commission I rather wish it was not in the
Bill, because it is likely to lead to
trouble.
desire the scheme.
As it stands it is very difficult
to see how it is going to work. The
county councils have got to consider a
dam with some care, and apparently, in
the case of an emergency, the county
council has got to sit specially and give
a man notice. That cannot be the intention. It must be the intention that
the county council will delegate that
duty to someone else. I do not want to
lay too much stress on that, because the
Clause is part of the recommendation of
the Commission, but I do think it will be
found very difficult to work.
I cannot agree with the man in control of the dam being compelled to do
something within twenty-four hours. In
the first place it will take twenty-four
hours in some cases to find the man in
control of the dam. Perhaps fortyeight hours would be sufficient. Whatever the number of hours notice he may
be given, the proposal is that if the
county council is of opinion that immediate action is necessary he must do it
without the twenty-four hours' notice.
That Clause is one I spoke of as requiring more protection for the individual.
The exercise of the powers it is proposed
to confer may cause the man to incur
expenditure or suffer loss, and there does
not seem to be any provision for meeting
that. I think we shall have to put that
in.
be

three-fourths of the occupiers agree to a

approved. It has been found in the past
that nearly everybody wanted a scheme to
go through, but one person or possibly
two persons objected and in fact the
scheme was blocked. It is now proposed
to meet that difficulty, but I am not sure
that the method of meeting it, by providing for a decision by a majority in
number, is the most equitable. It is a
question we ought to consider whether the
majority should not be made up of value
as well as number. It may very well be
that two or three large ratepayers may
be on one side, and a larger number of
smaller ratepayers may be on the other
side. That is not at all an impossible
proposition, and it seems to me the test
should be, does a majority in number and
value of ratepayers in the affected area
That is a simple

you expecting to deal with a thousand

were levied in a lump sum. In Clause 7

county for the purpose of preventing
Drainage Bill—
Second Reading
12 MAY 1942

injury to agricultural land. Would it not
be as well to say also for the purpose of
benefiting agriculture or the land of that
county. Then in regard to the removal
of a dam, that dam may be connected
with a water supply, and I have always
been under the impression that a water
supply in this country is very often just
as important as drainage. I should,
therefore, like to be assured that steps
will be taken to preserve the water supply
if the dam is done away with. Incidentally, I would like to have a definition of
the difference between a dam and a weir.
The dictionary gives you a weir as a dam
across a river or stream, but I do not
think weir is meant in that way in this
Bill.
In Clause 10 power is taken to enter
upon lands.
I am not going to cavil at
that, because I am certain it is necessary
to have those powers, but I do hope those
powers will be used with reasonableness.
One knows of cases where certain powers,
such as those given to the Electricity
Board, have been used without reasonableness. Again, how is this work going
to be carried out? I do hope that the
supply of farm labourers will not be curtailed to carry out these schemes, because
if you do that you may find that in order
to obtain a problematical increase of food
at next year's harvest you will be losing
tons of food at this year's harvest because
the farmer will not have the men necessary to do the work. I understand it is
the prevailing opinion that the drainage
schemes are to be carried out between this
year's sowing and harvest, which is in a
matter of two and a half months' time.
Are these schemes ready to start now?
I understand machinery is to be used
for this drainage work. Is this
machinery actually here in this country?
If it is not you are going to miss the tide.
There is another thing. Is the cost of
that machinery going to be at all in
keeping with the work that is going to
be accomplished? I am very sceptical
that it will be possible to do any reasonable amount of drainage that will be of
any use for next year's harvest, although
it may be very important in the years
afterwards. Personally I like the Bill,
provided you do not reduce the farmers
supply of labour to carry out these
schemes, and that point ought to be
heavily underlined.
The farmers will know what they have
lost this harvest if you reduce their supply
of labour. They will also know how much
has been obtained next harvest from that
drained land, and consequently you will
be judged by actual results and not by
paper results. I am in favour of the
Bill, and I am sorry it was not introduced years ago.

Mr. BARCLAY: As regards the
point about the difference between a weir
and a dam, as far as I understand it a
dam is a place for holding water and a
Iweir is called a carrie. A weir, or what
is known in the country as a "carrie"-
a bank built of stones-was erected across
the river in order to convey the water to
a dam which, in turn, made the waterwheels revolve in order to grind corn or
scutch flax. I am pleased that this
matter is mentioned in the Bill, because
I know where there is one such "carrie."
It was built before I was born, and it
has kept the water back and has helped
to flood the land in the vicinity. There
is an old churchyard at the place which
is always under water when it rains, and
I am given to understand that before
this "carrie" was erected the churchyard
was always dry. These things can be
removed under this Measure.

Belfast Electricity Order, 1942.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, "That the Belfast Electricity
Order, 1942, made and confirmed by the
Ministry of Commerce for Northern
Ireland under the Electricity (Supply)
Acts (Northern Ireland), 1882 to 1936,
be hereby approved."
For some years past the Belfast Corporation have had in mind the acquisition of the premises of the Church of
Ireland known as St. John's, Laganbank,
so that the site might be used as an extension of the electricity works at East
Bridge Street. The parties were unable
to come to terms, however, and finally
the Corporation made application to the
Ministry of Commerce for a Special
Order which would enable the premises
to be acquired compulsorily. The requirements of the Electricity Supply
Acts and Rules have been complied with
and after full notice to all concerned,
and no objections having been lodged by
or on behalf of any interested party, the
Order was made and afterwards confirmed by the Ministry. It will not come
into operation, however, unless and until
it has been approved by a resolution
passed by each House of Parliament, and
accordingly I beg to move.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause

that twenty-four hours is too short a

required is that he should do it within
forty-eight hours. It will be observed
that in a case of real urgency there is
power to require him to do it even more
expeditiously. If the man is of goodwill the question will not arise, but I do
not think that twenty-four hours is a
reasonable requirement.
Mr. ROBB: The Government cannot

appear to be short, but the Government

possible.
Mr. LESLIE: I do not think I can

in default of anything to the contrary,

The
Mr. MCALLISTER: I oppose this
Amendment entirely on a question of
principle. Those found quilty of comparatively minor offences are liable to
various terms of imprisonment. Offences
under this Bill must be committed by
mill owners, and to them a fine of fifty
pounds is a comparatively trivial thing.
If such a fine is incurred it is done after
the man has deliberately refused to carry
out an order of the locally elected council,
and I think that is an offence that should
be dealt with pretty drastically.
ordinary man in the street is subject to a
term of imprisonment for comparatively
trivial offences against the law, but if this
Amendment is accepted it will mean class
legislation for the benefit of the mill
owner. It will also mean that for the
payment of a paltry fine of fifty pounds
he can override the locally elected
council and the Government. These
people should be subject to the same laws
and the same punishment for breaking
those laws as the ordinary man in the
street.
Major BOYLE: I would like to support the Amendment. In my part of
the country most of these little mills are
run by one man, and for every one of the

speaker there are dozens of small mills

entirely water power. I think the
Amendment is a very wise one, and I
support it.
Mr. ROBB: The Government is not
prepared to accept the Amendment.
After all, these offences will vary in
degree of criminality, and the court before which the offenders will appear will
be in a position to consider the gravity
or otherwise of the offence. The offence
might be a very grave one, and in that
case it is desirable that a severe penalty
should at least be available. The
penalties under this Bill are less severe
than those in two recent Acts passed by
this Parliament. Section 18 of the Bann
Drainage Act of 1929 imposes as a
penalty for obstruction a maximum of
six months' imprisonment and a fine of
£5 a day for a continuing offence.
In the Agricultural (Miscellaneous
Provisions) Act of 1940 a similar class of
offence being prosecuted on indictment
might result in imprisonment for two
years and a fine of £100 or both. Here

keep a big stick for the
more grave
offence.

Mr. LESLIE: I spoke with some
hesitation about this Clause on the
Second Reading. I did not much like
the Clause as it stood then, and I like it
still less now, having regard to what has
just occurred. The Committee ought to
consider very seriously before they pass
this Clause. It has, as it seems to me,
endless ramifications. It is quite a new
idea that a county council should
take over the water power of the
country, which is really what it means.
Amongst other things, any man operating
these sluices and things in accordance
with the directions of the county council
will become an agent of the county
council, and if he is negligent all sorts of
liabilities may arise. I would much have
preferred that this Clause had stood over
and been dealt with as part of the general
scheme we are promised in the future.
I would also have preferred that it
should not have been extended to every
dam in the county, but that it should
have been limited to what I may call the
scheduled dams; that is, dams that have
been inspected as being important obstructions to water ways listed and communications entered into between the council
representatives and the owner. By that
means you would have got over a good
many of your difficulties, including the
twenty-four hours notice difficulty, and
I think that would have suited us better.
It is a very dangerous thing to interfere as regards the water power of a
Mr. J. Maguire
Rt. Hon. J. H. Robb
Mr. W. S. Stevenson
Mr. H. Taylor
Mr. G. Whaley
country. It raises the very grave question of whether it is within the powers
of this Parliament. It looks to me very
much as though it is taking possession of
a man's property. I forgot that by an
Amendment just passed you have given
him compensation, but in any case it is a
very serious matter, and it may affect
practically every scutch mill owner in the
country. The whole thing depends on
how it is worked, and whether the county
council representatives succeed in getting
the good will of the water owners. I said
on the Second Reading that I did not like
to oppose the Clause because it had been
recommended by the Drainage Commission, and that still holds. Also those
sitting in another place have passed the
Clause, but this Committee should consider very seriously whether they should
allow the Clause to pass.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

part of the Bill.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
10, Sub-clause (1), after the word

sort it is really the majority as regards
the valuation of lands affected that
should be the ruling factor and not the
mere number of heads. You could have
one or two large men objecting who would
be seriously affected if the scheme passed,
and you might possibly have a large
number of smaller farmers who were not
affected very much and who might take
the other view. As the Committee knows
it is quite a common practice to require
that the majority shall be of both number
and value, and of the two, if we must
choose one only, I would prefer value
rather than number. It is a point for
the Committee to consider and, therefore,
I suggest we insert the words "and value"
as that is the expression that will be
found in the Companies Act dealing with.
a meeting of creditors about the winding
up of a company.
on
Mr. MCALLISTER: I feel called upon
to oppose this Amendment. I do not
think the right hon. Gentleman has put
forward any argument in favour of the
insertion of these words. I thought we
were passing away from the old days and
that we were living in a more democratic
age. Take the case of a large farmer
whose land has a high valuation
account of its size. He may be the most
unprogressive farmer in the district. He
may till none of his land and employ
practically no labour, grazing his whole
lands, but simply because he holds a
large amount of land why should he be
enabled to out-vote a large number of
progressive, industrious farmers who have
devoted their whole lives and the lives of
their families to cultivating their land.
We know that in normal times these
large farms are devoted almost entirely to
grazing and are of no substantial benefit
to the community. I am not finding
fault with them, because farming may not
pay them, but I cannot see why they
should be allowed to hold up a whole
drainage scheme affecting the great
majority of the farmers in the district.
I, therefore, oppose the Amendment on
its merits and on principle.
Mr. STEVENSON: I think Senator
McAllister's argument cuts much more
the other way.
I know of a case in my
own county where a large and most progressive farmer is absolutely held up by
smaller men who will not agree to any

in supporting the Amendment.

Mr. ROBB: The Ministry of Agriculture pay 75 per cent., and the balance of

they ought to have it. The objection is

more than one-quarter of those who will
benefit may in fact own more than one
quarter of the land which will benefit, but
the view of the Government is that in this
matter it is fair to count heads, and if the
majority, who amount to three-quarters,
ask for a compulsory scheme the other
quarter should be compelled to fall in,
even though they do happen to own more
than a quarter of the valuation of the land
that will be benefited. On that ground
the Government is not willing to accept
the amendment.

Mr. McALLISTER: On this occasion

principle. If you take the basis of
assessment we find that after the payment of 75 per cent. of the cost by the
Exchequer the county council itself has
to determine what amount it will pay
and what amount the occupier shall pay.
The county council, being a debtor, is
Drainage Bill-
Committee
19 MAY 1942
enabled to determine the amount of its
debt and what amount the other debtor
should have to pay. In the case of an
ordinary debtor and creditor it would be
illogical to say that instead of resorting
to the ordinary law the debtor should be
allowed to determine the amount for
which he was liable. I do not think the
county council is the proper authority to
determine a question of this sort. It has
no machinery and no experience for
settling disputes. The Ministry of Home
Affairs have had experience in these
matters, and I do not think any additional expense would be incurred by
leaving the matter in the hands of the
Ministry.
Mr. ROBB: I would ask Senator
Leslie to defer this Amendment until the
Report stage, and in the meantime the
Government will consider it.
Mr. LESLIE: Under these circumstances I ask leave to withdraw the
Amendment.

FIRST SCHEDULE.
The following Amendment stood on the

Page 12, leave out line 12.
Mr. LESLIE: I do not move this

Amendment not moved.
Question proposed, "That this be the
First Schedule to the Bill."
Mr. LESLIE: I may now explain to
the Committee the position at which I
have arrived. I have come to the conclusion that as the object of the Bill is
to confer power on the county councils it
would not be becoming for Parliament to
give that power with one hand and take
it away in respect of a particular instance
with the other. The only excuse Parliament would have for taking such a course
would be that Parliament is approving
the voting of a large amount of public
money. But the purport of the Bill is
to commit these matters to the county
council, subject to the approval of the
Ministry, and therefore I do not think
we should make a special exception. I
have made inquiry into this particular
case, and I do not think we should express
any opinion as to whether the scheme is
a desirable scheme or not. Accordingly,
I do not oppose the Schedule.
First Schedule agreed to.
SECOND SCHEDULE.
Mr. LESLIE: In the Second Schedule
I beg to move to leave out from the word
"with" in line 1 on page 14 to the end
of line 3 (and that the scheme has been
duly made and is within the powers of
the Drainage Acts as amended by this
Act.) The words in the text are, I am
sorry to say, to be found in the Schedule
to the Act of 1925. They were always
objectionable, and I do not recollect that
we have used these words on any occasion
since Sir Leslie Scott's Committee
reported in language particularly condemnatory of this form of words in 1932.
Accordingly I feel, as a matter of
principle, that one must move to leave
out these words. It is not for the
Ministry to say whether the powers are
within or without the powers of the
Statutes. It is a matter for the courts.
Drainage Bill
Adjournment
THE SENATE
Mr. ROBB: I recognise the source of
the right hon. and learned Senator's
objection to these words. It is quite true
that the Sir Leslie Scott Committee made
a recommendation that this sort of legislation should not be normally introduced,
but in their recommendation they excepted
emergency legislation, and this Bill is
emergency legislation. That is why, in
spite of the report of that Committee,
we venture to insert the words here. The
fact that they have not been inserted in
other Acts may be due to the fact that
it was not emergency legislation.
Mr. LESLIE: I think we may take it
that the right hon. Gentleman is rather
penitent, almost in an excusing frame of
mind, and he does not mean to do it
again except in very special cases.
think I may withdraw the Amendment
without sacrificing the principle.

Questions to Minister.
PAINTING OF ELECTRICITY
PYLONS.
Major DOBBS: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether the Electricity Board are taking, or will take, any
steps to camouflage their pylons so that
the disfigurement of the countryside by
these pylons may be reduced as far as
possible.
cause
I am quite certain that all the
Members of this House are fairly jealous
to uphold the dignity of the countryside,
just as they are to uphold the dignity
of this House. Therefore I think this
House is the place above all others where
this question should be ventilated. The
reason I am asking the question is bea few days ago I was travelling
down the shore road from Carrickfergus
to Larne, and to my surprise and horror
my eye was caught by these pylons at
Islandmagee. I did feel that something
might have been done to make them a
little bit less the silent monstrosities they
are at present. It is very hard to say
what should be done to cure it, and I do
not think you could possibly get a complete cure, but, in my opinion, something
might be done. After all, they are
skeletons, and not solid structures.
I would like to suggest what might be
done when erecting these pylons in the
future. It cannot be done with the present pylons because they have been built.
The pylons are composed of heavy angle
irons. If these heavy angle irons,
especially the horizontal ones, are placed
in the reverse way to what they are at
present it would make no difference to
the strength of the structure, but they
would not throw a shadow of themselves
when the sun was shining on them. The
top side of the angle throws a black
shadow. Then again I think it would be
an improvement if, instead of using
aluminium paint, they were painted
according to their surroundings and the
position from which they are going to be
seen. If you are going to look up at
them naturally you would expect that a
very light blue shade would make them
almost invisible against the sky, just the
same as, when you are looking down from
a place, light green would make it almost
invisible against the fields.
Then I think something might be done.
with regard to mixing your colours as is

Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I

countryside. The cost of such a scheme

PRICE OF BRIQUETTES.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie :
To ask the Leader of the House
whether there is, or is about to be, any
arrangement to ensure that the price
charged to the public for fuel supplied
in the form of briquettes shall be in
equitable relation to the price charged
for coal supplied in the normal state.
Mr. LESLIE: I think the question
very well explains itself. There is a
general feeling amongst the public that,
although they may have to put up with
the receipt of fuel in this briquette form
in the present unhappy circumstances,
there is no reason why they should have
to pay for it at the price of fuel of the
first class. We all know how these
briquettes are made, and we know,
speaking generally, that they are the
product of a kind of refuse which can
perhaps be best quoted as a sample of
goods for export which are far too bad
for home consumption. The feeling is
that the price that is to be paid for these
briquettes, if it is necessary to accept
them at all, should be very materially
below the price of coal. That is the point
of the question.
Mr. ROBB: Following consultation
with my right hon. Friend, the Minister
of Commerce, I am in a position to say
that the price to be charged to the public
for fuel supplied in the form of briquettes
is under consideration by the Divisional
Coal Officer and his Advisory Committee.
In fixing the price to the consumer, consideration is given to the f.o.b. price,
frieght charges, discharging and handling
costs, overhead expenses, and delivery
charges. The margin of profit will not
be in excess of that obtained for coal sold
in the normal state.
I am informed that it is anticipated
that this price investigation will result in
some diminution of the cost to the public
of this type of fuel.
Mr. LESLIE: I presume that the right
hon. Gentleman's advisers and colleagues
are considering the cost of the production
of these briquettes. He rather left that
out. He very properly referred to the
cost of freight and insurance and other
details which would be common to any
kind of coal, but there was nothing said.
about the original cost of production of
these briquettes.
Mr. ROBB: One of the things to be
taken into consideration is the f.o.b. price,
which depends on the cost of production.

Business of the House.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's sitting
for the purpose of taking the Second
Reading of the Sale of Ice-Cream (Temporary Provisions) Bill, and all stages of
the Agricultural Wages (Flax Workers)
Bill, when they are brought from the
House of Commons." The Minister of
Agriculture will explain why the House is
being asked to take all stages of the
Agricultural Wages Bill to-day.
the
The MINISTER OF AGRICULTURE
(Lord Glentoran): I must apologise to
the House for asking it to take
Agricultural Wages (Flax Workers) Bill
through all its stages at this sitting, but
I do so for two reasons. The first is that
it is solely a war measure. The second is
that the flax-pulling season will be upon
us in about three weeks time. The
Agricultural Wages Act of 1939 provides
that when any alteration is going to be
made in the wages or conditions of agricultural workers, both the workers and
the farmers shall be given fourteen days
notice. Therefore, after this House has
passed this Bill there will have to be an
interval of a further fourteen days before
the Agricultural Wages Board can decide
the actual piece-work rates.
Hon. Members who are familiar with
what happens in the country will be
aware that in three or four weeks time
the first of the flax pulling will begin,
and it is because of that fact that I am
asking you to pass this Bill this afternoon.
It was impossible to get it sooner, because
it is an agreed Bill, and it is always difficult to get two sides to agree in a matter
of this kind. We had many meetings
between the representatives of organised
labour. the Ulster Farmers' Union, and
other bodies, and I am glad to say that
in the end they came to a complete
agreement, and we are really here to confirm that agreement and pass it into law.
Therefore, I ask the House to pass the
Bill to-day purely as a war measure.
Mr. LESLIE: I am sorry that on this
occasion I must deny myself the pleasure
of falling in with the desire of the Minister of Agriculture. I do not think it is
right for this House to pass in one day,
at one sitting, all stages of a Bill of this
description. As a matter of fact, I believe
that in another place the proceedings on
this Bill were accelerated to that extent,
and I believe that that was done on or
about 23rd June, so that the Bill has been
before the country only seven days. I
Business of the House
Business of the House
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
have not myself yet examined the
measure. The Bill seems to me in theory
-because it has not yet reached us from
another place to be a Bill which is capable of amendment, and in any case it
cannot be contended, I think, that merely
because the measure has been agreed
between certain outside bodies the consent
of Parliament is to be taken as a matter
of course, and Parliament has got no say
in the matter.
The proper course is to assent to the
Motion of the right hon. Gentleman so
far as it extends to the First and Second
Readings of the Sale of Ice-Cream Bill
and this Bill, and to stop there. In order
to meet the noble Lord's suggestion of
urgency about the Flax Bill the House
might sit to-morrow, and perhaps it may
be possible to take the remaining stages
then, but I cannot consent to this House
taking all stages of that Bill to-day. The
noble Lord suggests that the pulling
season begins in three or four weeks. The
fact that the flax-pulling season will

examining the Bill. I cannot agree to
the Motion before us.
Lord GLENTORAN: I would like to
explain to the right hon. Senator that
this is not a Bill in the ordinary sense of
the word. What we are doing in this
Bill we could have done by an amendment
of the 1939 Act, but we did not do that
simply because the agricultural labourers.
look upon that Act as their charter, and
they did not want it touched. They
therefore asked us to bring in this Bill.
It does not interfere in any way with the
1939 Act, and the minimum wage still
remains unaffected. It is merely an
amendment of the 1939 Act dealing with
piece-work, and I should be loath to bring
the Senate back again to deal with such a
narrow issue. There is no question of
policy attached to the Bill, which merely
seeks to confirm a working arrangement
between the farmers on one side and the
workers on the other.
Mr. BARCLAY: Would I be right in
saying that the object of the Bill is
merely to ratify a decision that has been
come to on the part of the farmers and
the farm labourers?
Lord GLENTORAN: That is so, but
at the same time I do not quarrel with
Senator Leslie in his attitude to the
Motion before the House. I admit that
the passing of this Bill is the responsibility of this House, and if I were in
Senator Leslie's position I would probably object to the Motion. But in view
of the explanation I have given as to the
urgency of the matter I hope he will
accept my assurance that I am not trying
to force on him something I would not
like to accept myself.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: It seems to be
getting the custom to rush Bills through
this House, and after a little while, if we go
on as we are doing, I do not think there
will be any sittings of this House at all,
because I think the Government want as
few sittings as possible. After all, the
Government have had a year to consider
the introduction of this Bill. However,
if the Minister will give me a guarantee
that this measure will help the farmer
from the point of view that he will not
be overcharged for the pulling of his flax
I am prepared to meet him. At the
same time, I think this House should not
be asked to rush a Bill through all its
stages in one day.
We are at least entitled to a day or
two to consider the Bill between the
Second Reading and the Committee stage,
and I do not think a day or two days
would make any difference so far as the
Bill is concerned. For these reasons I
agree with Senator Leslie's suggestion
that the Committee stage of the Bill
should be taken to-morrow. I have

rushing Bills through this House, and in
the future I shall certainly oppose that
practice as far as I possibly can.
passing of this Bill is the responsibility
of this House, and we should not pass it
without the most careful consideration.

Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Will this Bill

beat
and a shilling a stook, and they made the

The Lord Mayor of Belfast
Mr. W. Barclay
Major J. C. Boyle
