Lord GLENTORAN: Yes, that is
exactly what the Bill is for. Last year
rich firms and some rich farmers and
speculators came along and offered the
farm labourers a very high wage, with the
result that these farm labourers left the
farmers they were working for and labour
became disorganised. In many
cases
these farm labourers fell out with the
farmers they were working for, and that
fight went on throughout August, and
the whole farming industry was
dislocated. It is for that reason this Bill
was brought in on the advice of the
farmers and flax growers. The only
reason for this Bill is to put a stop to
this dislocation.

Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Sale of

and the Agricultural Wages (Flax

Agricultural Wages (Flax Workers)
Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Moved, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time."-(Mr. Robb.)
Lord GLENTORAN: This is a very
simple measure. It deals with a standardised rate for piece workers in agriculture, and, as I have already said, it
does not interfere in any way with the
minimum rate for agricultural workers
laid down in the Act of 1939. We found
that under that Act certain people came
along and offered a very high wage to
farm labourers for flax pulling, and, in
fact, they enticed labourers away from
Mr. W. S. Stevenson
Mr. H. Taylor
Mr. G. Whaley
Mr. J. McHugh
Mr. T. McLaughlin
their ordinary work. The result of that
was that there was dislocation in the
farming effort, and it had the effect of
making the growing of flax unpopular.
Men who for years had grown flax would
not grow it this year, because they said
there was such a disturbance created by
their men being taken from them, and
they just would not grow flax at all.
Considering that the growing of flax
is so essential for the national war effort
it was up to my Ministry to do whatever
was possible in order to put a stop to
this state of affairs, and create a better
feeling between the farm labourers and
the farmers. Having been approached
by the Ulster Farmers' Union on
the subject, the next thing I had to
do was to get in touch with organised
labour, and I had a considerable amount
of negotiating to do with organised labour
because they were very shy and thought
something was going to take place that
would interfere with the 1939 Act, which
they looked upon as something gained for
the workers of this country. I assured.
C
Agricultural Wages Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Lord Glentoran]
them, as I assure this House, that the
last thing I would do would be to bring
in any sort of Bill that would interfere
with the 1939 Act, and anything contained in this measure does not in any
way interfere with the minimum wages
as fixed by the Agricultural Wages Board
under the 1939 Act.
A question I was asked in the other
House was, who is to settle the piecework rates. That is to be settled by the
Agricultural Wages Board. One other
point of importance that was raised in
another place was about ordinary wages.
The wages fixed under the 1939 Act are
minimum wages, but under this Bill there
is no reason whatever why, for instance,
a mill owner or a firm should not say to
their employees, "I want you in the
national interest to come and pull the
flax which I have grown and which will
afterwards be used by the firm." In that
case there is nothing in this Bill to prevent the firm paying their employees the
engineer's or fitter's or whatever other
wages are applicable to their particular
trade.
The Bill does not prevent a man
receiving a higher weekly wage for flax
pulling, but it does prevent him being
paid a lower weekly wage than that fixed
by the Board. The basis of the whole
Bill is to endeavour to create good-will
as between the farm labourer and the
farmer, and thus to put a stop to the
dislocation of our farm effort, and,
generally, to improve the production of
flax. It is a very small Bill, and in many
senses it is not an ordinary Bill at all. It
was passed with good-will in the other.
House, as I am sure it will be in this
House, and I am confident it will do
away with a lot of the trouble we had
last year.
Mr. LESLIE: The Bill has been, to
some extent, discussed on the preliminary
Motion that it should be expedited
through its several stages, and I must
avoid repeating anything that other
Members said during that stage. I think
I may claim that I myself kept to the
point before us, which was whether the
procedure would be accelerated, and I did
not say anything about the merits of the
Bill. I did not even claim to have read
it. I am bound to say I am very doubtful whether it will not do more harm
than good. I am bound to say doubtful.
I cannot make a definite assertion because
I think it is one of those cases where it
may do good in some parts of the Province
and do harm in others.
The noble Lord talked about the
desirability of getting good-will. Sometimes you got good-will last year without -
the Bill and sometimes you will get it this
year, but there is something in the Bill
which will stop you getting it universally.
That is the main point. It is all very
well to try to standardise prices for
piece work, but to do that satisfactorily
your piece work must be uniform in
character. Flax pulling is not uniform
in character, and never can be. There
are too many uncertain factors. One
field may be full of thistles, and in
another field the flax may be lying down,
and the field may be at some distance
from where the puller lives.
All those are matters which were taken
into consideration, or might be, by the
owner of the field and the man or men
who were going to pull the flax. As the
Bill stands now there is no play whatever.
You are going to fix a standard price,
but there is no play at all. There is no
such provision as, if a field is full of
thistles a man may be paid five per cent.
extra, and it is the absence of that that
seems to me likely to prevent the good
will which is so very desirable.
Again, the method of differentiation as
between one farmer and another is not
solely a question of price. There are
other things. One man, for instance,
may give refreshments to his pullers, and
another man may give a higher class of
refreshments and so on, and there are
other differences. If a man wants to
gain an advantage over a fellow employer
by giving his workers better terms, he can
do it without making any variation in the
rate of wages.
In the case of the thistle
man, I think he ought to get more money
than the man who pulls a clean field of
flax, but he will not.
Your present condition will continue,
and the flax puller will look about for
the easiest job he can find, because some
of these men who are expert pullers make
between £1 and 30s a day. They do not
work hours which the Ministry of Agriculture would approve, but they do work
and get the flax pulled, and that is what
Agricultural Wages Bill-
Second Reading
30 JUNE 1942
or
the country wants. It is not so much
concerned with whether one man earns
than another, whether
more money
one man gets his field cut before another.
The price will be fixed by the Agricultural
Wages Board.
I must say that the Board made itself
rather unpopular in the country this
year, because it re-adjusted wages once
and a very short time afterwards put
them up again. That annoyed people a
good deal, because they laid their schemes
on the assumption that the wages would
be so and so, and then, after only fourteen days notice, which was given in a
very indifferent manner, it was changed
again, and there will be a natural apprehension as to how long the rate fixed by
the Board is going to last. It will
scarcely be varied this season, but the
Bill deals with next year also.
The price of flax has been fixed for this
year, but I do not think it has been fixed
for next year. That is the point that comes
in. Before a man allows himself to grow
flax next year, he will want to know what
wages he has to pay. If the Board fixes
the wage too high, then the growth of
flax will certainly go down. If a higher
wage can still be paid and the amount of
flax does not go down, it follows that the
present price paid to the farmer for his
flax is too high, and I do not think that
is a suggestion that will meet with much
favour.
Also, of course, there will be a disturbance of work in other branches of
tillage, but that is rather a small pointat least, I hope it is. If, on the other
hand, you fix the wage too low the casual
flax puller will not pull flax, and it will
not be pulled. Whichever way you look
at it, however much it may have been
discussed between the Farmers' Union and
organised labour-and I do not know
quite what the noble Lord meant by
organised labour, because I do not know
that in my own part of the country the
flax-pulling people are organised, and I
am not at all sure that they will accept
that very able and very vigorous organisation called the Transport Workers' Union
as representing them. At all events, I
do not think the country flax puller has
been duly consulted, and if he had been
I am inclined to think he would have
objected to all flax, good and bad, being
put in the same class.
SO
I do not propose to oppose the Bill,
because I recognise that the conditions
may be
very different in different
districts and amongst various people, and
the Minister has evidently made some
inquiries. It may be that what is proposed may make for the general good and
for the maintenance of the supply of flax.
I do not think it will increase the supply
next year,
but it perhaps will not do
anything to diminish it, and it may be,
as the Minister says, that there may be
more peace this year. I cannot say I
observed any serious state of warfare as
between the farmer and the flax puller,
but, as I say, I do not propose to oppose
the measure.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: While this Bill
may not do a very great deal of good it
cannot do much harm, and I think the
Minister of Agriculture is to be congratulated on bringing in a Bill to bring
the farmer and the labourer together.
If this Bill succeeds in stopping the profiteering in the pulling of flax everyone
of us will be glad. In the past, particularly when there was a rush on, it
was the custom of farm labourers to get
together and form a company and to
wring out of the farmer all they could
possibly get. If that practice is done
away with I shall be satisfied. In fixing
the wages for piece work I hope the Board
will not fix the rate too low and make
the labourers grumble, because I believe
it is time there was a levelling up of
wages in this country. It is not often I
congratulate a Minister of this Government, but I should like to congratulate the Minister of Agriculture,
because I think he is doing great work.
I saw a report he sent out last week, and
that was certainly to his credit. Lord
Glentoran is doing very good work for
agriculture in this country.
Major BOYLE: I should like to ask
the Minister whether the rates to be fixed
will apply to the men who pull the flax
out of the dams. That seems to me to
be a more difficult job than the pulling
of the flax. I understand that in many
cases last year when the flax was pulled
and put into the dams there was great
difficulty in some places in getting men
to take the flax out of the water, for the
same reason as there was difficulty in
getting it pulled, namely, the difficulty
of agreeing to wages, and therefore I
hope this Bill covers that point. Senator
Agricultural Wages Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Major Boyle]
Leslie referred to the refreshments given
to their workers by some farmers, and I
am told that in some cases even whisky
is provided.
Major DOBBS: I would like to ask
the noble Lord one or two questions. So
far as I can make out a standard rate is
proposed; not a minimum rate. What
is to happen to a farmer who offers to
pay a wage higher than the standard
rate? Will that standard rate be one
rate for the whole Province, or will it
alter as the Agricultural Wages Board
has altered its rates for various localities?
As it is piece work you cannot very well
have overtime, but supposing the weather
is bad and you want to work every day
of the week, what about the Sunday?
Will there be an extra rate for Sunday
work, or, if the men do work on Sunday,
must they accept the rate fixed for
Monday, Tuesday, and the other days of
the week? I am sure we all welcome the
Bill, because we realise that something
had to be done, and I do hope the Bill
will effect its purpose.
Lord GLENTORAN: I would like to
thank the House for the way it has received this Bill. Senator Leslie asked
some very pertinent questions which I
would like to answer. He referred to
the difference in the character of flax
fields, and said the man who had a
difficult field to cut should receive more
than the man who had an easy field to
cut. That happens in all classes of piece
work. In the case of a plater on a-ship,
according to the way the ship is perhaps
listing, the man working on one side has
a soft job compared to the man on the
other side. That is always the difficulty
in the case of piece work. In any form
of manual work it is impossible to have
the same conditions. It is just luck.
You may get some squads at the Queen's
Island who will make £30 or £40 a week,
and you will get another squad that will
hardly make their minimum wage.
is the same with painting and all other
piece work.
Mr. LESLIE: And coal mining?
It
Lord GLENTORAN: It is very much
so in coal mining, because very often you
get an easy seam and more difficult seams.
Mr. LESLIE: Do they not vary the
rates?
Lord GLENTORAN: No, not where
the men are working on the same seam.
That is a difficulty experienced in every
industry with which I have had anything
to do, as those connected with labour
organisations will agree. The right hon.
Gentleman also referred to the case of one
farmer who treated his men better than
another farmer, and Senator Major
Boyle said some farmers even gave their
men whisky. I think we would all be
pulling flax these days if we got whisky.
A man is not going to leave good employment simply because he is getting tea. He
is only tempted away because he will get
a bigger wage. This Bill deals only with
the ordinary farm labourer and not casual
labour
or even the professional flax
puller.
Senator Leslie also referred to the fact
that the Agricultural Wages Board raised
wages twice last year. That was not such
a crime as it might appear. The prices
of farmers' products were raised and the
labourers were given a little piece off the
cake. Although I am an employer of
labour, when I am making a bit I do not
mind the other fellow also making a bit.
When the farmers got a subsidy of £10
an acre for potatoes the Agricultural
Wages Board thought the labourers
should get their share of it, and that is
the answer to that. It may be that next
year the price fixed may be higher if the
growers are going to get a higher price
for their flax. We all want to see fair
play all round, and we will never get
that unless we share our profits with our
labourers. Senator Major Boyle referred
to the difficulty of getting the flax out
of the dams. That is not dealt with in
this Bill, but it will probably be borne
in mind by the Board when fixing the
rates.
In answer to Senator Major Dobbs, if
a farmer pays a higher rate for this work
than that prescribed he will be prosecuted. The question of working on
Sundays is not one I should like to deal
with, but I have no doubt whatever that
the Wages Board will consider the question
of overtime generally. All these matters
will come within the purview of the
Agricultural Wages Board, which is composed of intelligent men on both sides,
and men who thoroughly understand
farming conditions. I again thank hon.
Members for the favourable reception they
have given to the Bill.

casual labour other than employment in

I

it is more acute than in others.
Lord GLENTORAN: This is purely
one of the matters which will have to be
taken into consideration by the Agricultural Wages Board. They will have
before them all the evidence as to what
has happened in these particular districts.
The object of the provisions in this clause
is to see that these workers receive a fair
general wage, one that is neither too
high nor too low, and one that they will
be likely to accept. I have not got the
necessary evidence before me.
It
Mr. LESLIE: What I am afraid of is

will be very much easier for the competent flax puller to get very much more

Sale of Ice-Cream (Temporary Provisions) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time." This
is a very short Bill, and quite simple.
Under the Act of 1937 vendors of icecream were bound to put in milk fat as
a constituent of ice-cream. Owing to
the difficulty of getting milk fat it is
proposed by this Bill to exempt them
from that restriction and to allow them
to use vegetable fat. That will be the
only difference in the virtues of ice-cream
in the future, so I am informed.
Mr. LESLIE: Of course, this is a
perfectly simple little Bill, but according to the newspapers there will be no
ice-cream manufactured after 30th
September next. I should like to ask
if there has been any sale of ice-cream
contrary to the provisions of this Statute.
If so you ought to introduce words of
indemnity. What I thought of doing
is to suggest on the Committee stage that
we should say the Bill is deemed to have
come into operation on 1st January last,
which would protect anybody who has
sold ice-cream in the meantime. If
people have not conformed to the
principal Act they stand to be shot at,
partly by the Minister of Agriculture
who need not pull the trigger unless he
wants to, and also by the local authorities who are concerned with the enforcement of the Ice-Cream Act.
Mr. ROBB: I really cannot say
whether that point has been considered,
and if the hon. Member will put down
an amendment for the Committee stage
I will consider it in the meantime.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Royal Assent.
Mr. ROBB: I have to report to the

assent to the following Bills agreed upon
by both Houses of Parliament:-

Questions to Minister.
INTERNEES IN BELFAST PRISON.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :-
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Government proposes to
release the remaining 16 men who have
been interned since 22nd December,
1938, or, if not willing to release them,
will put them on trial on whatever
charges he can prefer against them;
whether he can state how many internees are now in Belfast Prison
without trial; whether it is a fact that
at present there are 320 internees
living in cells erected in basements to
accommodate 150 men; whether
number of internees are boarded in the
convict portion of the prison, and, if
so, for what reason, and can he state
why a permit for a Flag Day was refused to the Green Cross Fund.
a
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I should like to
say that the internees ask for no protection from the Minister of Home Affairs.
All they ask is the right of a free trial
before a properly constituted tribunal
if the Minister has any charges to make
against them. If he can frame no
charges, then surely it is not too much.
to ask that it is time that men interned,
some for almost four years, without trial
should be released.
These men are not criminals. They
have every right to that freedom to which
citizenship of any democratic country
entitles them, and no government professing to fight for freedom can justify
depriving these men of their liberty any
longer without trial. If it is true that
these internees are obliged to sleep in that
part of the prison assigned to convicts,
then I say such a state of affairs would
not be tolerated in any freedom-loving
democratic country, and I here and now
draw the attention of the Red Cross
Society to this state of affairs. These
internees are entitled to at least the same
treatment and conditions as prisoners of
war. The only difference I can see is, if
they were prisoners of war the Governdare not treat them in such a way.
Again, if it is true that upwards of 320
of these interned men are confined in
cells in the basement of the prison
which were originally fitted out for the
accommodation of 150 men, then I think
the sooner an inquiry is held into the
question of accommodation for these internees in Belfast Prison the better for
all concerned. A man called Mulgrew
was interned for six months. After he
was released he spent 13 years in the
Special Constabulary. In another place
reference was made to my visit to internees on the ship Al Rawdah. I consider it very low and mean for any.
Minister of the Crown to poke fun at
these men, and to use his position in
censoring their letters for the purpose of
spying on his fellow Members of Parliament, and in the course of censoring
letters of internees using portions of them
which he considers suit his policy to
attack Members of this Parliament, whose
duty it is to protect in this House their
fellow citizens.
The Minister's duty, under the Censorship Act, is to see that nothing is contained in any letter leaving the prison
which would impair the war effort or the
peace of the community. There his duty
ends, and I say emphatically he has no
right whatever which entitles him to use
any portion of these internees' letters for
his own political purposes. I leave it to
the public to judge his action in this
matter. All I say is that such misuse by
the Ministry of its powers under the
Censorship Regulations will not deter
either my colleagues or myself from doing
our duty and exposing any unjust treatment of these men during their internment and while under the Minister's
protection.
I now wish to refer to the refusal to
allow a flag day to be held for that
deserving fund known. as the Green
Cross. In doing this the Minister has
again shown his bigotry. I am a trustee
of the Green Cross Fund. The constitution of that fund can be examined at any
time by the Ministry. The Minister had
no ground for stating that the fund was
for the defence of these internees; that
statement is untrue. The Fund is solely
for the relief of the dependants of those
internees who require it. We intend to
stand by them, and see to it that they do
not starve. It is a perfectly legitimate
fund, and is as much entitled to a permit
to hold a flag day as any other fund in
the Six Counties. I know the Minister
of Home Affairs does not like it or those
connected with it, and if he can prove the
objects or intentions of the fund are not
legitimate he will
allow it to
function.
not
I have put this question out of a sense
of duty. I do not mind how many letters
are censored by the Minister of Home
Questions to Minister
4 AUGUST 1942
Questions to Minister

and destruction of that elementary right
of citizenship?
Affairs as far as these internees are
concerned. I certainly do say that no
Minister of the Crown, or any official of
the Crown, has any right to
any portion of an internee's letter,
which is private and not connected with
public affairs, against his colleagues in
this House or any other House.
use
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The reply to the first part of the question
is in the negative. In December, 1938,
34 persons were interned. Of these, 16
appealed against internment and 14 were
subsequently released. The fact that the
majority of these men have not appealed
is some justification for their continued internment, and the Government does not,
therefore, propose either to release them
or to prefer specific charges against them.
There are at present 347 persons interned in Belfast Prison. Only 10 are in
the basement cells, the remainder being
in cells on the ground floor and upper
floors.
There are 178 cells in this part of the
prison which have been fitted with double
bunks. None of the internees are
boarded in the convict wing of the prison.
A permit to hold a flag day in Belfast
in aid of the Green Cross Fund was refused because of the undesirability of
increasing the number of flag days in the
city.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I have no
authority for speaking for any of these
internees. I have not been approached
by any of them, but I feel impelled to
condemn this whole system on the ground
of humanitarianism, justice, and equity.
It used to be our proud boast, a principle
which we cherished, that a man was presumed to be innocent until he was proved
guilty. Not only has that position been
reversed by the reactionary methods of
this Government over the last 20 years,
but even an innocent man is presumed to
be guilty and is deprived of his right to
prove his innocence because he is not
brought before an impartial tribunal.
The right of a man to be tried by his
peers was a right conferred by Magna
Charta. That was a cherished right
which, before the establishment of this
Government, was treated as sacrosanct,
and what justification has been given by
the Leader of the House for the repealing
I think the House will agree-although
it may not say so vocally-that there has
been no answer, good, bad, or indifferent,
given by the Leader of the House, who
is briefed by the Minister of Home
Affairs, to the indictment that has been
made against this reactionary and, I say
deliberately, grossly partial Government
in its transactions during the past 20
years. I have seen it stated in public,
and I have been assured by men of the
most irreproachable character, scrupulous
men whose word cannot be challenged,
that a number of these interned men,
breadwinners who are deprived of their
liberty, are perfectly innocent of any
breach of the law or contemplated breach
of the law. I was going to say they are
entirely innocent of any charge that has
been brought against them, but in this
time of a world-wide war for democracy
and liberty, not even a charge is preferred
against these men. Is not that a diabolical state of affairs in these days?
If that is the character of a number of
these men, what are we to say about their
jailor? It is public property, and it is
history, that he was steeped up to the
neck in rebellion and treason. That is
the history of the Minister of Home
Affairs, who was chief of the gun running
and had certain members of the Cabinet
associated with him. That is the man
who has the political audacity to deprive
decent, law-abiding citizens of their
liberty. Is it not a shocking state of
affairs, and a shocking political outrage
in this civilised and so-called Christian
country? What answer has the jailor of
these men to that charge? He boasted of
his treason and rebellion, and he deprived these people of the right to be
tried, or has not the courage to charge
them.
With regard to the Green Cross Fund,
my colleague, Mr. McLaughlin, is the
treasurer of that fund. It is a well
known fact that innocent breadwinners,
who have followed constitutional methods
all their lives, are detained. Are their
families to be allowed to starve in this
so-called Christian country under a
Christian Government? Are people who
want to do an act of charity to be deprived of contributing to the support of
these innocent people?
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
With regard to the reference that has
been made by Senator McLaughlin to the
Parliamentary Secretary's publication of a
letter, I intend to take a more dignified
and, I hope, more effective course, for I
think any man or public representative
capable of such conduct should be
ignored, and that is what I suggest should
be the treatment meted out to the Parliamentary Secretary, because any public
representative debasing his position and
adopting such methods is of such a temperament that I do not think any
remarks, however scathing and damning,
would have the least effect upon him. We
are here to demand, as an elementary
right of justice, that these men be released or have the right of Magna Charta
to be tried by their peers.
It is common,
elementary justice. It was justice in the
days before this reactionary Government
was established in Northern Ireland.
That is the only thing that we demand on
behalf of these men who are so shamelessly detained.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: The Leader of

are interned in the convict portion. My

that portion of the prison?
Mr. ROBB: I would assume that the

boarded."
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: None of them
sleep there at night.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I am greatly surprised that only 16 internees appealed.
Mr. ROBB: Sixteen of 34 at the date
in question.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I would like very
much to get the numbers who have
appealed during the past three years.
Mr. ROBB: If the hon. Member puts
down a question I will get the information.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I know many cases
where internees appealed on several
occasions as many as three occasionsand they were prepared to sign all the
documents required by the Ministry and
give bail, but their appeal was turned
down on each occasion. I feel that the
internees are very disheartened. They
think there is no possibility of being
released after making an appeal to the
committee. I think that has deterred
them from appealing. They have had
experience of that for two or three years.
I have known some 32 cases where appeals
were made, and they were all turned
down. That was 18 months ago. Since
that several have appealed again, but
they were also turned down, and the men
are still interned.
SALE OF WHEAT, BARLEY AND
OAT STRAW.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the
of Major
Dobbs :-
name
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state what, if any, restrictions will be placed on the sale of
wheat, barley, or oat straw in the
coming season; whether the shipment
of straw to England will be permitted;
whether local paper mills can now use
straw commercially for paper-making;
and, if so, whether the sale of straw to
them will be permitted.
Major DOBBS: The farmers in my
district are very anxious to know the
position in regard to straw, especially
whether they will be able to sell straw for
shipping across to England as last year,
or direct to the local market.
Mr. ROBB: The sale of straw in
Northern Ireland is governed by an order
made by the Ministry of Food.
This order fixes the maximum price,
but places no other restrictions on the sale
of straw or its shipment to Great Britain.
There is no restriction on the use of
straw for paper making, but the manufacturers must purchase their requirements from the Ministry of Supply in the
form of straw pulp.
The local paper manufacturers are
aware of this arrangement.
answer.
Major DOBBS: I did not quite get the
Will the straw be allowed to be
shipped to England, because last year
there was great difficulty in getting permits for ships to take the straw.
Mr. ROBB: Yes.

Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, “That the

to make either grants or loans for the

respect of applications received by the
Ministry up to and including the 31st
December, 1944.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in subclause 2, after "Act," insert the words,
"shall be deemed to have come into
operation on the first day of January,
nineteen hundred and forty-two, and.'
It may be remembered that I mentioned
this little matter on the Second Reading.
The point was, as far as I could see, that
although we could protect a man from
action on the part of the Ministry of
Agriculture, we could not protect him
against action by an inspector of the
Food and Drugs Act appointed under the
local authority. It seemed to me, if a
case had arisen, we ought to protect him,
and the only way I could think of doing
that was to date the Bill back to the 1st
January. That will give all the neces
sary protection.
Mr. ROBB: The Government accepts
the amendment.

part of the Bill.

Laid before the House (pursuant to

Sale of Ice-Cream (Temporary

Order for receiving Report of Amendment read.
Resolved, "That this Report be now
received." (Mr. Robb.)
The said Amendment was accordingly
reported and agreed to by the House.
Ordered, That the Bill be read the
Third time on Tuesday next.
Electricity (Emergency Supplies) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, "That this Bill be now read
a Second time."
When the present war broke out, and
experience had shown the great damage
modern bombing could do, then in all
parts of the United Kingdom anxiety
was felt as to what would happen should
the electrical generating stations be
damaged. A duty was imposed on electricity undertakers to provide stand-by
plant in reasonably safe positions, so that
should their original plant be destroyed.
or damaged, then the manufacturies
which depended on electric power should
not be entirely stopped, and the amenities
which electric light and power gave to
the public generally should not cease.
In Northern Ireland, by far the largest
producer of electricity is the Belfast Corporation. So great a delay occurred in
providing a stand-by plant for their
station-I am not now laying the blame
for this on anyone-that under pressure
from England the Northern Ireland
Government undertook directly to provide
this re-insurance plant. It is not thought
advisable to go into any detail as to the
actual measures taken. I need only say
that the plant provided includes generating plant and transmission lines. But in
strictness the Minister of Commerce,
through whom the work was carried out,
had legally no authority either to supply
a power station and transmission lines or
operate them.
This Bill, in its first clause, supplies
this authority, and further provides that
anything the Ministry has done since 1st
January, 1941, and which would have
been within the powers conferred by the
Bill, shall be deemed to have been done
within the law. As to the cost of the
work, the Imperial Government made a
grant of one-half, and following the general
principle adopted in all war measures
that each industry shall bear the cost of
its own insurance; the Bill, in Clause 7,
supplies a method for recovering this cost
from the electricity undertakings in our
area.
There are other things incidental to the
main purpose as I have described it. It
may be advisable during war time, that
the Ministry should take over some plants
either entirely or partially by directing
their operations. The Bill gives this
power, but it expressly provides that the
undertaking of the Belfast Corporation or
Derry Corporation shall be taken over
only by agreement, and in any other case.
there are elaborate provisions in the Bill.
and Schedules so that such acquisition.
shall only be made on the payment of
compensation fixed by arbitration.
The provisions as to spreading the cost
are in Clause 7. In outline, they are
that the Ministry shall arrive at a certified deficiency shown by accounts kept in
Electricity Bill—
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
accordance with the terms of the Bill,
and shall then be reimbursed by the
existing electrical undertaking by contributions fixed by the Ministry.
There are in the Bill provisions incidental to the main purposes. By Clause
4, when the Ministry supplies electricity
in bulk the price to be charged shall, in
default of agreement and subject to the
terms of any existing contract, be fixed
by Order made by the Ministry and shall
be framed so as to show separately (a) a
fixed kilowatt charges component, and (b)
a running charges component. But before any order fixing a tariff comes into
operation it must be laid before Parliament, and if either House of Parliament,
within the statutory period, resolves that
the order ought not to come into force,
no further proceedings are to be taken
under it.
Clause 5 enables the Ministry to make
connections between transmission lines on
agreed terms, and Clause 6 forbids, without consent of the Ministry, the variation
of existing agreements as to supply of
electricity.
Clause 9 provides for the exemption
from local rates of any new station constructed by the Ministry. Clause 10 protects the employees of existing undertakings taken over by the Ministry
against loss, either by the loss of their
employment or change in the nature of
their functions. Clause 11 provides for
the meeting of the Ministry's expenses.
from moneys provided by Parliament.
Clause 12 is the definition section.
The compulsory powers of the Ministry
are expressly limited to the period of the
present emergency. This is defined as
beginning on 1st January, 1941, and
ending on such date as the Governor may
by order declare, and the statutory period
within which an order made by the
Ministry may be annulled by either House
of Parliament as 5 days on which the
particular House sits after the making of
the order, but in no case less than 10
days.
This is purely a war Bill. The compulsory powers of the Ministry are expressly limited to the period of the present.
emergency. The Bill does not purport to
decide what shall, after the war, be the
structure of the machinery for the supply
of electricity generally, or how that
machinery shall be framed or controlled.
People have different interests and
different ideas on this, which will have to
be discussed and decided after the war.
This Bill leaves those problems untouched,
and is confined to the matter with which
I have dealt.
Question put, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
Mr.
I
JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
think every Member of the House will
admit this is a Bill that is necessary,
owing to war-time exigencies and the
dangerous position in which we may find
ourselves. It is necessary to scatter the
generating stations as much as possible
so that there would be no great inconvenience caused to consumers of electricity. Clause 7 is perhaps the principal
clause in the Bill, and I think it is not
altogether satisfactory. It says the
Ministry shall apportion the certified
deficiency among such authorised undertakers, including the Ministry, in respect
of any undertakings acquired under this
Act, and in such proportion as the Ministry may think it equitable to determine.
I think it would have been much better
to have left it to some outside body to
decide in what manner the deficiency
should be apportioned. The Belfast Corporation have the most up-to-date and the
most extensive plant in Northern Ireland,
and it is not likely they would show a
deficiency in the working of their concern
or that they would be responsible for any
deficiency in the pool.
One would think that whatever undertaking caused a deficiency would be entitled to bear that responsibility, and in
that respect the Ministry is one of the
undertakings. I do think that instead of
leaving it to the Ministry to apportion
the deficiency, it would have been much
better to have left it to some outside body,
such as an arbitrator or some accountant
body. That would have given a great
deal more satisfaction than will the provision in the Bill. Apart from that, I
do not think there is anything in the
Bill one can complain of. It makes very
necessary provision for the supply of
electricity to the whole of the people of
Northern Ireland, and particularly for
those who are doing all they possibly can
to provide munitions and machinery for
the prosecution of the war.
Electricity Bill-
Second Reading
6 AUGUST 1942
Mr. LESLIE: It seems to me that
what I might describe as fairly extensive
powers are asked for by the Ministry of
Commerce under this, and it is now clear
-I say now, because I am not at all sure
that it was clear when this Bill was first
introduced in another place-but it is
now clear that the Bill is a war measure.
Therefore, I do not think this House is
likely to raise any objection to the main
principles of the Bill. I do not think
that, in principle, anybody will attack
what the right hon. Gentleman described
as the re-insurance principle which runs
through it, and I am inclined to think
that the House will not agree with the
hon. Senator who has just sat down as
regards apportionment.
I looked at the question of apportionment of cost, and I think that the question of some form of appeal was the first
thing which arose to my mind, but afterwards I came to the conclusion that the
matter was one of figures and that the
Ministry might do it as well as anybody
else. I do not think there would be any
cause to interfere on that head.
a
I do not suppose one can help having
a good deal of curiosity as regards what
will happen in the future, after the war
is over. All we can do is to suppress that
curiosity for the moment. We can remember that there is an Electricity
Board in existence, and there is nothing
in this Bill which proposes to determine
the energies and occupations of that
Board. Taking it as war measure,
which it is agreed it is, it seems to me to
have a dual object. That dual object
seems to be, first of all—this is the point
on which the right hon. Gentleman laid
the greatest stress-power to deal with
possible war damage; power to counteract
the operations of the enemy, and,
secondly-this is the point on which the
right hon. Gentleman did not lay so much
stress-to enable provision to be made for
assisting the war effort. I think both
these aims may be described as the object
of the Bill before us.
In each of those two cases it seems to
me it is essential that the Bill should be
as simple as possible, but above that, it is
more essential that we should do everything we can to see that expedition is
secured. We want a Bill which will
enable the Ministry to work with the
utmost possible expedition. It is a Bill
which must have been a rather difficult
one
to draw, and not one easy to
follow. I think the difficulties arise
principally from the fact that the electricity code is very intricate. Part of it,
as hon. Members will see, is 60 years old.
Two measures bulk very largely in
the text of the Bill-our own Act of Parliament which was passed in 1931-it is
more than ten years old-and the
Imperial Parliament Act of 1919, which
is more than 20 years old. Those are
pretty long periods. During such periods
things change a good deal, even definitions
which upset matters. Certainly science
advances in one way or another. In fact,
there are one or two little puzzles which
are left in my own mind, matters which
I shall bring before the House later on
in the hope of getting explanations which
will prevent the Bill being subject to any
further criticism. I shall put those
matters on the paper.
There are one or two trifles which we
will deal with in the ordinary course, and
which I need not mention to-day. I do
wish it were possible to get the word
"main" out of the Bill. I say that because it is coupled with transmission lines
and thus causes confusion. At all events,
in the main we ought to aim at the two
points I have mentioned-ensuring expedition in our proceedings, and avoiding,
if we can inadvertently tying ourselves up
in any form of knot. There was one
form of knot which I thought was incapable of being untied until the right
hon. Gentleman spoke. I do not think
it now exists, but we shall see later on.
There are two or three matters which I
want to mention now, because they are
really, I think, rather vital. I am taking
them in the order in which they appear
in the Bill. First of all, I do not admire
very much what I may call the slimming
treatment proposed to be applied to the
shadow of his late Majesty King Henry
VIII-sub-clause (2) of Clause 2. I know
there is some sort of precedent, but I do
not think that method is suited to our
present case. It does not provide for
expedition, and, accordingly, I am going
to ask, later on, the Committee to substitute a somewhat similar form of wording,
taken from the Chartered Bodies (Temporary Provisions) Act which we passed
two years ago.
That form will be found
rather better, and it will certainly make
for expedition. Before we do that it
will be necessary to insert some four or

[Mr. Leslie]
five words limiting the power of adaptation to the present emergency. That
simply follows an amendment introduced
in another place which is now, I think,

simple matter.
-
The second important point this
arises on the question of compensation
and was mentioned by Senator Joseph
Cunningham-is in regard to Part I of
the First Schedule. We talk of fixing
something rather by special order. As
the House knows, the expression "special
order' has a very special meaning under
the Electricity Acts. When we come to
that stage we can get rid of it and substitute the words "by order" in the same
way as we are doing in Clause 4 of the
Bill before us. I want to mention that
specially, because the question of that
expression, "special order," is one that
requires looking into. On the face of
things the very subject of it is, one would
think, a case in which the special order
procedure would be fair and simple.
Again, it is not expeditious.
The third point is the question of
rating. The Clause that relates to the
exemption from rates, Clause 9, was subject to some alteration in another place.
I do not object to it myself as it stands,
but apparently an urban district council
operating not far from where I live wrote
to the Ministry, and, according to this.
morning's newspapers, the reply which
the Ministry sent does not correspond
with the wording of the Bill before us.
I do not think I need go into that in
detail, because I am hoping that the right
hon. Gentleman will explain that on the
Question, "that the Clause stand part of
the Bill," at a later date.
The only amendments I am putting
down are directed to secure greater
expedition, and, in one or two cases, to
make sure there is no complication.
There are two or three things which I
cannot follow, but that may be due to my
lack of experience in regard to electrical
matters. It seems only necessary to support the Bill as regards its main parts.
There is nothing in the subordinate points
I have alluded to which would lead anybody to offer opposition to the Bill.
Major DOBBS: May I, first of all,
thank the Leader of the House for his
very clear explanation of the Bill. I am
surprised that we are dealing with this
measure now, when we might have dealt
with it two weeks after the 1st January,
1941, or in the beginning of 1938. The
part dealing with power stations is very
important and should have been dealt
with long ago. The urgency of the Bill
from the war point of view is obvious to
us all, and I think we are all agreed. I
hope the long view will be taken and that
the Bill will mean that all sorts of electrical supplies will be amalgamated; that
all companies and bodies engaged in the
distribution of electric current will be
amalgamated so that, eventually, we shall
have in the Province a good and cheap
supply of electricity. While the war has
done us a tremendous amount of harm,
if the state of affairs I have mentioned
comes to pass, the war will have benefited
us to some extent. I have pleasure in
supporting the Bill.
Mr. ROBB: I wish to thank the House
for the reception given to the Bill. I
shall endeavour to clear up any points on
the Committee stage, when I see the
amendments that may be put down. The
only point raised to-day was that by
Senator Cunningham in regard to the
Ministry acting as the apportionment
authority. The view was held that the
Ministry were the people who had the
best means of getting information as to
how these charges should be apportioned.
Of course, they pay a proportion themselves, and there is a technical objection
to their acting as the apportionment
authority, but we do not think, in practice, any real difficulty will arise.
expedition is one of the main objects of
our legislation, the delay of appointing
some outside body to hear the cases should
be avoided. I hope to deal at a later date
with any points which may arise on any
amendments that may be tabled.

INTERNEES IN BELFAST PRISON.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Maguire:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state the number of
persons interned in each of the following years, 1939, 1940, and the seven
months ended on 31st July, 1942; the
number who appealed to the Appeals
Tribunal for release in each year; the
number of persons released in each
year; and how many internees have reappealed and been turned down by the
Appeals Tribunal.
Mr. MAGUIRE: My information in
regard to these internees is that a great
percentage of them have appealed, and
although they were prepared to sign all
the undertakings required a large percentage of them have been turned down.
In consequence they are getting very despondent and disheartened, and a good
many of them are prevented from appealing owing to the fact that the great
majority of those who have appealed have
had their appeals disallowed. Several
of them have appealed on more than one
occasion, and they have been turned
down.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The numbers of persons interned in the
year 1939 and subsequently are as
follows:-
Number interned 83

appealed

ending

Twenty internees who have appealed on
more than one occasion are still in
internment. These figures do not include
the cases which were heard by the
these
Appeals Tribunal last week, as
cases have not yet been decided.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I suggest to the
right hon. Gentleman that there is grave
dissatisfaction regarding the question of
these appeals, and that they are causing
great agitation. I suggest to him that
a verbatim report of the proceedings at
this so-called appeal court be taken and
be made available to the Members of this
House, and, therefore, to the public, so
that the public may have some idea of
what is taking place with reference to
these appeals.
Mr. ROBB: It is a suggestion I will
put before the Minister concerned, but
I do not expect it will be accepted.
Mr. MAGUIRE: The Leader of the
House has stated that during the past
seven months 21 internees out of the 44
who appealed have been released. I am
surprised that a greater percentage have.
not been released, because I am informed
that those internees who have appealed
were prepared to sign all the necessary
documents asked for by the Government,
and also to give bail. Under these circumstances I am surprised that only 50
per cent. of those who have appealed
during the past seven months have been
released.
Mr. ROBB: The percentage of those
who have been released has varied
tremendously. In 1939, 29 out of the 31
who appealed were released; in 1940, 58
out of 73; and in 1941, 53 out of 70.

Mr. McLAUGHLIN: If the Minister

internees will go before this tribunal expecting to get fair play. If the Government want to have a tribunal that is fair
the present tribunal should be reconstituted to include members of each party,

OWNERSHIP OF RECLAIMED
LAND.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state who, in the case
of lands reclaimed under the Northern
Ireland Drainage Acts, the Bann
Drainage Scheme, or other Drainage
Acts such as exhausted and reclaimed
bogs, are the owners of the lands so
reclaimed; whether these lands became
the property of the Government at the
passing of the Land Purchase Acts, and
if so, whether it is the intention of the
Government to vest these lands which
were not vested in the tenants, in the
vested owners of the lands adjoining
these reclaimed lands.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: This question
speaks for itself. It is one regarding
reclaimed land.
I quite understand
that, as far as vested owners are concerned, these lands were vested in the
owners. There is a map which shows
the boundaries of the farms, but a great
of these farmers do not think of the
many
map. A. good deal of the land to which
I refer has been flooded for a considerable number of years and has now been
reclaimed under the new drainage scheme
adopted by the Government. Where the
vested owner has a map showing the
boundaries of his farm I quite appreciate
that this reclaimed land is his property,
but what we do want to get is this. Who
are the owners of the land that has been
reclaimed? There are no owners at the
present time except the Government. I
suppose the Leader of the House will say
the landlord owns this reclaimed land,
but I want to know who the landlord
is, and where he has been for, Lord knows
the time. This is land that the original
landlord has forgotten all about. The
fact of the matter is that it was no good
to him. I
suppose he will waken up now
that he finds it has been reclaimed at the

now walk into occupation of that land
and reap all the benefits? If that is so
it is not fair, because I hold that he has
been out of the occupation of the land
for such a long time that it should now
go to the Government and should be at
the disposal of the
of the Government to

adjoins. The matter is of considerable

where a drainage scheme has been

Cusher River where a great deal of land
has been reclaimed. The farmers are
anxious to acquire as much of this reclaimed land adjoining their farms as
possible.
One farmer has about eight acres
which adjoins some of this reclaimed
land, and he is anxious to get a few more
acres to make his farm worth while working, but he does not actually know who
the owner of this land is. I would like
this point cleared up, and I hope the
Government will be helpful, because it is
a matter that affects not only the landowner but the ratepayer, while it also
affects the county councils in their efforts
to make these drainage schemes a success.

Belfast County Borough Administration
Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. ROBB: As the House is aware,
from the time of the Housing Inquiry in
1925 an uneasy feeling was generated
throughout the city of Belfast that things
were not all they should be so far as the
Corporation is concerned. Last year the
report on the Whiteabbey Sanatorium was
published and there was a strong demand
from many quarters-it was so strong that
one might almost call it universal-that
the Government should take drastic action
with regard to the Belfast Corporation.
The only course open to the Government
at that time, if they were to take the
action suggested in the Press and by
resolutions passed by various bodies, was
to supersede the Corporation altogether
and appoint in its place Commissioners to
local government affairs in
Belfast. That was a step which the
Government were very reluctant to take.
Accordingly, they suggested that time be
given in order that the Corporation might
make suggestions which would carry a
guarantee of pure administration in the
city.
carry on
I need not touch upon the events that
have happened during the past year, but
it must have been obvious to any person
who watched the course of such events
that there was no solid intention on the
part of the Corporation to bring about
the object which the Government had in
view. A time limit was fixed, and after
it had expired the Government stepped
in with its proposals. Those proposals
are embodied in this Bill. As the House
is aware, they do not involve the supercession of the Corporation at all.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Oh!
Mr. ROBB: The Corporation remains
in being as the director and selector of
general policy in connection with local
government in Belfast. What the Bill
does is this; it provides that the carrying
out of that policy shall be entrusted to
officers appointed by the Ministry of Home
Affairs. It will be the duty of those officers
to carry out the policy upon which the
Corporation decides. That is brought
about in Clause 1 of the Bill,
which provides that-
The Minister of Home Affairs shall
appoint such number of persons as he
11 AUGUST 1942
169 Belfast County Borough Bill-

Second Reading
thinks fit to be city administrators for
the county borough of Belfast.
Clause 2 sets specifically the powers that
are to be committed to the administrators.
They arethe appointment of officers and servants
to the service of the council, the
removal of officers and servants from
that service, the fixing and regulation
of the remuneration to be paid to
officers and servants in, or to be
appointed to, that service
the
superannuation, in accordance with any
enactments for the time being in force,
of persons in the service of the council to
whom those enactments apply.
.
Then there is the purchase of goods, the
purchase and disposal of land, the making
of contracts, the receipt and payment of
moneys, the making, levying, collection
and recovery of the rates, the raising of
loans, issue and management of corporation stock and mortgages, and in regard
to consultations with the War Damage
Commission under Part I of the War
Damage Act.
That is to say, once the Corporation
has decided that a certain policy should
be pursued the carrying out of that policy
is to be committed to the city administrators. The Bill, as framed, is a system
which the Government proposes shall be
put on trial in Belfast for a period of
roughly three and a half years. During
that time this system will be tried, but
it is not to be continued unless Parliament shall so determine. The Government thinks that will be a sufficient length
of time in which to try out this system.
If it is found to be satisfactory it can be
continued; if it is not found satisfactory
it can be modified-it can be totally
abolished or modified in some of its
various branches. Some of the activities
may be extended, some may be cut down,
but this is the system which the Government proposes to meet the public
demand for some system calculated to
avoid the scandals that have arisen in
the past.
I should point out that certain activities are excepted from the operations of
the Bill. First of all, the Committee of
Management of the Mental Hospital,
which has been on a different footing
from all the other commitees of the
Corporation, and has not a statutory basis
in reporting by way of minutes to the
Corporation, and is, to a large extent,
independent. Secondly, the Tuberculosis
Committee which, on the report of the
inquiry, was superseded by Commissioners. In addition, the School Management Committees are excepted generally
from the scope of the Bill in order that
the right of selection of teachers given
under the 1923, 1925, and 1930 Acts may
not be interfered with.
These are the main outlines of the Bill.
The other provisions in it are supplementary, and I do not think they need any
further comment from me. They do not
affect the general principle of the Bill. I
only call attention to the fact that the
last Clause says that the Act is to come
into operation on 1st October, 1942, and
shall continue in force until 31st day of
March, 1946, and for such period as
Parliament may determine. I beg to
move, "That the Bill be now read a
Second time."

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
has been stated that differences occur

far as this Bill is concerned I find myself
in opposition to the Leader of the House.
In introducing the measure he spoke as
if it was of very little significance. He
said it does not even supersede the
Corporation; that it is only to last for a
period of about three and a half years,
and that, in fact, it is a very simple
matter. It is so simple and so unimportant it takes power out of the hands of
the citizens; power which they have had
for a considerable number of years; power
to control the administrative affairs of
the city through their popularly elected
representatives. In my opinion it is
really a very drastic Bill. It puts power
into the hands of civil servants-I presume that is what is intended-and we
know there is an outcry throughout the
whole country in regard to this.
England it has been stated that the
Government of the country and of municipalities is drifting into the hands of
paid servants. The Civil Service is a
very valuable body, but I do not think
they are valuable enough to take the
place of the popularly elected representatives. This is a right which has been
enjoyed by the citizens for a very long
period.
In
I was looking up the Belfast Corporation Charters, and I find that on 27th
April, 1613, Belfast, then a small town,
Belfast County Borough Bill---
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Joseph Cunningham]
was constituted a Corporation by charter
of King James I, to consist of a Sovereign,
or Chief Magistrate, and twelve Burgesses and Commonalty, with the right
of sending two members to Parliament.
This Charter was annulled by King
James II, and a new one issued in 1688,
but the original one was restored by
George II. Since then other privileges
have been conferred on the Corporation
of the City of Belfast. I see that on
the 26th February, 1923, the Duke of
Abercorn informed the Lord Mayor of
Belfast that from that time onward he
would be able to enjoy the right to the
style of "The Right Honourable." These
are privileges which every decent thinking citizen values, and to be robbed of
them is something which is not easy to
swallow.
There may be, as the Leader of the
House has stated, an outcry for something to be done in reference to the Corporation. That is quite an easy matter.
At the present time we hear criticism
even about the Government. You know
perfectly well that there are people who
say there are men in the Corporation who
could be done without. Others think
there are people in the House of
Commons who could be done without,
but democratic government is "government of the people, by the people, for
the people." These people are elected,
and I suppose they represent the views of
those who returned them. That being so,
I do not think there should be any surprise at the fact that things may not go
on in an ideal way. I do not think you
will find anything strictly correct in this
world. It appears to me that we will
have to wait for perfection until we go
to some better conducted place.
I am surprised that the Home Office
should think this a suitable time to introduce such a drastic and important Bill,
a period when the country is in the
throes of the greatest struggle in her
history. We read the newspapers every
morning to learn what is happening, as
a good many of us have friends serving
in the war zone. We are thinking more
about these matters at the present time
than the affairs of the city of Belfast. Is
this a right time in which to create controversy amongst the citizens? When the
war started the late Lord Craigavon said
we should bend all our energies in the
prosecution of the war, and that no controversial questions should be introduced
during the struggle. Is it not a pity the
Government did not respect the promise
of such an able leader as the late Lord
Craigavon? Even though he is gone I
think it would have been well if they
had done so.
This is no party question. I find that
in Belfast all sections of the populace are
opposed to this. Every man, whether he
is a Nationalist, a Socialist, a Unionist,
whatever he may be, certainly must have
pride in his city. It is a fairly progressive city. It has a very good reputation throughout the whole world.
am told by those who know something of
finance I myself am not very well
acquainted with it-that money can be
borrowed for Belfast as cheap as for any
other city. Therefore, the reputation of
Belfast must stand high. In spite of the
fact that the Corporation are such a bad
crowd they have been able to have some
I do not know
very fine undertakings.
where you will find anything better than
the electricity and gas undertakings
which they own, and then the transport
here can hardly be surpassed in any other
city. That is the result of the hardheaded businessman giving his time and
attention to the benefit of the citizens of
Belfast.
I would like to know if the Government think that the members of the
Corporation are there with some ulterior
motive or for some financial benefit? I
do not think they are. I believe a man
feels proud that he is able to serve his
fellows and do something for the benefit
of his city. As far as I am concerned,
since 1921, when I was asked to occupy
a certain position in a trade union, I do
not think my spare time has been lost.
It has been given in the interest of my
fellows. For many years when I left my
work at half-past five or six o'clock I
hurried home, and when I had washed
and changed I went to some meeting
which I attended without remuneration.
I went there proud that I was able to
serve my fellows. We think of the old
saying about trying to make the world a
little better than we found it. That
should be the ambition and desire of all
those who give their services voluntarily.
There is another thing I would like to
point out. The complaint is very often
heard that the citizens are apathetic. If
173 Beltast County Borough Bill-

11 AUGUST 1942
Second Reading
you take power out of the hands of the
citizens and tell them they are going to be
ruled and controlled in the future by two
administrators, or dictators, or commissioners, whatever you may call them, they
will see that the Corporation are only pretending to administer the affairs of the
city. That is stated very clearly in the
Bill where it says-
The city administrators shall have
and exercise, to the exclusion of the
council, the functions of the council
with respect to the following matters,
that is to say, the appointment of
officers and servants to the service of the
council-
They have taken particular care that the
members of the Corporation will not have
the opportunity of recommending a man
for even a binman's job. What could
be more humiliating to a respectable body
of men? I do not want you to think they
are a lot of robbers, such as they were represented to be in the Press, because only
a short time ago there was a case in the
law courts. I think the Press went to
extremes in trying to blacken the
Corporation.
That is the position of the citizens.
They have a great deal more confidence in
their popularly elected representatives
than in civil servants. Their elected representatives are men whom they can ask
to do them a favour. It may not be
proper to say that, but I live in a district
populated by working-class people, and it
is surprising what one is asked to do. One
is asked to recommend someone for a
house. One woman who has been courting a soldier thinks he is a married man,
and she recently came to ask me to find out
whether he is married or single. I know
that is a peculiar case. People come to
the elected representatives, who assist
them in many directions. They make out
appeals and sign forms for them. It is
quite a common thing for the people to
ask things like that, and, as I say, they
have more faith in the popularly elected
representatives than in civil servants.
Some years ago a debate took place in the
Imperial House of Commons, in which
someone described a civil servant as a soulless machine, a man who stuck strictly to
the letter of the law and did not deviate
in the least for either sympathy or
sentiment.
Again, the people are asked to pay
rates. Surely they would be more content to pay if they knew the amount had
been apportioned by public representatives. In future this is to be done by
civil servants. They are to settle what
rates are to be paid, and the people must
pay them. Is it not likely that if you
train the people to think you can run the
Belfast Corporation with civil servants,
the citizens of Belfast and of the whole
Province might ask, why not run the
Government of Northern Ireland by civil
servants? They may ask: if it is so easy
and convenient to run the Belfast Corporation, which is a large undertaking,
why is all this money being spent on Parliament when it can be run by civil servants? I am sure that the civil servants
who recommended that the Corporation be
run this way would have no hesitation in
suggesting that the Government as well
could be run by them.
I do not think that voluntary service
should be despised. It should be encouraged rather than despised. Every
encouragement should be given to the
public-spirited men in Belfast, or in any
other part of Northern Ireland, to do all
they possibly can for the welfare of the
people. You have business men, medical
men, and some working people giving their
services voluntarily. These voluntary
services must have been of great benefit
to the whole of the British Isles in the
past, and I am sure will be in the future,
but I expect they are more appreciated in
other places than they seem to be in
Belfast.
Let us come to the point. What is the
real cause for this legislation? The right
hon. Gentleman the Leader of the House
told us it goes as far back as the housing
question. We remember that question,
but we also remember that money was
stolen by one of the rate collectors.
Many of the things that have occurred
in the Belfast Corporation have occurred
in other places, for instance, in Glasgow,
Liverpool and Leeds. Some of the occurrences in those cities were probably worse
than those in Belfast, because there were
prosecutions, but the British Government
did not think of superseding the councils.
I have heard of no case of the British
Government doing that, but I have heard
of its being done in Eire. It was done
in Dublin and Cork. I do not know
whether our Home Secretary is following
in the footsteps of the President of
Eire

Mr. McALLISTER: I do not interest
myself in this matter from any local or
isolated point of view. I am not here to
attack or to defend the Belfast Corporation, but I am here to oppose this Bill
with all the zeal and vehemence in my
power, because it trespasses on the most
precious right of the citizens the right
of democracy and freedom.
When we
think of the sufferings and efforts and
labours of our forbears to secure emancipation and the rights of franchise which
is the only way to establish democracy
and freedom we should, I think, be true
to those forbears and not allow this Bill
to pass without offering it the most
vehement opposition.
It is to attack the principle involved in
this Bill I rise to speak.
We have no sympathy with maladministration or civic
injustice. We are here to help the
Government by every means in our power
to punish any man guilty of conduct of
that type, but I do think that if the
Government really wanted to clean out
any maladministration in the Corporation,
this was not the method by which to do it.
This was a mere excuse to suppress and
abolish a local body. Why are we asked
to pass this very drastic Bill, which involves a major issue? On a general statement by the Leader of the House that
there has been a strong opinion passed
against the Corporation, without giving us
any information as to who offered that
strong opinion or what it was, we are
asked to destroy democracy and freedom
-that is what it means. Without a single
concrete fact, or a scintilla of evidence
179 Belfast County Borough Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. McAllister]
being put before us, we are asked to adopt
that drastic and humiliating method.
If the Government had been honest they
would have instituted a sworn inquiry
presided over by a judge. There are precedents for doing that. The public would
then have known who were the malefactors
in the Corporation who were guilty of
criminal practices, whether the Corporation were right or wrong, and we would
have supported the Government in taking
means to deal with any question of maladministration. That would have been
the proper course to adopt, and if any
injustice was found to be the custom, or
even if there was an isolated case, then the
step to be taken was to prosecute the
people guilty. Had there been such an
inquiry, and the public had the opportunity of knowing the facts with the full
evidence before them, no man guilty of
malpractice would have been allowed to
face the public again at an election. I
would go further and say it would be the
unanimous wish of this House to pass
legislation suspending the activities of any
man found guilty at that public inquiry of
any malpractice or maladministration.

these benches, at all events, cannot accept

they are bringing in this Bill for the purpose of clean administration.

comparing the

I agree with Senator Cunningham that

only method upon which democracy can
be built and can endure. One would be
inclined to speak with passion in a case
like this. In a time like this, in a world
war with poor nations like Poland and
Belgium struggling for democracy we
have a Bill introduced here which is a
vandalism of every ideal and liberty for
which they have fought and endured
during the last three years. If countries
like those knew of the efforts that have
been made in this Parliament for
democracy and freedom what would they
think of the value of the sympathy and
vocal support extended?
But there is more involved in this Bill,
in my opinion, than meets the eye. I
suggest to the House that the Bill is just
another stepping stone to the setting up
of a dictatorship to suppress and destroy
elective democracy. As I said at the first
available moment the Nationalists were

disfranchised; democracy was torn to
tatters as far as they were concerned.
The Belfast Board of Guardians have
been abolished and now. the Belfast
Corporation is abolished de facto beyond
all question. I have no doubt in my
mind that if this goes on without effective
protest of the people or fight by the
people the next step will be the abolition
of rural and urban councils throughout Northern Ireland. When I read
some of the legislation in this House,
really in a progressive age it does make
one feel bitter. I suggest that in the
method of administration in the legislation we are accustomed to in this House
this Government is standing with its legs
straddled, one foot fixed on the mediæval
ages and the other glued to the Battle
of the Boyne, 1690.
Mr. LESLIE: I cannot but feel that
the less any hon. Member says on the
subject of the Bill now before the House
the better, but at the same time I think
it is necessary that some person, at all
events, should point out that the Bill
before us does present an almost unprecedented example of the necessity for
clear thinking on the part of those of us
who have to help in legislation. Therefore, I do not altogether care to give a
silent vote on the occasion. The point
to which, I think, clear thinking
should be specially directed is this. The
hon. Member who proposed the rejection
of the Bill, and the hon. Senator who
supported him, both seemed to suggest
that the purport of the Bill is to take
the government of the city of Belfast out
of the hands of the Corporation. That
is where, I think, the necessity for clear
thinking comes in. It is not the government that is interfered with, but the
administration, and those two things are
not the same.
It appears to have been accepted by
those who sit in another place that all
has not been well of late in the interior
affairs of the Corporation. With those
matters I do not think this House is
called upon to deal. It is really not for
us to inquire into the amount of black
cloth used at Whiteabbey or where it
was obtained. These matters have been
considered elsewhere. I think the general
opinion is that the question of the black
cloth is simply an item in the schedule.
It will serve no useful purpose for us to
recapitulate anything of that sort. The
fact remains that things were not
regarded as altogether satisfactory. The
Corporation was given the opportunity of
making internal reform. The correspondence between the Ministry and the
Corporation was published in the Press.
I do not know that it ever was laid on
the Table of this House or in another
place. At all events it was available.
Frequent opportunities were offered for
internal reform, and these opportunities
were not availed of. Now, as has been
said before, these matters are not in the
interests of the ratepayers of Belfast. It
would be easy enough to leave the ratepayers to their fate, but it would not be
statesmanship.
.
Therefore it is, as I see it, necessary
for the Government to come to this House
for powers and the powers they ask for
are, indeed, I think, much more limited
in this respect than in some other Bills
that have come before us. It is not
proposed to subject the Corporation to
what I might describe as a major operation. Some of their limbs are to be put
in plaster of Paris. That is not the same
thing as cutting them off altogether. The
power of administration in some matters
is to be taken from the Corporation. In
such matters as the promotion of and
opposition to Bills before Parliament the
power is not taken away. They can do
that. They can go on signing the papers
my hon. Friend referred to. There is
nothing to stop them doing that. But
the new administrators will be as I see
it not masters of the Corporation or of
be their
the ratepayers. They will
servants-
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: Not
at all.
Mr. LESLIE- -using the term in the
highest sense of the word. It is sometimes said that the King himself is the
servant of the people. In that sense the
administrators will be the servants of the
Corporation and the ratepayers.
The
Bill as I see it is curative as regards the
present. That is the intention. It is
intended to be prophylactic as regards
the future. It is not intended to be
either penal or punitive as regards the
past. I propose to vote for the Bill.

Electricity (Emergency Supplies) Bill.
Order of the Day for the House to be
put into Committee read.
Resolved, "That this House do now
