Bill." (Mr. Robb.)
In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair.]
CLAUSE 1 (Powers of the Ministry of
Commerce for ensuring essential supplies
of electricty).
(1) For the purpose of enabling the
Ministry of Commerce (in this Act referred to as "the Ministry") to ensure
the provision and maintenance of essential supplies of electricity, the following
provisions of this section shall have
effect.
(2) The Ministry shall have power-
(a) to construct generating stations and main transmission lines, and
(b) to acquire any generating
station or main transmission line
belonging to any authorised undertakers.
*
*
*
*
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
1, sub-clause (1), to leave out the word
"and" in line 11 (stations and main), and
insert the words, "main transmission lines
and transmission lines ancillary to."
This, as of course the Committee will
observe, is really a drafting amendment.
It seemed to me that the Ministry will,
under Clause 2, as long as they become
authorised undertakers, have all the necessary powers, and therefore I am not suggesting the insertion of the words on the
Paper with the intention of giving them
new power. Had that been so, I think
you, Mr. Chairman, would have felt constrained to pounce upon me because of the
limitations imposed by the Money Resolution. I suggest the addition of the words
only because, as we are inserting specially
the generating station and the main transmission line, we ought to specify the
ancillary transmission line also. I think I
may add that if the expression "transmission line" without that unfortunate
word "main" had not occurred in Clause
5 I should not have thought it necessary
to have troubled the Committee. As it is,
I think these words should go in, but it is
not suggested that their insertion confers
any additional power.
Mr. ROBB: The Government cannot
accept this Amendment. Perhaps if I
were to explain now the exact position in
regard to transmission lines it might clear
up some difficulties which Members may
have found as represented by later
amendments. The position is this. Where
the Ministry acts solely by way of erecting
a generating station or a transmission line
it is for the purpose of supplying electricity to various undertakings which have
need of it. In that case the undertakings
provide all the equipment necessary for the
receipt of the supply from the main transmission lines. Accordingly, the Ministry
does not require to have any power to
provide such equipment by way of nonmain transmission lines.
On the other hand, where the Ministry.
has taken over an undertaking, then by
Clause 2 (1) of the Bill the Ministry becomes the authorised undertaker and has
all the powers of erection of transmission
lines which this position carries with it.
There is one curious exception, to which
I might refer now in order to make the
picture complete. This exception is dealt
with in Clause 5 of the Bill. The Corporation of Belfast has a ring main, as it
is called, surrounding the city. This
transmission line, however, has not in fact
been declared a main transmission line for
the purposes of the Electricity Acts,
although it is physically possible for the
Ministry's main lines to be joined up with
this ring main. The Belfast Corporation
were afraid that if the provision in Clause
5 were not inserted it would be impossible
for the Corporation to charge for the use
of the ring main up to the point where it
is tapped into by the Ministry's main
transmission line, in the event of the Corporation supplying electricity to the
Ministry. In the reverse direction, of
course, the Ministry would supply electricity at a stated price at the junction
between the Ministry's main transmission
line and the ring main, and the cost
between the ring main and Belfast Power
Electricity Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
Station would be borne as part of the cost
of electricity in Belfast.
I hope that this explanation will clear
up the matter, particularly as there are
various amendments which concern it.
The explanation is that it is not necessary
that this power should be inserted.
Mr. LESLIE: Am I to take it that the
right hon. Gentleman accepts the position,
but does not want any extra power.
I am
not clear that he has got it already in the
Bill. At all events, he does not want it.
Mr. ROBB: I do not want it.
Mr. LESLIE: In that case, I think I
might ask leave to withdraw the Amendment.
Major DOBBS: I understand that the
object of this Bill is primarily to ensure
that if a power station gets destroyed, the
district it serves will not be without electricity. Supposing a power station from
which Belfast draws its electricity is partially destroyed, will it be possible to link
up some other power station with the main
transmission line of the Belfast Corporation?
Mr. ROBB: Yes.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Clause ordered to stand part of the

a
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
2, sub-clause (2), after the word "made"
(made by the Governor) to insert the
words, "during the period of the present
emergency." This amendment is of
somewhat different class to the last one.
The whole point of this Bill, as I understand it, is to make provision for circumstances arising out of the present
emergency either to take prompt measures to counteract enemy action, or take
measures, presumably also without delay,
for the supply of electricity in furtherance
of the war effort. In either case, the
utmost possible expedition is obviously
desirable, but when the Bill first appeared
in another place it was left very doubtful
as to what was behind the Bill; whether it
was intended to endure for all time-so
far as a Bill can endure for all time-or
whether it was intended to be purely a
war-time measure.
Since then it has been established that
it is a war-time Bill. I think that is
universally accepted not only by the
Houses of Parliament but also by the
Government itself. That being so, it does
not seem to me that the Government can
possibly ask this House to consent to
giving the powers asked for in Clause 2,
except as a war-time measure. That is to
say, that if this Order in Council is to be
made making the exceptions and modifications adumbrated, that can only be done
as a war-time measure. When the period
of the present emergency has expired, that
power must expire also. If more is required afterwards, then the Government
must come to Parliament again with concrete proposals.
Let us see for a moment what these
powers asked for are. The enactments
which, in their application to the Ministry, are to be subject to such exceptions.
and modifications as may be made by
Order in Council, are those mentioned in
sub-clause (1) of this Clause. Those enactments are the whole electricity code as
we know it from 1882 to 1936, the provisions of the Schedule of the Electric
Lighting (Clauses) Act, 1899, which is not
incorporated in that code, and the Act
which this Bill will become. We have also
brought in under the provisions of the
Schedule to the Electric Lighting
(Clauses) Act, 1899, every local Bill dealing with electricity which has become an
Act. Those we cannot go into now, but
the main and principal point is that the
13 AUGUST 1942
Electricity Bill—
Bill as it stands asks for power to make
exceptions and modifications as regards
the Ministry in the Bill which we are now
engaged in considering.
I could not possibly stand for that. It
is a great concession to allow it at all,
but to allow it for otherwise than the
duration of the present emergency would
be entirely wrong. Parliament is here,
and we cannot allow a Bill to pass through
this House allowing the amendment of
other Bills which have been passed, except
in the most exceptional circumstances and
for a time of very limited duration.
most we can do is to pass that sub-clause
for the period of the present emergency,
but not for longer. It is to that end I
have suggested we should insert these
words "during the period of the present
emergency."
The
Mr. ROBB: I appreciate the point the
right hon. Gentleman has tried to make,
but the Government cannot accept the
Amendment for this reason. While the
actions which may be initiated by the
Ministry in order to meet conditions
arising in the present emergency should
be limited to the emegency, yet there are
a number of matters which must continue
after the date of the emergency. To give
one instance, let us say that an undertaking has been acquired by the Ministry.
As soon as the emergency ceases it would
be quite impossible to require the Ministry
to cease production of electricity in that
undertaking.
Since the Ministry would have become
an authorised undertaker under the Electricity Acts, and the modifications of these
Acts are, for the purposes of this Act, to
enable the Ministry to proceed without
going through the formality of asking for
its own consent when it proposes to do certain things, it is essential that the power
to adapt these Acts in this way should
have reference to any period during which
the Ministry remains an authorised undertaker. And as I have explained, this
state may exist some time after the emergency has ceased.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, to leave
out the second paragraph in sub-clause (2)
and insert the following new sub-clause:
(3) Every Order in Council made
under this section shall, so soon as may
be after it is made, be laid before each
House of Parliament for the statutory
period, and if either House of Parliament before the expiration of that
period resolves that the Order shall be
annulled, the Order shall, after the date
of the resolution, cease to have effect
except as regards things previously
done thereunder and without prejudice
to the making of a new Order.
In this case I am leading off on another
tack altogether. The point in the suggested alteration is this. The whole object
of this procedure of Order in Council is to
secure expedition, and in my opinion the
method proposed to attain it is in the
second part of sub-clause (2). It does not
secure expedition. I suggest that to get
an Order in Council through, according
to the words before us, it would take about
six weeks. Notice of the proposal has got
to be made and copies thereof have to be
left somewhere. We are not told where
as a detail. We are told how long the
draft is to remain open for inspection,
and that it is going to be published in the
Belfast Gazette. That is the notification
the general public are to have, but the
general public does not read the Belfast
Gazette and would not know about it.
These words appeared in the Electricity
Act, 1931. In that Act it provided for
publication not only in the Belfast Gazette
but by such other means as the Ministry
considered best adapted that the public
should know about it.
After the draft Order in Council is
made and left open to inspection, it has
to be laid before each House of Parliament, but before you can lay your draft
Order in Council before Parliament, it is
necessary for Parliament to be sitting.
If the Order is laid before Parliament
when it is sitting Parliament can object.
if it likes by an address, which should be
related to the subject of the Order,
although the wording in the sub-clause
does not say so. The sub-clause I propose
is taken from our own Chartered and
Other Bodies (Temporary Provisions)
Act, 1940, and provides for the making of
the Order in Council and laying it before
Parliament, and if either House objects
by resolution to the Order, then it shall
be annulled, but anything done under the
Order before that annulment will remain
and there will be no prejudice to the
making of a new Order. I suggest that
unless we can ensure speed there is no use
in passing emergency Bills.
Mr. ROBB: Of course, as the right
hon. Senator says, what he now suggests
would make it easier for the Ministry, but
the Ministry thought it right that before
any Order was in fact made, notice should
be given which might be notice to any
persons concerned, so that they could consider it before the Order was made. With
regard to the question of delay, the Ministry does not anticipate that the provisions
they have inserted in the Bill will cause
any real delay in the carrying out of their
intentions. The form of the sub-clause
is such as to give notice to the people
really affected before the Order is made,
and not lead them to know what has
happened after it has happened.
Ministry do not anticipate that there
will be any difficulty proceeding in the
I cannot
way suggested in the Bill.
accept the Amendment.
The
Mr. LESLIE: The point is that the
Ministry has got the idea that no enemy
action may take place. The Ministry
does provide for giving due notice of the
proceedings, but it cannot provide against
a general crashing and smashing of our
electrical system, and then will come the
repairing and replacing of the lines. I
do not think that I should withdraw this
Amendment. It is our duty to press upon
the Ministry of Commerce the urgent
necessity of getting on with the war. We
must not allow our war effort or our war
production to be delayed by any form of
red tape. If Parliament is not sitting it
will be six weeks before an Order could
be made. I am not in the least complaining of the steps taken by the Ministry. What I do urge is that it is our
duty to see that the utmost possible despatch is used, and the adoption of the
words in the Bill is not the most rapid
method.
Question put, "That these lines be left
out."

the definition of a main transmission

shall be construed as if the expression
'overhead' did not therein occur.
I put this as a separate sub-clause, because it was the most convenient way of
doing it. It seems to me the expression
"overhead" in the definition contained
in the Electricity (Supply) Act of main
transmission lines gets rather in our way,
because, since 1919 at all events, it has
become possible to take an electric line
underground. It may be in a tube or
not. I am afraid I ought to read to the
Committee the definition of "main transmission line" as it appears in Sec. 36
of the Electricity (Supply) Act, 1919,
because I cannot help thinking that
possibly some Members of the Committee
may not have referred to it. It states:
The expression "main transmission
lines" means all extra high-pressure
cables and overhead lines (not being
an
essential part of authorised
undertaker's distribution system or the

a railway

and switch-gear
necessary to, and used for, the control
of such cables and overhead lines, and
the buildings or such part thereof, as
may be required to accommodate such
transformers and switch-gear.

We are told nowhere what a cable is.

Act to prevent a difficulty arising. On

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in the
first paragraph of the First Schedule to
leave out the words "special order" and
insert the words "order made by the
Ministry."
This is another case of some difficulty.
As I think I said on the second Reading,
in normal circumstances there would be
no objection whatever to the words
"special order" or to proceeding in the
manner suggested, but I may remind the
Committee that we are at war, and if the
thing has got to be done at all it has got
to be done quickly. This is not a thing
of the first importance, anyhow, but the
point of speed is of importance. How
long does the right hon. Gentleman suggest it would take him to get his special
order through? I suggest three months.
I am going to tell the Committee what a
special order means. If the Committee
rejects this Amendment, I shall have an
amendment on Report to define a special
order in the same manner as a generating
station has been defined. The definition
states:
"Generating station" has the meaning assigned to that expression by
section thirty-six of the Electricity
(Supply) Act, 1919.
This definition would be much the same.
The proceedings as to a special order
cover no less than two pages of our own
Electricity (Supply) Act of 1931, and I
shall tell the Committee what is going to
happen. First of all, a special order
made by the Ministry of Commerce will
not have effect unless and until confirmed
by that Ministry. Before the Ministry
of Commerce confirms any such special
order, that Ministry shall publish in such
manner as it may think best adapted for
informing persons affected notice of
the proposal to confirm the order and of
the place where copies of the order may
be obtained, and of the time within which
an objection made with respect to the
order, by or on behalf of persons affected,
must be sent to that Ministry.
Every objection must be in writing and
state-(a) the order or portions of order
objected to, (b) the specific grounds of
objection, and (c) the omissions, additions, and modifications asked for. The
Ministry of Commerce shall consider any
objection made by or on behalf of any
persons appearing to that Ministry to
be affected which is sent to the Ministry
within the required time, and the Ministry may, if it thinks fit, amend the order
before confirming it. Where the Ministry
does not amend or withdraw any order to
which objection has been made, it shall
direct an inquiry to be held, and after
Electricity Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Lesliel
considering the report of the person who
held the inquiry, confirm the order either
without modification or subject to such
modification as the Ministry of Commerce thinks fit, or may refuse to confirm
the order. Then a special order that
may be made by the Ministry of Commerce shall be laid before each House of
Parliament, and shall not come into
operation until it has been approved,
either with or without modification, by a
resolution passed by such House, and
when so approved shall have effect as if
enacted in this Act, and shall not be
questioned in any legal proceedings.
To get that through would take three
months. I do not know if the right hon.
Gentleman could get it through in a
shorter time. When the country is at
war and passing through this emergency,
it is perfectly ridiculous, it is an insult
to the intelligence of the public, to ask
them to subscribe to a thing of this sort.
We must put into the Ministry of Commerce a little bit of vim. The public
have been complaining for months that
the Ministry of Commerce are very slow
in getting on with the war, that they do
do not do all that they might do. Surely
this is a case in which they must move
with celerity if they move at all. I must
confess this is doing a little violence to
my constitutional principles, and it is
only because of the war emergency I suggest it.
Mr. ROBB: The right hon. Gentleman has not appreciated the situation at
all. The Ministry can take over at once
a station. This paragraph only deals with
the fixing of the price. In fact, this special
order only deals with the fixing of the
rate of depreciation which the arbitrator
is to allow when he comes to sit
to find out the price that is to be
paid the undertakers whose undertaking
is taken over. There is no desperate
hurry about that. The Ministry can get
on with the taking over of the station
and the working of the station. This
refers only to the arbitration that is to
fix the price to be paid; in fact, the
special order only deals with the rate of
depreciation the arbitrator is to allow.
The time would not be so long as the
right hon. Gentleman has suggested, because the special order only deals with
the rate of depreciation and the intermediate state, the confirmation by the
Ministry, will not apply, so that the length
of time even at the worst would not be as
much as he suggests. As I say, this does
not interfere with the getting on of the
winning of the war, it is only fixing the
payment to some undertaker. It is an
endeavour to preserve some control to
Parliament over these orders instead of
allowing the Ministry a free hand. Under
the circumstances I cannot accept the
amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: As the right hon.

Mr. ROBB: It will not cause any delay in getting on with the war.

"com-
This is another small point. The expression here is that "the matter in
question shall be referred to the arbitration of such engineer or other fit person.'
You cannot use the word "engineer," I
think, without some further definition.
"Engineer" is anything but descriptive.
It means, I understand, a civil engineer
to the exclusion of those who serve His
Majesty in the Royal Engineers. The
only case where I can find the expression
"engineer" used in any of these Acts is,
one where his qualifications are defined
as "an engineer having experience of
such matters." I think, therefore, you
should get rid of that expression "such
engineer" and insert "such competent
and impartial" person. Those are the
words used in the Schedule to the Act of
1939. I do not think the best expression
is "engineer" without any qualification.
""

the sort of person who may be appointed.

Belfast County Borough Administration
Bill.
Order of the Day for the House to be
put into Committee read.
Resolved, "That this House do resolve
itself into Committee on the said Bill.”—-
(Mr. Robb.)
In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair.]
CLAUSE 1 (City administrators).
(1) The Minister of Home Affairs
shall appoint such number of persons
as he thinks fit to be city administrators
for the county borough of Belfast.
(2) The city administrators shall have
and exercise, to the exclusion of the
council, the functions of the council
with respect to the following matters,
that is to say:-
(a) the appointment of officers and
servants to the service of the council,
the removal of officers and servants
from that service, the fixing and
regulation of the remuneration to be
paid to officers and servants in, or to
be appointed to, that service, and the
distribution of the work of the council among them;
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I beg
to move, in sub-clause (2), paragraph (a),
to leave out the words "and servants"
where they first occur. As it is, the
administrators will have to exercise the
appointment of officers and servants of
the council. I understand that long,
long ago the employer and employee were
described as master and servant, but the
people are becoming a little more intelligent. They object to that. There is no
man good enough to be another man's
master. We have dropped the word
master, but retained the word servant,
which I take to be employee. There are
850 salaried officials in the Belfast Corporation, and approximately 7,500
weekly wage earners, and to suggest that
two administrators are going to look after
7,500 weekly wage earners is ridiculous.
All that these people can do is to delegate
the authority to someone else.
I suggest that the words "and servants" be left out so that the City
Council that will be in existence, although
robbed of a great many of its powers,
would be able to decide how these servants
shall be employed. There has been considerable trouble in the past about the
employment of employees in the City
Council, and there may be trouble in the
future, because you have some very strong
employees' organisations. Only those
people acquainted with the reasonable or
unreasonable demands would be best fitted
to deal with questions of this kind. I
move that the words "and servants" be
deleted, so as to leave to the administrators the power to appoint the officers
and also the power of removal of officers
and servants.
I think the Government would be well
advised to remove the words "and
servants," which I take to be the 7,500
weekly wage-earners and leave it to the
City Council to decide how these people
shall be employed. It would be more
satisfactory to leave the Corporation
some little power. I have often heard
it said, when men were looking to be
elected to the City Council from the Falls
Road, that the people used to sneer at
them and say, "What are you talking
about? They are unable to recommend
the employment of a binman." It looks
if the Ministry of Home Affairs
wanted to humiliate the City Council as
much as possible by removing the employment of binmen from it. I accordingly move that these words be deleted.
as
Question proposed, "That these words
be left out."
Mr. McALLISTER: I enter a very
vigorous protest against taking up the

Mr. ROBB: The Government does not

Senator who moved it said, trouble arose

with the employment of servants and

This is one of the difficulties that created

Mr. McLAUGHLIN: On the Falls
Road.

House.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I am against this
Amendment. I have had fifteen years'
experience as a member of Belfast Corporation, and during that period the
system of employment of servants was
changed. I took a very firm stand
against the change from the employment
exchange to the council, and a great
many members of the council took a similar view. Many men have approached
me to get the nomination cards which
were issued to members of the council, but
I had no means of verifying the information they gave as to the length of time
they had been out of employment and the
size of their families. There is a department of the Ministry of Labour which
has full records of the length of time
these men have been out of employment.
I felt great dissatisfaction in recommending some of the men who applied to me
for nomination because I could not verify
their statements. I can safely say that
70 or 80 per cent. of the men employed
were unworthy of being employed, and
that others who had been unemployed for
much longer periods should have got the
vacancies. I will certainly vote against
this Amendment.
The CHAIRMAN: Does the hon.
Member wish to press his amendment?
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: Yes.

Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 18th August, 1942.
The Senate met at a Quarter before
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Questions to Minister.
ARMAGH TOWN RATE.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :--
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state what was the
town rate for the urban district of
Armagh for the years ended 31st
March, 1932, 1933, 1934, 1936, 1937,
1942 and 1943; what was the poor law
valuation of the urban district of
Armagh for the same years, and the
amount of principal and interest paid
on outstanding loans due by the
Armagh Urban Council for the same
period.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: In asking this
question, I should like to say that the
City Council of Armagh, the primatial
city of Ireland, was, owing to jerrymandering by the Government, the only
Nationalist city council left in the Six
Counties. It is not surprising to find it
was dissolved in March, 1934. A sworn
inquiry was then demanded, but for
reasons best known to themselves the
Ministry of Home Affairs, fearing publicity and knowing they would come out
second best, as well as fearing disclosures
as to their actions, refused any such
inquiry. The citizens of Armagh, the
majority of whom are Nationalist, have
been deprived of the franchise in this
land of so-called freedom for the past
8 years. Now they have been further
victimised by false and, if I may say so,
slanderous statements. I do not wish it
to be understood that the Minister of
Home Affairs made the statement, which
I am going to read, in another place on
his own initiative; he was using the
information supplied by his department.
This information, I again emphatically
say, was not alone misleading but it
was entirely untrue, as the following
facts will justify. First, here is the
statement made in another place by the
Home Secretary during the debate on the
Belfast Corporation Bill.
On the question of maladministration generally I might point this out,
that when Mr. Hanna was appointed
commissioner in Armagh, the first rate
he had to strike was 11s in the £, next
year he was able to reduce this town
rate to 9s in the £, and in his last year
he had reduced this town rate to 5s 10d
in the £. These figures do not show
maladministration.
That statement of figures was untrue.
During the whole of Mr. Hanna's commissionership in Armagh he never struck
a town rate of 11s in the £. Here are
the correct figures of the town rate for
the urban district of Armagh. by the commissioner, Mr. Hanna, during his term of
office. In the commissioner's first year,
ending 31st March, 1934, 9s 10d, plus a
special rate of d in the £, a total of
9s 101d, and not 11s in £ as stated by
the Home Secretary.
For the year ending 31st March, 1937,
when the Revaluation Act came into
force, the poor law valuation of Armagh
urban district was increased from £22,542
to £28,994, a fact the Home Secretary
carefully concealed. The rate struck that
year was 7s in the £, but owing to increased valuation it meant no relief to
the ratepayers. For the year ending 31st
March, 1939, when the Mental Hospital
paid Armagh Council £483 for water
supplied, compared with £8 3s 6d paid
for the year 1937, it will be seen the
commissioner got a sum equal to 4d in
the £ from the Mental Hospital. The
town rate for that year was 6s 4d in the
£.
Here I might point out that the last
urban council could not see their way to
supply water to the Mental Hospital Committee, who had their own water supply,
fearing a shortage of water in Armagh,
but this fact did not weigh with the
commissioner.
Now, for the year ending 31st March,
1942, when the poor law valuation was
again increased by £1,000 to £29,984,
which meant a sum equal to 9d in the £,
the rate struck was 5s 10d, plus a special
rate of 7d, making a total town rate of
6s 5d in the £.
Mark you,
the commissioner who succeeded him was obliged
to strike a rate for the present year, on

with the rate of 9s in the £ struck by
the last council on a valuation of
£22,542, which it then was, the present
rate on increased valuation of £29,988
should be ôs 6d or thereabouts in the £.
It will be seen clearly that the town rate,
with increased valuation, has not been

5s or 6s in the £ to bring the streets and
footpaths, which are full of potholes, to
the condition they were in when the commissioners took up office. There is an
open drain in and around the city which
is a disgrace to any sanitary authority.

ACQUISITION OF METAL
RAILINGS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether any authentic information can
be given as regards a scheme, recently
mentioned in the newspapers, for the
compulsory acquisition of metal rail-
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
ings for munitions purposes; (1)
whether arrangements will be made for
appeal in the case of gates in rural districts on the ground of utility; (2)
what arrangements will be made for
compensation; (3) whether it will be
ensured that the value of anything
given free of cost will accrue to the
State and not to any intermediary;
and (4) whether effective arrangements
for collection and shipment can
confidently anticipated.
be
Mr. LESLIE: I have designedly drawn
this question at some little length in order
to bring out the various points on which
questions arise or have been asked. The
notice I saw in the papers was one of
those rather vague notices that do
appear occasionally, but the general
effect was that all railings in Northern
Ireland were to be commandeered, and
those railings included gates. A form of
appeal would be provided in the case of
any railings alleged to be of historical or
artistic interest, and it was confidently
anticipated that railings would be given
for nothing. Accordingly, I want to
know whether we can have authentic information as to what really has happened.
My first question is whether arrangements will be made for appeal in the case
of gates in rural districts on the ground
of utility. There are a great many farm
gates in the rural districts. If whoever
is doing the requisitioning is so foolish as
to try to requisition farm gates, will the
owner of the farm gates have the opportunity of appealing to some more reasonable party? It must be remembered that
in the rural districts there are very few
railings in the ordinary sense. There are
a great many farm gates-iron gates--
and the iron gate has come into its own
pretty well of late years. During the
present emergency the iron gate has certain advantages over the wooden gate.
Will there be an appeal in the case of the
rural districts?
Secondly, what arrangements will be
made for compensating the owners of
these things which will not be given free?
It may be that the owner of such railings
may want to take the value in order to
pay his war damage contributions. A
man may be prepared to give up his
railings if they are allocated to the service
of the State, but he may not be willing
to do so in order that the iron founder
should take that metal and mould it down
for tanks, and from the sale of the tanks
get credit for the value of the material
which he received free. Something of
that sort is believed to have happened in
another connection.
As regards the iron available a good
many of us have thought that one of the
most prolific sources of supply is likely to
be those railings around graves in churchyards, a matter which was referred
to in debates some time during last
year. These stand on a different footing.
perhaps, from the ordinary railings. It
may well be that a man may be willing
to give a railing from the tomb of his
ancestor free gratis to help the country
in its struggle, while not prepared to give
up railings protecting his own garden.
Finally, I want to know whether effective arrangements for collection and
shipment can be confidently anticipated?
We all know that there have been what
are called drives in connection with scrap
metal. People have given scrap metal
which has been removed from the establishment and dumped somewhere else, and
it is there now. The arrangements for
removing scrap, which was sold or bestowed, do not seem to have worked very
rapidly. Everybody wants to know
whether that is going to happen again.
In short, they are anxious to get the most
authentic information they can. I beg
to ask the somewhat lengthy question
standing in my name.
Mr. ROBB: I have a somewhat lengthy
answer. The scheme to which the right
hon. Senator refers is quite distinct from
the campaign for the collection of scrap
metal. Metal railings provide a ready
source of good quality iron and steel and
are, therefore, particularly valuable for
war purposes.
The scheme for the removal of metal
railings in Northern Ireland is being
undertaken by the Ministry of Finance
on behalf of the Ministry of Supply,
which is the responsible authority.
Briefly, the proposal involves a comprehensive survey of every district to ensure
that all available railings are listed for
removal. Notice of the proposal to remove railings in any given area will be
published by the Ministry in the Press,
and owners will thus be afforded timely
intimation of the Government's intention
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
18 AUGUST 1942
to take their railings. Moreover, when
the time comes for severance of the
railings by the Ministry's contractors, an
individual notice will be served upon each
occupier informing him of the arrangements made.
The term "railings" includes iron or
steel posts, chains, bollards, gates, stiles
and similar articles, and the clearance
scheme will embrace all railings other
than those which fall within the following
broad categories:-
(1) Railings which should be maintained for safety and security reasons;
(2) Railings enclosing live stock, and
(3) Railings of special artistic merit.
or historic interest.
It will be the duty of the Ministry of
Finance to see that exceptions in respect
of public safety and security, as well as
those affecting agricultural interests, are
wisely and reasonably applied in Northern
Ireland. It is not intended that railings
of special artistic merit or of historic
interest should be scheduled for removal,
and, on this ground only, owners will be
given an opportunity of appealing, provided that their representations are made
within 14 days of the date of public
notice. Careful consideration will be
given to such appeals by the Ministry of
Finance, which will be advised in the
matter by a committee of experts.
Care will be exercised by the Government to ensure that disturbance and unnecessary damage to owners' property
through the removal of railings is kept to
a minimum, and the Government will, as
far as possible, make good unavoidable
damage in the removal. The Government
cannot, however, undertake to provide
substitutes for fixtures removed.
As regards the right hon. Senator's
specific questions-(1) The case of gates
in rural districts is receiving careful
consideration by the Ministry and, for
the present, it is proposed to exclude
isolated farm gates. Moreover, where
agricultural land is enclosed by a hedge
or wall with an iron gate, the gate will
not be removed. As already mentioned,
railings (including gates) enclosing live
stock will not be scheduled, and exception
will also be made in favour of railings
(including gates) necessary to protect
growing crops and orchards bordering a
public highway, where there is a risk of
damage or theft of the crops.
(2) With regard to compensation, it is
hoped that owners will surrender their
railings without claiming compensation.
In Great Britain the percentage of persons claiming compensation has been very
low. Under Defence Regulation No.
50B the compensation payable in respect
of the severance of fixtures will be a sum
equal to the price which might reasonably
have been expected to be obtained upon
a sale of the fixtures effected immediately
before the severance to a purchaser intending to sever them, no account being
taken of any appreciation due to the
national emergency. To those who seek
compensation, payment will be offered on
this basis, and the provisions of the Compensation (Defence) Act, 1939, will apply
accordingly.
(3) The arrangements for the disposal
of severed railings are in the hands of the
Ministry of Supply, and no responsibility
will rest upon the Ministry of Finance in
this matter. It may be mentioned, however, that the severed railings will usually
be delivered to local merchants at an
inclusive charge fixed by the Ministry of
Supply in the light of all the circumstances. Where railings are given free
of charge, the benefit of this generous
action accrues entirely to the State.
(4) The Ministry of Finance has received an assurance from the Ministry of
Supply that that department does not
anticipate any difficulty in the collection
and shipment of severed railings from
Northern Ireland.
Mr. LESLIE: May I be allowed to
thank the right hon. Gentleman for his
very extensive reply, which is satisfactory.

The following question stood on the

To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state what percentage
of arable land will be ordered to be

the Minister of Agriculture will take

and tell them what farm produce, such
as wheat, potatoes and flax, is considered of chief importance for next
year's cropping, and whether any
major regulations for next year's
harvest will be promulgated before the
end of this month.
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
Major DOBBS: The question standing
in my name is very clear. I want to
make two points. I hope that the
Minister of Agriculture will let farmers
know as much as he possibly can what he
wants them to do, and I am sure that the
farmers will endeavour to do what he
wants.
Another point which I do not
think is realised is the question of letting
farmers know as early as possible in the
season. Last year we had a very nice
little memorandum from the Minister of
Agriculture, and it was called "A Guide
to Food Production." It was sent out
on the 28th November last year. That
seems perhaps early enough, but one of
the points to which I wished to draw
attention was the question of wintergrown wheat. Winter-grown wheat,
especially in the district which I come
from, is sown in October. Any farmer
going to sow in October should know now,
not later. It is no use telling us on the
last day or so of November to sow winter
wheat. We simply cannot do it. Last
year I sowed winter wheat in October.
I was inclined to sow more after the end
of October, but was unable to do it. I
hope that the Minister will tell us what
they want as early as possible.
an
Mr. ROBB: It will not be possible
before the end of August to state what
percentage of arable land the Ministry
will require to be ploughed next year.
The matter is at present under consideration,
and it is hoped to make
announcement before the end of September. It can be stated now that it will be
the aim of the Ministry for 1943 to secure
a very much larger acreage under wheat
and flax, as well as an increase in the
acreage under potatoes.
It is hoped that farmers will make
every effort to have more land ploughed
in early autumn so that wheat can be
sown in good time.
Mr. LESLIE: Arising out of that,
may I ask whether consideration will be
given to the fact that the whole of
Northern Ireland is not, so to speak,
equally situated. The trouble is, of
course, that some parts of the country
differ from other parts. Up in the very
north, I am afraid the general impression
is that farmers at the moment, in endeavouring to follow out the Tillage
Order, are trying not to do more than
they ought or wish, but more than they
can.
At approximately this time of the
year a man is in swithers, as they say
locally, with his hay-seed crop before the
flax crop is saved, and then along comes
the oat crop. Nobody wants to suggest
that the farmer should not do all he can,
but the point is whether it is necessary to
compel him to till as high as 40 per cent.
The problem is lack of sunshine, and the
more land a man has, the longer sunshine
he requires. I am afraid the right hon.
Gentleman cannot ensure that, but I
would like this matter considered.
Mr. ROBB: I will bring the point to
the notice of the Ministry.
Major DOBBS: Arising out of the
answer of the Leader of the House, I
hope that what I said about the impossibility of growing winter wheat in my
part of the country in November will be
brought to the notice of the Ministry.

The enactments mentioned in subsection (1) of this section shall, in their
application to the Ministry, be subject
to such exceptions and modifications as
may be made by the Governor of
Northern Ireland by Order in Council.
He pointed out that among the enactments made was this particular mention
of this Act. It was not intended that
that should be modified by Order in
Council. Accordingly, to make it quite
clear, I move that the words, "The enactments mentioned in sub-section one of
this section" shall be omitted, and that
there shall be substituted the words,
"The provisions of the said Electricity
(Supply) Acts and Schedule." That
makes the point quite clear.

Armagh Town Rates.
PERSONAL EXPLANATION.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: On 18th August
last I put a question about Armagh city
rates for certain years. When doing so,
I quoted from a statement by the Minister
of Home Affairs, made during the debate
in another place on the Belfast Corporation Bill, that when Mr. Hanna was
appointed commissioner in Armagh the
first rate he had to strike was 11s in the
£." I denied this and stated:
That statement of figures was untrue. During the whole of Mr.
Hanna's commissionership he never
struck a town rate of 11s in the £.
I was wrong, and the Minister of Home
Affairs was right. I now wish to withdraw that statement and apologise for
having made it. I was under the impression that Mr. Hanna, having been
appointed commissioner in March, 1934,
struck his first rate for the year ending
31st March, 1934, and here is where I
made the mistake. Mr. Hanna's first
rate was 11s in the £ and was for the
year ending 31-3-35.
Lest it might be thought that I deliberately omitted to ask for the year
ending 31-3-35, I wish it to be clearly
understood that I did not. I was convinced Mr. Hanna's first rate was for
year ending 31-3-34, and therefore I was
not concerned with the rate for the year
Finance Bill-
Second Reading
1 OCTOBER 1942
I
ending 31-3-35. I trust I have made
myself clear, and that this explanation
will be accepted by all concerned.
would not like to abuse the privileges of
this House by stating what was untrue,
and I wish at all times to be frank and
just to everyone.
In this case I made a mistake, and in
now withdrawing and apologising, I wish
to say how much I regret any pain caused
to the Minister or any of his officials by
what I said.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
am sure the House sympathises with the
hon. Member in having made the mistake, and I will see that the apology he
has so fully made is conveyed to the
Minister concerned.
Business of the House.
Resolved, "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's sitting,
for the purpose of taking the Second
Reading of the Finance Bill when it is
brought from the House of Commons."
—(Mr. Robb.)
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Finance
Bill, to which they desire the concurrence
of this House.
Finance Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: This Bill proposes to give
legislative effect to changes in excise duty
which were foreshadowed by the Minister
of Finance in his Budget speech in
another place.
Generally, it falls into two parts. It
increases the rates of entertainment duty
and it gives power to adjust, as equity
demands, the amounts of retail liquor
licence duty payable by traders who have
been affected by war circumstances.
Clause 1 gives effect to the Budget increases in entertainment duty which have
operated since 31st May last, by virtue of
a Financial Resolution of the House of
Commons.
The rates now in operation correspond
with those in Great Britain, the only
divergence from the rest of the United
Kingdom being a concession, in that outdoor sports continue for the present to
enjoy complete exemption from duty in
Northern Ireland.
There are two scales of duty, the
ordinary rate, as set out in Part I of the
Schedule, applying in the main to cinemas,
and the reduced rate, as in Part II of
the Schedule, which applies to entertainments in which the performers are actually present and performing, such as
theatres, music-halls, and concerts.
Hon. Senators will have noted that
under the new scales of duty, concessions
have been given in the case of the more
cheaply-priced seats, in that no increase
of duty has been imposed where the maximum admission price, inclusive of duty,
does not exceed 7d to entertainments
liable for the full rate of duty, or does not
exceed 1s where the reduced rate of duty
is applicable.
The remainder of the Bill deals with
liquor licences. It is highly technical,
and I do not propose to go into a detailed
explanation of its various provisions.
Generally, it fulfils an undertaking, given
by the Minister of Finance, to introduce
any concessions given in Great Britain in
the amount of duty payable by traders
who have been affected by war circumstances, and the Bill accordingly adopts,
with the necessary minor alterations to
meet local circumstances, the concessions
already applicable in Great Britain under
the Finance Act, 1942.
The general object is to take power to
adjust the amounts of retail liquor licence
duty payable by traders under war conditions, and the related one of providing
for the suspension and subsequent revival
of licences for the sale of liquor where
was circumstances involve the temporary
discontinuance of a licensed business.
The licence duties in question are those
of (a) on-licences, which cover the sale of
liquor for consumption on the premises in
public-houses, hotels, and restaurants,
etcetera, and (b) off-licences, which permit sales only for consumption off the
premises.
It should perhaps be made clear that
the equity of the proposed concessions
arises from the present general and severe
shortage of supplies and the fact that, as
hon. Senators are aware, the current
licence duties are based on an annual
value, fixed before the war, related to the
Finance Bill
Adjournment
THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
expectation of future trade in each.
licensed premises, which expectation has
been falsified under war conditions which
were not visualised at the period of assessment. The remedy of a general revision
of the annual values of licensed premises,
instead of concessions related to excise
duty, has not been considered because,
apart from its impracticability under wartime conditions, temporarily adverse trade
conditions are not peculiar to the licensed
trade.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That the
Bill be now committed." It is not usual
in this House to send a Finance Bill to
Committee, but this measure is not a
simple Finance Bill imposing charges and
duties. The second part of the Bill deals
with excise licences and contains provisions with regard to the suspension of
certificates for excise licences, and is not
simply a measure dealing with the imposition or remission of duties. Accordingly,
although it is not usual in this House to
send a Financial Bill to Committee, I move
that the Bill be now committed, and, if
I may, I would advise the House to vote
"Content," and not "Not Content," as
is usual.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Thursday next.
Resolved, "That this House do now
adjourn until Thursday next."-(Sir
Joseph Davison.)
Adjourned accordingly at Three
o'clock until Thursday next.
Finance Bill

Business of the House.
Resolved, "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's sitting
for the purpose of taking the Second

Bill, and Expiring Laws Continuance
Bill, when they are brought from the

Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Children
(Juvenile Courts) Bill, the County Court
Jurisdiction Bill, and the Expiring Laws
Continuance Bill, to which they desire
the concurrence of this House.
Children (Juvenile Courts) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, "That this Bill be now read
a Second time." This rather short
measure is designed to give immediate
legislative effect to one of the recommendations of the Lynn Committee on the
protection and welfare of the young and
the treatment of young offenders.
Bill brings about certain changes in the
present constitution of the juvenile court
which that Committee thought desirable
should be made. I do not propose to say
very much about the various clauses of
the Bill, which are really self explanatory.
The
The Bill provides for the appointment
of persons in a lay advisory capacity to
assist the resident magistrate on the
hearing of cases in the juvenile court.
The persons to be appointed will be
called children's guardians. It is proposed that, as far as practicable, one of
the guardians shall be a woman. The
duties of the guardians will be to assist
the resident magistrate in arriving at a
conclusion in individual cases. They will
have the right to interrogate witnesses
coming before them, but no other judicial
functions will attach to their office.
term of office for which guardians will be
appointed will be fixed at the time of
their appointment, and after their time
of office expires they will be eligible for
re-appointment.
The
This Bill must not be regarded as a
solution of the juvenile delinquency problem. It is merely an effort to put into
effect one change which is regarded by
many people as being one from which
good results will follow. I beg to move.
Mr. LESLIE: I really do not think
that this little Bill is a Bill which this
House ought to give a Second Reading to,
and therefore I am afraid I must make
the customary Amendment to leave out of
the Motion the word "now" and insert
instead the words "this day six months."
I make that Motion not from any objection to what I might describe as the substance of the Bill, and certainly without
any desire to anticipate the debate which
Children Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
I suppose will take place some day on the
main reports of the Lynn Committee.
The Lynn Committee, it may be recollected, reported as long ago as 1938, four
years ago, and this little scrap is the first
attempt that has been made to give
legislative sanction to any of its recommendations. In some ways one would
have welcomed this Bill as in the nature
of an experiment, but it does seem to me
that if the Bill is to do any good at all it
requires so much alteration that it really
would be simpler to introduce a new Bill.
That is the course which I hope will be
taken, for it is not a Bill one would like
to see defeated, but it is a Bill one would
like to see deferred, and therefore, to that
end, withdrawn and brought up again
next session in a more effective form.
The Bill did not, so far as I can
observe, evoke any extraordinary amount
of enthusiasm in another place, nor was
it subjected to anything in the nature of
constructive criticism, but it got through
without alteration, and the result is
before us to-day. As the right hon.
Gentleman indicated, the Bill does not
directly deal with juvenile delinquency,
although presumably that is what it is
intended to ensure. As a matter of fact,
juvenile delinquency, according to the Lynn
Report and the subsequent reports we
have received, does not appear to be any
too bad. It is not much worse now than
it was, but it does exist, and it is a question whether the passing of this Bill will
do anything which will alleviate it for
the future. I am afraid I do not think
it will, and therefore, as I say, I do not
think we ought to pass the Bill.
With that view, one has to inquire
what is wrong with the Bill. There
seems to me to be three or four points on
which the Bill can be attacked. The first
of them is really to some extent a question
of words. Words are always important
in an Act of Parliament. Sometimes
they affect the psychology of the Province,
and this Bill is an instance of the latter.
The Bill is for the appointment of what
are called children's guardians. The
people with whom it deals are not
children, not all of them. A child is a
person under 14 years of age, and at present a young person in Northern Ireland
is a person between 14 and 16, and I am
going to suggest to the House that it is
the latter class, the young person, to
whom the question of juvenile delinquency
particularly relates. If you study the
tables appended to the Lynn Report as
to the number of offences both indictable
and non-indictable, the greater number
of them, and the greater increase were
on the part of persons over 14 years of
age. The person you want to get at, if
you want to reduce juvenile deliquency,
is primarily the person between 14 and
16.
When you are dealing with a person
between 14 and 16 he is not
a child
according to the accepted definition. He
certainly does not want to be called a
child. If you grouped the child with the
young person the child would perhaps
feel uplifted, but if you grouped your
young person with your child the young
person will feel distinctly downcast. It
seems to me entirely wrong in a Bill
primarily intended for the young person
to use the expression "children" at all.
People commonly talk of the Children's
Court, but that is not the proper name.
The proper title is the Juvenile Court,
but everybody calls it the Children's
Court. If you want to inspire, as I
think you do, some sort of confidence in
your Court that is to be constructed in
the future in the minds of the young
people you do not want anything in the
shape of a misnomer; you do not want
to call people children's guardians.
In the second place, what are they
going to guard the young people from?
Are they to be protectors between the
child and the resident magistrate, if it
is going to be a resident magistrate, and
I am not quite sure that it always will
be, because I am not quite certain that
the Summary Jurisdiction Act of 1935
did not leave some powers in the hands
of the lay magistrates which might be
exercised in the case of children and
young persons. At all events, these
people are not children, and guardians
is not the right phrase. All children
are, of course, juveniles, but all juveniles
are not children, and I do not think we
should class the young persons with the
children. I do not know what other
form of expression could be used. The
best term I can think of in place of
children's guardians is juvenile confidant, and I get that from the Lynn
Report which says that these courts are
to be of a very informal character, and
the delinquent is to be brought to the
Children Bill-
Second Reading
27 OCTOBER 1942
table and talked to, not as man to man,
but as friend to child. The only phrase
I can think of at the moment is juvenile
confidant. I think you must not use
the word "children", and I do not like
the word "guardian”.
There is not, so far as I can see, any
hope of success in proceeding, as regards
the appointment of these people, by
whatever name
them,
you call
suggested in the second sub-clause :-
as
The Governor of Northern Ireland
shall appoint as children's guardians
for each petty sessions district such
number of persons qualified to deal
with juvenile cases as the said
Governor may think necessary.
If the Governor of Northern Ireland
is going to be asked to find the appropriate number of competent persons for
each petty sessions district in Northern
Ireland, and if he is going to wait until
he has got all the petty sessions districts
started, he is going to wait a very long
time. There are just over a hundred
petty sessions districts in Northern Ireland. Does anybody really think you
are going to get an adequate staff of
people really qualified to act as juvenile
confidants from each of these petty
sessions districts. The difficulties in the
matter are dealt with on page 47 of the
Lynn Report. It is pointed out that it
is very hard to get, not staff but officers,
because you do not want to use the
ordinary petty sessions court, and the
ordinary petty sessions court in many
districts leaves very much to be desired.
You are going to have a very great deal
of trouble in staffing the entire Province
in that way, and you want to staff it
with qualified persons. How the Governor
is going to know how the persons
appointed are qualified we are not told.
It would seem from the Lynn Report
-and I think something was said in
another place about it-that guardians
should receive advice from such organisations as the Boy Scouts, the Girl Guides,
the St. Vincent de Paul Society, the
Young Men's and Young Women's
Christian Associations, and people of that
kind. I am going to remind the House
that, according to a report that appeared
in the Belfast newspapers on Saturday, of
some 44,000 youngsters in Northern Ireland only about 11,000 belonged to any
of these organisations at all.
The only
two organisations likely to be of any use
in dealing with what I was going to describe as the submerged tenth whom you
want to get at is the St. Vincent de Paul
Society on the one side and the Salvation
Army on the other, because it is not the
Boy Scout or the Girl Guide who comes
before the juvenile court; it is children
who do not belong to those institutions.
It is no use looking to the recognised
youth institutions, because the child you
want to get at does not belong to those
societies at all. The clergyman of the
parish may be of some use, but if the
child was a young person as well under
the influence of his proper spiritual pastor
you are not going to be troubled with
him. It is the child outside all these institutions you want to get at, and how
are you going to get a satisfactory supply
of people competent to deal with those
children?
But assuming you have got your panel
of persons to be associated with the magistrates, how are they going to work? You
are not told how it is to be decided which
two persons out of the list you have
appointed are to act. Presumably they
are to make a rota for themselves in
order that the work may be shared
amongst them. Even that does not seem
to me to go far enough. Their powers
are going to be very limited. They are
only to interrogate a young person, and I
find it hard to believe that the people you
are likely to scrape up for this duty are
likely to be peculiarly competent to ask
questions. It is very unlikely. That is
the only power they have got to ask
questions. They are not given power to
confer with the presiding magistrate. That
is a power they want, and if you do not
give them that power I do not think they
can properly do their duty. If they are
to do any good they must not only confer
with the resident magistrate, but they
must be prepared to follow up their cases
very much in the manner of a district
visitor. It is no use bringing your young
person up one day and then throwing
him out on the street with a caution
unless some further interest is taken in
him. I know you have got probation
officers, but at all events you want these
lay advisers to be people who will continue to take an interest in the cases that
come before them.
Hence it follows that, having first
selected your rota you must provide for a
Children Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
surplus of guardians. It is no use sending two people to take any cases that may
come before them. The right hon.
Gentleman has suggested that one of the
two persons should always be a woman,
but you are going to have cases where
your two persons will be women, and
cases where neither of them will be a
woman, and even the religion of those
two associates becomes important. Above
all, you are going to have cases which it
is desirable to have heard by the same
people who heard the cases before as
regards the same persons. You want not
only a rota, but people to arrange
amongst themselves not only as regards.
their actual attendance, but which of
them shall hear a particular case. Of
course all these things could be put into
the Bill, but I think it would mean so
much change that it would be simpler to
withdraw this Bill and bring in another
one.
I think we shall find it is necessary to
bring the Bill into operation by degrees.
The clamant need at the moment is in
Belfast, and to a lesser degree in Londonderry. Juvenile courts in other towns sit
very infrequently. When I was a practising magistrate some years ago, we held
a children's court very rarely indeed, two
or three times a year, and I have no
reason to suppose the number has
increased since. Offences by children in
the country are mostly of the orchard
raiding type, and are usually dealt with
by the parents. It would be a good
thing if you could get the parents to
punish their offspring for breaking the
law, but unfortunately in this Province
you do not always get that. Parents are
too often the receivers, and the willing
receivers, of property they know to be
misappropriated. It is the parents you
want to get at and, above all, you want
to bring in a Bill to make it specially
applicable to the young person between
14 and 16, if you are going to keep the
age at 16 as at present. I know there is
a proposal to raise it to 17, but at the
moment this Bill is applicable to the
child and young person between 7 and
16, and I do not think this Bill is going
to help. Whether an altered Bill might
be of use as an experiment, first of all in
Belfast, then in Londonderry, and then
extended to the smaller towns and districts I do not know, but as things stand
at the moment I cannot see that this Bill
will be of any use whatever, and in some
directions I think it will have a mischievous result. Accordingly, I move the
rejection of the measure.
Question put, "That the word 'now'
stand part of the Motion."
Mr. ROBB: As no one else seems inclined to speak on this Bill I would like
to deal with the points the right hon.
and learned Senator has made. First of
all, he objects to it being called a
Children's Bill, because some of the persons to be dealt with under it are not
by legal definition children. It is really
called Children (Juvenile Courts) Bill.
The title of the Bill is-
To provide for the appointment of
children's guardians to assist courts of
summary jurisdiction on the hearing of
charges against, and of applications
relating to, children and young persons.
The title of the Bill, therefore, covers
young persons, although the right hon.
Gentleman seems to think it does not. It
is quite true that the short title of the
Bill is "Children (Juvenile Courts) Bill,"
but the reason for that is obvious.
It is
one of a code of Acts of Parliament which
are referred to in the last clause of the
Bill, and they have been called as a group
the Children's Acts, Northern Ireland.
That is the reason for calling this Bill a
Children Bill. It does not deal only with
children, and as far as the question of
words is concerned, there really is nothing
in it. Senator Leslie objects. to the word
"guardians" and prefers the word "confidant." I do not know how that appeals
to the Members of the Senate, but to me
it does not seem to have anything in it.
We are accustomed to the word
"guardian," and we are not accustomed
to the word "confidant". If you go a
little further and call them "confessors"
you may find yourselves in great
difficulty. I think the word "guardian"
is as good as can reasonably be expected.
in
The right hon. and learned Senator
says there will be great difficulty in
getting qualified people to act in this
capacity. That is quite recognised, and
that is why the Bill is drawn in the
form it is. It is recognised that the Bill
can
be put into operation only by
degrees first in Belfast and then in
Londonderry, and afterwards possibly in
other towns. Senator Leslie made one
Children Bill-
Second Reading
27 OCTOBER 1942
suggestion which I have not heard made
before-that possibly from the personnel
of the Salvation Army eligible persons
might be found. I thank him for that
suggestion, and I will have it conveyed
to the proper authority. The right hon.
and learned Senator also said it was
stated in another place that organisations
to be consulted with regard to the
appointment of suitable persons as
guardians would be such organisations
as the Boy Scouts and the Girl Guides,
and that, in fact, the children with whom
we are going to deal are not members of
such organisations. The suggestion was
that these organisations should be consulted as to persons likely to be useful
in the capacity of advisers as children's
guardians. Connected with those
ganisations, there are persons who have
been accustomed to deal with children
and with children's problems.
quite true that Sunday school teachers
would perhaps be equally eligible, but it
is not laid down in the Bill that any
person so selected shall be the nominee
of any such organisation.
or-
It is
Then Senator Leslie says the Bill will
be difficult to work; that you will have to
have a rota, and provision that if A and
B try one case they shall not try the next
case. There are difficulties in working
out all schemes of this sort, and the Bill
has been designedly made flexible and
does not lay down any provisions with
regard to matters of that sort, because
this is an experiment. Nobody knows
how it is going to work, and it is only
by experiment that you can find out the
best way to work this scheme. It is not
an ambitious scheme in any sense. It
takes only one small step forward, recommended by every Committee, which
has sat touching this matter that there
should be associated with the resident
magistrate some persons having a knowledge of the district, who could convey
advice to the resident magistrate as to
what would be the best course to be
adopted and, possibly, the most suitable
punishment. The Bill does not deal with
the general problem of what is called
juvenile delinquency. I agree with the
right hon. and learned Senator that a
great deal of nonsense is talked about
the increase in juvenile delinquency. The
police figures certainly do not support
any such general charge against the
young people of this Province. Of
course, we all recognise as a fact that
parental authority is not exercised to the
extent it was exercised in my youth. I
do not know that the children are any
worse off for that, and I do not think
their acts justify any charge of a general
demoralisation of the youth of this
community of ours.
On the other hand we have to consider
that the police system is better organised
to deal with the young offender than it
was 40 years ago, and it may be that
more young people are made amenable to
the law. I am not quite sure that that
is a good thing. Personally I have always
had some doubts as to the propriety of
bringing young children before courts,
but Parliament in its wisdom has decided
otherwise. This Bill is an endeavour to
constitute a court better qualified to deal
with these young people when they do
come before it. In other words it is an
experiment. Whether it will work out
well or not nobody knows, but we hope it
will produce good results. It is in that
spirit that the Government have produced
this measure, and I ask the House to give
it a Second Reading.
Mr. LESLIE: I am afraid I must say
that the right hon. Gentleman entirely
misinterpreted what I said about the
wording of the Bill. What I objected
to was the expression "children's guardians," because I pointed out that the
effect of that would be to put your young
person for whom I pointed out the Bill
was directly intended-at arm's length at
once. When a young person is charged
before this court he will turn round and
say, "Who is going to look after me?"
He is told, "The children's guardian,"
and he replies, "I am not a child." It
is the psychological effect I am looking
As regards the use of the expression

"adviser” or anything you like.
at.
If it is the wish of the House that this
experiment should be tried let it be tried.
I cannot do other than express my regret
that opportunity has not been taken of
the four years' interval to produce something more likely to be more generally

County Courts Bill
Expiring Laws Bill
THE SENATE
Bill accordingly read a Second time and

House for Tuesday next.
County Court Jurisdiction Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time." (Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: In moving the Second
Reading of this Bill I wish to emphasise
that its object is not to modernise the
county court, but rather to adjust the
existing jurisdiction up to present money
values.
Hon. Members will appreciate that the
county court with its present limitation
of jurisdiction is not, in effect, serving
the purposes intended when the original
jurisdiction was fixed some 80 years ago,
when the value of money was more or less
double that of to-day. A great many
cases originally intended to come within
the county court jurisdiction have now to
be brought in the high court with its
attendant heavy expense out of all proportion to the amount involved to the
litigants concerned.
For some considerable time past there
has been an insistent demand from interested persons
the
and bodies in
Province for the extension of the County
Court jurisdiction in order to bring
within its scope a large number of cases
which must at present be brought in the
High Court. For the purpose of rectifying this state of affairs it is proposed to
increase the ordinary civil bill jurisdiction.
of the County Court from £50 to £100,
and to increase the equity and probate
jurisdiction from £500 to £1,000 in the
case of personal property, and from £30
to £50 annual value where land is
involved.
I should perhaps explain that the
County Courts in
Courts in Northern Ireland
are mainly governed by the Civil
Bill Courts (Ireland) Act, 1851,
the Civil Bill Courts (Ireland) Act,
1874, and the County Officers and Courts
(Ireland), Act, 1877. Minor amendments have been made by other statutes,
but those just mentioned may be regarded
as the basic enactments under which the
County Court functions. The jurisdiction conferred by the Act of 1851 covered
every cause of action at law for debt or
damage, except slander, libel and breach.
of promise of marriage, and was limited
•
to £40. It was increased to £50 by the
Act of 1877 and has since then remained
unaltered. The Act of 1874 deals with
actions involving questions of title to
land and the jurisdiction is limited to
£30 annual value. Equity and probate
matters are covered by the Act of 1877,
and the jurisdiction of the court in these
matters is limited to £500 where personalty is involved and to £30 annual
value where land is involved.
The measure now before the House
amends these statutes in order to extend
the jurisdiction in the manner I have
indicated. The Bill is more or less selfexplanatory, and I do not, therefore, propose to go into any great detail other
than to refer to the first four clauses.
Clause 1 extends the ordinary or the
general jurisdiction of the County Court
from £50 to £100. Clause 2 extends the
equity jurisdiction of the court from
£500 to £1,000, and from £30 annual
value to £50 annual value. The increase
in the probate and administration jurisdiction is dealt with by Clause 3, and this,
like the equity jurisdiction, has been
increased from £500 to £1,000, and from
£30 annual value to £50 annual value.
Clause 4 relates to the jurisdiction of the
County Court in actions involving questions of title to land, and this is increased
from £30 annual value to £50 annual
value. The remaining clauses in the Bill
are merely consequential.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.

XXV be suspended for this day's sitting

Exchequer and Financial Provisions
(No. 2) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That the
Bill be now read a Second time." There
are four Clauses in the Bill. The first
Clause deals with the Civil Contingencies
Fund. Under the Exchequer and
Financial Provisions Act, 1941, the
Ministry of Finance have power, up to
the 30th September, 1942, to increase the
capital of this fund to £350,000. Under
present conditions, it was thought advisable that this power should be retained
for some time longer, and in order to
avoid further legislation on the subject
next year, this Clause proposes to extend
the period by two years, that is, until
the 30th September, 1944.
I will
Clause 2 is rather complicated.
try to explain it to the House. New
borrowing powers are not conferred upon
the Ministry by this Clause, the chief
purpose of which will be found in subclause 2. This merely brings into operation the same provision in relation to
Exchequer capital borrowing which exists
under United Kingdom legislation and
which in fact already applies in Northern
Ireland in relation to the proceeds of
Ulster Savings Certificates. The effect
is that the proceeds of borrowings are
"pooled" so as to enable new flotations to
be used without any particular earmark,
and to allow repayments of advances to
be re-issued in the same unrestricted way.
It provides a greater degree of flexibility,
but at the same time retains the limitation that Government may only borrow in respect of any purpose for which
Parliament has sanctioned advances from
the Consolidated Fund. It is not, however, intended that the clause should
apply to money raised by Treasury Bills.
The effect of that is: Besides the
Ulster Savings Certificates we have two
main capital borrowings-Ulster 3 per
cent. stock, redeemable next April, and
the Northern Ireland 33 per cent. redeemable in 1950 and 1954. The 3
per cent. stock is appropriated to two
purposes-supplementing the Unemployment Fund and the Loans Guarantee
advances. So far as the Unemployment
Fund is concerned, there is no drain on
the fund at the present time, and the
same may be said of the Loans
Guarantee advances. The 3 was created
in order to finance the Transport
Board and advances to the Government
Loans Fund. Owing to the changed
conditions the Transport Board not only
does not need to be financed, but it is
hoped to be in a position to repay some
of the advances which have been made.
Owing to war conditions the Government
Loans Fund is in such a state that the
Exchequer is left with a certain amount
of money. Something may arise for
which advance may be required from the
Consolidated Fund, but these moneys
could not be used. All this Clause does
is to enable the Ministry of Finance to
finance out of the money it has in hand
purposes other than the four specifically
mentioned as the object of these two
funds.
Clause 3 deals with a minor matter.
Besides his licence duty a retailer of
intoxicating liquor pays a claim fund
charge, or what is more commonly known
as the compensation levy. That was to
finance spirit grocers whose licences were
taken away from them. Under certain
conditions existing legislation permits
Children Bill
Expiring Laws Bill
3 NOVEMBER 1942
the refund or the remission of licence
duty where premises have been blitzed or
trade has diminished, but there is no
statutory authority for a similar concession with regard to the compensation
fund, a charge which is directly related.
to the licence duty. This Clause provides
for the removal of that anomaly.
Clause 4 provides for the retention in
office of the present holder of the post of
Comptroller and Auditor-General until
he has attained the age of 68 years; that
is to say, an extension of one year.
Mr. LESLIE: I rise only to ask a
question on sub-clause (2) of Clause 1.
Does the proposal to have retrospective
operation mean that advance action has
been taken, so to speak? That is a point
which will be dealt with on the Committee
stage.
Mr. ROBB: I do not know, but I will
find out.

this year's payment?
Mr. ROBB: It is retrospective.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: The compensation will be refunded.
Mr. ROBB: If it is paid it will, I presume, be refunded.

The Winter Assize Order, 1942.

Report of Court of Inquiry appointed
