Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act), and ordered to lie on the Table.
Question to Minister.
RELIGIOUS INSTRUCTION IN
SECONDARY SCHOOLS.
Mr. LESLIE: I have to ask a private
notice question of which you have been
acquainted. I beg to ask the Leader of
the House, has his attention been called
to the discussions at recent meetings of
the Diocesan Councils of the Church of
Ireland here in Northern Ireland, in
which it was urged that the time spent
by teachers in secondary schools in giving
religious instruction should be counted as
part of the time qualifying for grants to
the schools in respect of those teachers,
and whether the Ministry can see its way
to meet the request which such discussions implied?
As the House knows, this is a matter
that has caused a good deal of discussion
outside, discussion which it is unnecessary
to repeat.
The MINISTER OF EDUCATION
(Mr. Robb): The Ministry has for some
time been considering what steps can be
taken to meet the requests put forward,
not merely by the Church of Ireland but
also by the Presbyterian and Methodist
Churches, that the service mentioned in
the question should be allowed to count
as part of the time which teachers must
give to instruction in secondary schools
to enable the schools to receive grants in
respect of such teachers. The position at
the moment is that each teacher in a
secondary school in Northern Ireland
must give to the subjects on the compulsory programme a minimum of 18
hours a week before he can be admitted
to the pension scheme or appointed to the
authorised staff. In practice, on the
average, so far as the Ministry can estimate, teachers do give 23 hours a week
to the work of the school, and the
Ministry has taken the view that there
was plenty of time above the 18 hours
minimum which the teachers could give
to religious instruction should the boards
of the secondary schools, in whose hands
the matter lies, so require. But this has
apparently not worked out in practice,
so the Ministry has decided to alter the
regulations in this way. The minimum
time will remain at 18 hours a week, but
for the future only 16 hours will be
required to be given to subjects of the
ordinary curriculum, the remaining two
hours to be spent on such other teaching
duties in connection with the school as
the board of the school shall decide, and
Bible instruction may be the subject of
instruction for the whole or any part of
these two hours as the governors may
wish.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Am I to understand that this regulation applies to all
secondary schools?
Mr. ROBB: Certainly, but some
secondary schools do not need it.
Business of the House.
Resolved, "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's sitting
for the purpose of taking the Second
Reading of the Government Loans Bill,
the Appropriation (No. 2) Bill, and the
Finance (No. 2) Bill, when they are
brought from the House of Commons."

Government Loans Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That the
Bill be now read a Second time."
E
Finance (No. 2) Bill
Government Loans Bill

THE SENATE
[Mr. Robb]
The Government Loans Act, 1939,
limits the sums which may be issued from
the Government Loans Fund for the purpose of loans to the cumulative total of
£11,500,000. We have almost reached
this total, though by reason of repayments the total amount of principal outstanding is under £7,000,000. To enable
further sums to be issued it is now proposed to place the restriction not on the
sum total of issues made but on the total
amount of principal outstanding at any
one time, and that the new limit should
be £10,000,000.
It
The second Clause is concerned with
the income account of the Government
Loans Fund and alters the provisions of
sub-sections (4) and (6) of section 6 of
the Government Loans Act, 1939, which
determine the action to be taken when
a surplus or a deficiency occurs in any
financial year. Sub-section (4), as it
stands, provides that surplus income
should be paid into the Exchequer.
has never been the practice to do this,
but rather to retain such surplus income
within the fund so that interest rates to
borrowers might be kept as low as possible. It is also felt that this provision
regarding the payment of the surplus into
the Exchequer has helped to give rise to
the misapprehension current in some
circles that the Exchequer makes a profit
on the operations of the fund. The new
sub-section accordingly directs that any
surplus income shall now be paid into the
sinking fund created for meeting any
capital deficiency in the fund.
Sub-section (6) provides that when the
income account of the fund shows a deficiency as a result of the year's working,
such deficiency shall first be made good
from the Consolidated Fund, and that
the Consolidated Fund shall be repaid out
of moneys voted by Parliament. As in
all probability a deficiency in one year
will be followed by a surplus in the
following year, it is felt that such a
provision is unnecessary, and the new
sub-section allows the deficiency of one
year to be met out of the income of the
fund. The effect simply is that repayments to the Consolidated Fund in respect
of sums issued to meet the deficiency may
not be made so promptly. Part of the
value in having a deficiency met out of
voted moneys lay, of course, in the fact
that Parliament's attention was thus
drawn to the matter, but the new subsection also provides that Parliament
should still be kept informed when a
deficiency arises.
As to Clause 3, there is some doubt as
to whether the principal Act allows us
to use any other name than "Ulster
Loans Stock" for any new security that
may be issued. There have been suggestions from time to time that other
names should be employed. Whether
there would be an advantage in employing another name I do not know, but at
any rate this sub-clause gives power to
use some other different name.
Finally, it is proposed to write-off all
the principal of a land improvement loan
which has been outstanding since 1924.
The total amount was £61 19s 6d.
Though the loan will be written off from
the assets from the Government Loans
Fund, borrowers' liability will still
remain and efforts will continue to be
made to secure repayment. I beg to
move.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.

Finance (No. 2) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That the
Bill be now read a Second time." I will
Finance (No. 2) Bill-
Second Reading
10 NOVEMBER 1942
read you first the brief given to me, and
I will then try to explain the first
Clause as best I can.
Since the passing of the Finance (1909-
10) Act, 1910, it has always been assumed
that the documents mentioned in subsection (6) of section 74 of that Act were
exempt from the provisions of sub-section
(2) of the same section. The English
Court of Appeal has very recently ruled
that this assumption is incorrect, and the
effect of this ruling is to place thousands
of documents executed in the past thirty
odd years in the position of being improperly stamped. In order to legalise
past practice and to give effect to what
was the intention of the Act, this present
Clause now places the matter beyond all
doubt. Similar action has already been
taken by the Parliament in Great Britain.
Up to the year 1910, if property was
transferred from A to B and nothing paid
for it, it could be done by a 10s stamp on
the deed. The Government saw this was
a source of income, and it was accordingly
enacted by Section 74 of the Finance Act,
1910, that where property passed from
A to B the deed should, in addition or
in substitution for the '10s stamp, bear a
stamp corresponding to the value of the
property transferred. Under sub-section
2 it was enacted that such deeds should
not be admissible in evidence unless
furnished with an adjudication stamp and
had been submitted to the Commissioners
of Inland Revenue. Sub-section 6 exempted from this provision certain classes
of deeds. For instance, if you transferred
property to your bank to secure a loan
you did that with a 10s stamp, without
having to pay the amount of the value
of the security. There were various exceptions, and that is one.
From 1910 down to last year, it was
always assumed that if a document was
of that class the 10s stamp was enough,
and clearly enough. It was also assumed
that the deed did not have to be adjudicated. The point was raised in England
last year, and the Court of Appeal quite
rightly decided that, although such deeds
did not have to bear anything but a 10s
stamp, still there must be an adjudication. Members of the legal profession
had acted otherwise for thirty years.
Not only had they been under the
impression that it was all right, but the
Commissioners of Inland Revenue had
themselves issued a circular in which they
said it was all right. The Court of
Appeal said: "Notwithstanding circular
or no circular, and no matter what the
opinion of the Commissioners may be, we
are here to see that what Parliament did
enact is enacted, and we decide that Parliament did enact that although these
deeds did not need to have the ad valorem
stamp duty, they have to be adjudicated." A large number of these deeds
have been executed during the last
thirty years and were not adjudicated.
Accordingly, in England the law was
amended to conform with what had been
the practice for 30 years, and we are proposing to do that here.
The second Clause is designed so as to
allow our Estate Duty Branch to give
such information as is required from time
to time by the Inland Revenue Department to help in the course of its investigations. Though there has always been
a statutory bar placed upon our Estate
Duty Branch disclosing such information
to a department of the Imperial Government the need did not really arise because
our department could say: "Well, if you
go to the Probate Office and look at the
affidavit upon which the letters of
administration are given you will get all
the information that we could give you,
consequently we do not need to give you
any information." A couple of years
ago the filing of these affidavits was discontinued, and, accordingly, when the
Inland Revenue people came to the Estate
Duty Branch in this country there was
no place to which we could refer them.
They gave us all the information they
had, and they said: "We give you all the
information
we
we have, and
have
statutory power to do so. You ought to
have statutory power to give us all the
information you have." In order to
enter into a reciprocal arrangement between the two branches this Clause has
been introduced. It provides that our
Estate Duty Office can communicate to
the Inland Revenue, and also to the Commissioners of Excise and Customs, information within their power to help them
in their investigations. I beg to move,
"That the Bill be now read a Second
time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Financial Provisions Bill 264
Children Bill
THE SENATE

Mr. LESLIE: Before we finally pass

again what I endeavoured to say on the

for ensuring, so far as practicable, that
the people selected to sit as children's
guardians in the case of a child under
fourteen, and the people selected to sit
in the same capacity, still called
children's guardians, for a person between
fourteen and sixteen are not necessarily
the same. Their qualifications are not
necessarily identical. That is a point
which, I think, should be borne in mind.

tions, 1934, Amending Regulations, No.

15,
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Business of the House.
Resolved, "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's sitting
for the purpose of taking the Second
Reading of the Fisheries Bill when it is
brought from the House of Commons."
-(Mr. Robb.)
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Fisheries
Bill, to which they desire the concurrence
of this House.
Fisheries Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
beg to move, "That this Bill be now read
a Second time."
This short Bill empowers each of the
four Boards of Conservators to increase
the existing rates of licence duty on all
commercial fishing instruments and devices by a maximum of 50 per cent. and
to raise up to a similar maximum the
minimum contribution of 10 per cent. on
poor law valuation at present payable by
the users of productive commercial
fisheries.
The Bill has been brought forward in
consequence of the increased expenditure
on the provision of fishery protection services, due to the general increase in the
cost of labour. For several years past
the
Government has made an annual
grant of £240 to the Coleraine Conservancy Board, whose district comprises
roughly half of
of the entire area of
Northern Ireland. The Coleraine Board
found, however, that their revenue, including this grant, was insufficient to
enable them to maintain their protection
services on the same level during 1941
and 1942, and the Board was, therefore,
obliged to reduce their staff of water
bailiffs to a substantial extent. In order
to avoid the necessity of a further cut in
staff the Government agreed to make a
special supplementary grant of £350 to
the Coleraine Board in aid of its funds
during the latter part of 1942 and the
early months of 1943. This step, however, was taken only as a temporary
expedient, as, in the Government's view,
the high prices now being obtained for
fish by the owners of commercial fisheries
warranted an increase in the existing
rates of licence duty payable by such
owners. It is possible, of course, that not
all the Boards will find their existing
revenue insufficient for the maintenance
of adequate protection services, and, for
this reason, the powers given by the Bill
are merely permissive and not obligatory.
As the law now stands, the owner of
a productive commercial fishery is required to pay to the conservators of the
in
fishery district which fishery is
situated an annual rate which, together
with the total amount of licence duty paid
by him in the year, brings his total payment to the Board up to a sum representing 10 per cent. of the poor law valuation
of his fishery. As, obviously, cases might
arise in which an increase in the rate of
licence duty would not in itself entail an
increased total payment by the owner of
a productive commercial fishery, it is
necessary to empower the Boards to in
crease the annual rate of supplementary
payments to which I have referred.
Accordingly, the Bill gives the Boards
power to increase the annual rate to an
Fisheries Bill-
Second Reading
1 DECEMBER 1942
extent which would bring the total payment by the owner of a productive commercial fishery to a sum not exceeding
15 per cent. of the poor law valuation.
In other words, as in the case of licence
duty, the Boards would be empowered to
increase the existing 10 per cent. on poor
law valuation up to half as much again.
Salmon rods have been excluded from
the list of instruments in respect of which
a Board may exercise the powers conferred
by the Bill. In making this exclusion,
the Government has been influenced by
two important considerations. The first
of these is that strong objection was made
to the proposal in the 1934 Fisheries Bill
to increase the duty on salmon rods from
£1 to £2, and the Minister of Commerce
finally agreed to compromise by fixing the
duty at 30s per annum. The opposition
to the larger increase was based on the
fact that a large proportion of those who
took out salmon rod licences were persons
of modest means who would find it difficult to pay the higher duty proposed, and
that an increase to £2 per annum would
deprive the Boards of Conservators of
the valuable assistance which they were
obtaining from those who could not afford
to pay so high a rate. Salmon fishers are
perhaps the most effective bailiffs we have.
The second consideration which influenced
the Government in excluding salmon rods
from the scope of the present Bill was
that, under existing law, a salmon rod
licence issued by any one of the four
Boards is valid without further payment
in the other three districts. It will be
apparent, therefore, that if Boards of
Conservators were, as in the case of commercial instruments, given discretionary
power to increase the duty on salmon rods,
they might not all exercise this power, or
might not exercise it to the same extent.
As a result, salmon anglers would be left
with the choice of taking out their licences
from the Board whose rate of duty was
lowest, and there is no doubt that some of
the Boards would thus be deprived of their
fair share of the revenue derived from
salmon rod licences.
As the powers given by the Bill are discretionary, the Boards can safely be left
to use them only to such extent as the
maintenance of adequate protection services requires. beg to move.
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: I do not think any exception can be taken to the general
It is, as
principles of the Bill before us.
the right hon. Gentleman stated, not at
all unreasonable that those who derive
exceptional profits under the present
conditions of the fishing industry should
have to pay these duties. That seems
fair enough. At the same time, one
would like to be assured that Boards of
Conservators have expressed their
approval of the terms of the Bill, and
also whether the owners of the several
fisheries have been consulted. There is
nothing about that in the speech of the.
right hon. Gentleman, nor can I find anything pointing to that opinion in the
speech delivered in another place by the
Minister of Commerce. I would like to
know about that.
I think it would have been helpful if
we had been told something about the
figures. I do not mean the figures given
as to the amount paid out of Parliamentary funds to the Coleraine Conservators, but the amount of licence duties
attributable to these various thingsengine, net, and all the rest. The House
would then get some idea of the extent of
the difference in the amounts. Perhaps
that would be given at a later stage. As
a matter of fact, I do not think increasing the duties or rates, whatever you call
them, would make any serious difference
to the owner of a commercial fishery,
because that owner is already taxed on
his profits, and he would, therefore, have
an additional sum to deduct as an exception, so that the extra cost will ultimately
fall rather on the Imperial Exchequer
than on anywhere else or rather it will
fall on the fund which shares in the
income tax raised in Northern Ireland.
There remains one question. In the
first sub-clause the Board is empowered to
increase the licence duty by 50 per cent.
from the 1st January next. That will
then become a duty payable under the
Fisheries Acts. When they make that
increase of 50 per cent., are they to be
allowed in January, 1944, to bring it up
another 50 per cent.? That is where the
Bill is not clear. I would suggest that
words be put in there to make that clear.
I imagine the intention is that the rate
should rest at 50 per cent. and no more.
That is a Committee point. I mention it
now to allow the point to be looked into.
I imagine it is not to be accumulative,
that it is final.
Mr. ROBB: It is final.
Fisheries Bill
Adjournment
THE SENATE
Mr. LESLIE: It is a question whether
the Bill should say so.
The second subclause, which is a necessary corollary of
the first, only affects several fisheries, but
I think the word rate is rather unfortunate. It suggests to one's mind
whether the 40 per cent. allowance under
the Derating Act on an industrial hereditament which happens to be a fishery
comes into the picture, or whether it does
not. My own impression is that it does
not. That is a matter we might have
settled at a later stage. I would not
think there is any other objection to the
clause. I do not think it actually is an
income tax matter. I do not think we
are interfering with income tax by varying the rate which is paid. I do not
believe any question arises as to that.
If it arises it can be settled later. There
is no reason to oppose the Second Reading.
In Committee I will endeavour to put
down words to make sure that the position as regards non-accumulation is safeguarded.
Mr. ROBB: The Bill was brought in
under pressure by the Boards of Conservators. There are four boards in Northern
Ireland Coleraine, Ballycastle, Londonderry, and Enniskillen. The licence
duties paid for the year up to 31st
October, 1941, were-Coleraine, £938;
Ballycastle, £242; Londonderry, £610;
Enniskillen, £106. It was not intended
that the increase in duties should be
accumulative and go up and up. The 15
per cent. is on the annual value of the
hereditament. That is a point I will look
into. I had not thought of it.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Thursday next.

283 Unemployment Assistance-

THE SENATE
Regulations
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 15th December, 1942.
The Senate met at a quarter before
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Papers Presented to Parliament.
Ministry of Labour:
National Health Insurance (Navy,
Army and Air Force) Amendment Regulations, 1942.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Royal Assent.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): I
have to report to the House, That His
Grace the Governor of Northern Ireland,
in the name and on behalf of His Majesty
the King, has been pleased to give his
Assent to the following Bill agreed upon
by both Houses:
Fisheries.
Unemployment Assistance (Determination of Need and Assessment of
Needs) (Amendment) Regulations,
1942.
Mr. ROBB: Members will notice that
there are three Motions down in my
name to approve of Orders in Council
that have been made. The first two are
in a way interlocked, and if Members will
permit me I will deal with them together.
The first two Regulations now before
the House were made to improve the
rates of unemployment assistance and
supplementary pensions respectively.
They form part of a plan to help the condition of people who have to look principally to State funds for their means of
support and they are similar-I might
almost say identical-in character.
would, therefore, give a clearer picture
of the whole situation if I dealt with
them together; and with the permission
of the House I propose to take that
course.
It
In June last His Majesty's Government in Great Britain undertook to see
if anything could be done to alleviate the
difficulties created by wartime conditions
for persons in receipt of unemployment
pensions.
assistance or supplementary
After inquiry, the Government found
that, without anticipating the work of
the Beveridge Committee in framing a
long-term comprehensive scheme for social
improvement, which is available, the
immediate position could be improved by
taking action in three different ways:-
(1) By the all-round increases in the
scale rates for assistance and supplementary pensions which we now
have before us;
-
(2) By improvements in the special
winter allowances; and
(3) By the supply of clothing and
blankets to those who particularly
needed them.
The existing powers of the Assistance
Board were wide enough to put the
second and third of these into operation
without any
new regulations; but I
should like to say a word or two about
them to make the picture complete.
In previous years the winter allowances
were given from the 1st November to the
end of March. This winter they started
as usual on the 1st November but will
be continued to the end of April; and
they will be increased from about 2s a
week to 2s 6d a week in the average run
of cases, and to 3s 6d in households
where there is exceptional hardship. This
applies both to supplementary pensioners
and to those in receipt of assistance.
As regards the supply of clothing to
pensioners, the Assistance Board began
a special inquiry in July last and completed it, except for a few cases, by the
end of September. The upshot of this
inquiry is that the Board has distributed
£26,964 to 10,996 pensioners who were
found to be in need of additional clothing; and a further sum of £7,500 is earmarked to meet probable contingent
demands.
The inquiry also disclosed that in
4,334 cases there was a
shortage of
blankets, some wanting one blanket and
others two. The Board ordered 6,000
blankets which are expected to be delivered very shortly; and when delivered
they will be promptly distributed.
I now come to the increases in rates of
assistance and supplementary pensions
made by these regulations.
The basic rate of assistance for a householder and his wife, without other means,
Supplementary Pensions
Adjournment
15 DECEMBER 1942
was 26s a week. In 1939 this was raised
by 1s for each person, making 28s in all.
A further similar increase was granted
in 1940, raising the total to 30s a week.
Under the regulations before us an
additional 2s 6d per head is given, which
puts the income of the couple up to 35s
a
week. The rate for an adult single
person has gone through the same
changes. It was raised by 1s a week in
1939, a further 1s in 1940, and now by
an additional 2s 6d; and the rate for
each child under 16 was increased by 6d
a week in 1939, another 3d in 1940, and
now by an extra 1s.
The effect, then, of both these Regulations is to increase the rates paid to
those in receipt of unemployment assistance and supplementary pensions by 2s
Ed a week for each person of the age of
16 and upwards, and by 1s a week for
each person under that age.
These changes actually took place from
the 16th August last; but under the rele.-
vant Acts they have to be approved by
both Houses of Parliament within 12
months of that date. I am sure that
everybody here is glad that it has been
possible to make these improvments in
our social service.
I therefore beg to move, "That the.
Regulations made by the Ministry of
Labour on the 4th day of August, 1942,
under Section 33 of the Unemployment
Assistance Act (Northern Ireland), 1934,
as amended by the Determination of
Needs Act (Northern Ireland), 1941, be
hereby approved."
Mr. LESLIE: I think we might be
told how long these revised regulations
will remain in operation without further
interference, if anything is known about
that.
Mr. ROBB: I do not think I can
prophesy.
Mr. LESLIE: At all events they are
variable?
Mr. ROBB: Yes, they are variable.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

Unemployment Insurance (Contributions) (Agriculture) Order
(Northern Ireland), 1942.
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That the
Order in Council made by the Governor
of Northern Ireland on the 6th July,
1942, under Section 65 of the Unemployment Insurance Act (Northern Ireland),
1936, providing that an increase in the
rates of agricultural insurance contributions, which under Section 2 (2) (b) of
the Unemployment Insurance Act
(Northern Ireland), 1940, would have
come into force as from the 6th July,
1942, shall not become effective, be
hereby approved."
With regard to this third regulation,
what happened was this. Under our
Act of 1940, contributions in respect of
unemployment in agriculture should have
been, from 6th July, 1942, raised by a
halfpenny from the employer and a halfpenny from the employee. Investigation by the Statutory Committee of the
Agricultural Fund Account for the year
ending 1941 showed that there were ample
funds, and there still appear to be ample
funds, to meet the demands likely to be
made on the fund without this increase.
Accordingly an Order in Council has been
made to provide that the increase which
should have been made from 6th July,
1942, is not to come into effect.
Mr. LESLIE: Again the same question arises. The present 3d contribution
from the employer and the employee will
stand until it is varied again?
Mr. ROBB: That is so.

Question to Minister.
DESTRUCTION OF WOOD PIGEONS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie :
To ask the Leader of the House
whether, in view of the large numbers
of wood pigeons now present in
Northern Ireland, some arrangement
can be made whereby owners of woodlands can procure cartridges in quantities varying according to the extent of
the woodlands, to be used in promoting
the extinction of the pigeons.
Mr. LESLIE: I think the question on
the Paper almost explains itself. I ran
more or less out of cartridges the other
day, and I have tried to get what I understand is called the agricultural ration,
primarily for shooting pigeons. The
Ministry were good enough to take some
trouble about it, and I was given the
means of obtaining a packet of cartridges.
which I understand was my ration for the
season. I wanted them for wood pigeons,
and the amount of cartridges allotted for
the season was 25. It will occur to anybody who knows anything about wood
pigeons that that is not a very large
allowance. It will also be apparent that
at this time of the year pigeons ought
to be destroyed if possible for the protection of that winter wheat which is so much
desired, as well as of produce and vegetable
allotments and gardens.
It seems to me that it ought to be possible to make some arrangement whereby
out of the agricultural reserve of cartridges some larger apportionment than 25
should be made to individuals, particularly those who, whether they want them
or not, have certain woodlands of their
own, because the larger amount of woodland a man has, the greater will be the
number of pigeons he will be required to
entertain, and the more it will be in the
public interest that he should destroy
those pigeons. That being so, I thought
it well to put the question on the Paper
in order that we might hear what the
Government can tell us about it, what the
view of other people is, and the number of
pigeons they are expected to destroy with
25 cartridges.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): In
distributing the reserve supply of cartridges available in 1942 due regard has
been had to the need for the protection of
crops from the depredations of pigeons
and other pests.
The Minister of Agriculture is not
satisfied that the diversion of part of the
agricultural reserve of cartridges in proportion to the areas of woodlands would
be equitable as the number of pigeons
which find harbour in woodlands has no
direct relation to the size of such woodlands.
Under existing arrangements only a
small proportion of the cartridges available in Northern Ireland is distributed to
farmers through the Ministry. By far
the larger proportion is sold by cartridge
dealers to their customers in the ordinary
way.
Mr. LESLIE: How is it expected that
this winter wheat is to be grown and
brought to fruition if pigeons are allowed
to exercise unrestrained depredations
upon it? The size of the woodlands must
surely have some relation to the number
of pigeons.
If
Mr. ROBB: Not a direct relation.
the Ministry is going to issue cartridges.
to agriculturists in order to destroy

[Mr. Robb]

Ministry must appoint inspectors to see
that the farmers who destroy those

Scutch Mills and Flax (Fire Insurance)
Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."—(Mr. Robb).
Mr. ROBB: As scutch mills and flax
stored at such scutch mills are peculiarly
liable to outbreaks of fire. by reason of
the combustible nature of their contents,
and as rarely are such scutch mills or
their contents insured in the ordinary
way, the Government has noted with concern the loss sustained by scutch mill
owners and the owners of the flax by
reason of the many fires which have
occurred during the past season or two.
The great increase in the amount of
flax necessitates that every scutch mill
possible should be in operation, and consequently the. destruction by fire of a
number of scutch mills, were these not
reconstructed, would be a severe loss to
the national effort at the present time.
Consequently, the Government introduces
this Bill to provide for the establishment
and maintenance of funds for the payment of compensation in certain cases to
the owners of scutch mills and flax.
Hon. Members will note that the
main provisions of the Bill only apply to
scutch mills where the work consists
solely or mainly of scutching flax on commission, and flax means dam retted or
dew retted flax straw and does not include tow.
Briefly, the Bill contemplates the
setting up of two funds. (1) The Scutch
Mills Fund; and every person operating
a scutch mill, unless he is already fully
insured, shall pay to the Ministry by
way of contribution to this Fund such
sums calculated on output. These sums
will be calculated on the amount which
may reasonably be expected to meet all
claims. When such scutch mill is destroyed or damaged by fire, the owner
shall be entitled to be paid out of the
fund by way of compensation, such
amount not exceeding 50 per cent. of the
cost of reinstatement of the mill as the
Ministry may determine.
The second fund is the Flax Fund, and
similarly, every person on whose behalf
flax straw is scutched on commission at
a scutch mill or flax factory shall pay to
the Ministry such sums as may be prescribed and which will be based on the
amount which the Ministry may expect
to have to meet, by reason of any such
flax being destroyed by fire, in which case.
the owner of such flax will be paid out
of the Fund such amount calculated by
reference to the acreage from which the
flax was produced as may be determined.
295 Sentch Mills and Flax Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Robb]
Naturally, the funds and the amounts
which may be required to be paid will
not coincide at any given moment, and
consequently the Bill provides that temporary advances out of the consolidated
fund may be made to the funds as may
from time to time be required. It will
be noted that the contributions by the
owners of the scutch mills or the flax are
compulsory and consequently provision is
made in the Bill for the recovery of any
sums which a person has failed to pay.
The Bill also makes provision as to the
storage of flax and precautions against
fire, and this part of the Bill applies both
to scutch mills and flax factories. The
Regulations which the Ministry may
make under the Bill shall be laid before
each House of Parliament, which can
resolve that such Regulations shall be
annulled.
The Bill makes provision for those
cases where the damage is caused by malicious injury, and the owner recovers compensation under the Malicious Injury
Code. In such a case, as the owner will
have recovered from the rates the amount
of his loss, the Bill makes provision for
him to repay to the Ministry any sum
which has been paid to him under the
Bill.
I beg to move, "That this Bill be now
read a Second time."
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: May we take it that
this Bill is entirely agreed by representatives of the interests affected? If that is
so I do not think we need trouble either
ourselves or the. House to criticise it in
detail. There are, however, one or two
things in it that may occasion remark—
the question of compulsion and the question of what is to happen in the case of
criminal injury if the amount of the
damages does not correspond to the
amount otherwise recovered? What is
to happen to the fund if contributions
are not sufficient? It might become insolvent. If we can be assured it is an
agreed Bill we need not concern ourselves.
Mr. ROBB: I am informed that it is
an agreed Bill.
Mr. STEVENSON: I should like some
points cleared up.
Sub-clause (2) of Clause 3 states:
or
Whenever any flax in a scutch mill
or flax factory or on the mill or
factory premises is destroyed
damaged by fire, the owner of such
flax shall be entitled to be paid out of
the Flax Fund by way of compensation,
such amount, calculated by reference
to the acreage from which the flax so
destroyed or damaged was produced,
as the Ministry may determine.
Are we to understand that this is only
for part of the damage caused to the
flax? So far as I can see green flax is
omitted from the Bill. That being so,
firms like my own are not interested in
the matter as they are not covered in any
way. It would be a very great hardship on scutch mill owners on being paid
compensation for fires caused maliciously,
if they cover half from insurance, to
have to pay the full amount to the
Government. That does not seem to me
to be fair.
The question of who is going to make
up the deficit in the fund under certain
circumstances has also been raised. I
welcome the Bill because I believe it is
needed and will be of great assistance to
the trade. I am not raising any points
in a spirit of obstruction, but in order
to get them cleared up.

mean that a farmer will only get 50 per

Mr. ROBB: Out of the fund.

there is any machinery by which the flax

have not read the Bill, but if Senator
Stevenson is right in his interpretation.
of it the scutch mill owner who receives

injury claim does not get the benefit of

a
I
Mr. ROBB: Taking the last point first,
he would only have to repay to the Ministry such sums as the Ministry have paid
him. He is not going to be paid twice
over. With regard to the 50 per cent.
that is payable to the scutch mill owner,
there are two reasons for that. In the
first place, the fact that it is limited to 50
per cent. is a precaution against unnecessary fires, to which reference was made by
Senator Major Boyle. In the second
place, the mill owner who has had his
place destroyed may get the other 50 per
cent. by way of a Government loan repayable in ten years. This is only partial
insurance; not full insurance.
Mr. McALLISTER: Is it a Government grant, or loan?
Mr. ROBB: It is a loan, repayable in
ten years, but it is not in this particular
Bill. It arises in another way. With
regard to the point that Senator Stevenson
raised, the amount that the owner of flax,
as distinct from the owner of the mill, will
receive is determind by an estimate of the
flax that would probably have been grown
the acreage on which he has grown flax.
That has been accepted as a method less
calculated to cause disputes than any other
method. The acreage on which a man
grows flax is a thing that is known.
Whether it yielded much or little is a
matter that would be open to grave dispute, and the method that has been
adopted is to lay down the principle that
the compensation to be paid to the owner
of flax shall be calculated on the amount
of the acreage on which flax has been
grown, applying thereto a standard which
the Ministry will fix by reference to the
normal yield of flax on such land in that
particular area.
It is not, of course, an ideal method; it
may be a rough and ready method, but it
is the only practicable and reasonable way
of getting over the difficulty where there
is not a pre-valuation of the flax that has
been burned. Green flax is not covered
by the Bill because its handling is really
an industrial process, and this Bill deals
with flax scutching and not farming. I
think I have now answered all the points
that have been raised.
Mr. STEVENSON: I cannot agree
with the right hon. Gentleman regarding
compensation for malicious damage, because the Bill says definitely that any
compensation paid to such owner may be
recovered from him by the Ministry.
That means that if he gets one hundred
per cent. of the value of his place he has
got to hand it over to the Ministry.
Mr. SPEAKER: I am sorry to interrupt the hon. Member, but he is not
entitled to make a second speech on the
Second Reading without the permission of
the House.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Might I ask one
question? In all cases the amount of the
compensation is to be determined by the
Government. Is the Government to have
the last word, or is the farmer to have any
redress if he is not satisfied with the
amount of the compensation fixed by the
Government.
Mr. ROBB: The Ministry will formu
late Rules governing the payment of this

Agriculture Bill
[Mr. Robb]
compensation, and those Rules have to be
laid before Parliament.

Mr. ROBB: They can appeal when the
Rules come before this House.

Agriculture (Destruction of Vermin)
Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."--(Mr. Robb.)
Mr. ROBB: It is common knowledge
that widespread damage is being caused
to poultry and lambs by the depredations
of foxes, which have multiplied in recent
years. Experimental schemes of fox
destruction were tried for three years before the war in the several counties of
Northern Ireland and proved most successful in reducing the numbers of these
vermin. Under the schemes rewards were
paid from the funds at the disposal of
county committees of agriculture to persons who produced the dead animals to
authorised officers. The schemes ceased
to operate after the experimental stage
had been passed.
County councils, county committees of
agriculture, and the Ulster Farmers'
Union have been urging the Ministry of
Agriculture to provide the means of
financing similar schemes, and this is the
aim of the present Bill.
The chief features are as follows:
(a) Discretionary power is granted to
each county council to levy an annual
rate not exceeding d in the £ upon
occupiers in the rural areas of the
county, the produce of which would be
devoted entirely to meeting one half
the expenses of operating the scheme in
the county for the destruction of
vermin. The scheme at the outset will
be confined to foxes.
(b) The remaining one half of the
expenses would be a charge on Government funds. Thus to every £ raised
locally another £ would be added from
the Exchequer.
Agriculture Bill-
Second Reading
22 DECEMBER 1942
(c) Agricultural land would continue
to be de-rated in accordance with the
provisions of the Local Government
(Rating and Finance) Act (Northern
Ireland), 1929.
(d) The schemes would be operated
by the county committees of agriculture, and would be subject to the
approval of the Ministry of Agriculture.
Representatives of county councils have
agreed to the expenditure on schemes.
being met on a 50-50 principle as between
local authorities and the central Government. It is felt that the ordinary taxpayer is interested in the preservation of
food from the ravages of foxes, even
though the destruction of these pests is
primarily the concern of the food producer. For this reason a grant from the
Exchequer of a sum equal to one half the
expenditure on destruction schemes is regarded as equitable. Any sum raised
from the rates for the purposes of a
destruction scheme would, of course, not
rank for a derating grant.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: Whatever sentimental
sympathy may be felt for the fox in some
parts of England, I am afraid it must be
admitted that in Northern Ireland nobody has any sympathy with the fox at
all. Consequently, I imagine we are all
quite ready to accept this Bill for the
destruction of foxes. If the Bill stopped
there I do not think anything would be
said about it, but unfortunately the Bill
does not stop there. There is a nasty
little sub-clause (3) of Clause 2. It
reads:
(3) If at any time the Ministry is
satisfied with respect to any species.
of quadruped other than foxes, or with
respect to any species of bird, that, for
the purpose of preventing damage to
livestock, crops or agricultural land,
it is desirable that the said species.
should be included in a scheme for any
county, the Ministry may make an
order accordingly, and the scheme shall
have effect in relation to the said
species.
If the House passes that sub-clause it
will in effect be giving the Ministry
power directly to legislate. That is
giving the Ministry a power which, I
think, ought to belong to Parliament.
That is what we might describe as the
main objection. There are other objections.
One is that the insertion of that subclause here rather brings us up against
sundry other Acts of Parliament. I will
remind the House of the Acts I am referring to. First of all it may bring
you up against the various Acts for the
preservation of game, because the game
likely to be concerned will consist of deer
and hares. I do not think deer will
come into the picture in practice, but
there are a certain number of hares and
I do not think it would be desirable for
the Ministry to legislate as to their being
regarded as vermin unless they were
advised to do so. Again, there are other
classes of game, for instance birds, and
there are other classes of birds besides
game birds, birds which the Wild Birds
Protection Act of 1931 deals with. That
Act does not deal with game birds and
that makes things rather difficult.
There is still on the Statute Book-it
is not very operative, an Imperial
Statute of 1919-the Destruction of Rats
and Mice Act, which gives certain powers
to county councils for which they are more
or less responsible not to the Ministry of
Agriculture but to the Ministry of Home
Affairs. The Ministry of Home Affairs
came in as the ministerial authority responsible for the Wild Birds Preservation
Act, and this Clause would seem to bring
the two Ministries, Agriculture and
Home Affairs, into more or less possible
antagonism. I thought at first we might
get over the difficulty by a brief amendment saying that any order made by the
Minister under this sub-clause should be
limited to those birds which are excepted
in Category 1 of Part 1 of the First Schedule to the Wild Birds Protection Act.
There were some dozen or so species of
birds excepted from the special protection. Special protection was the phrase
used generally throughout the Act. These
classes of birds, so far as I can remember,
were, first of all, the rook, commonly
called the crow; the magpie, the house
sparrow, the bullfinch, two kinds of
gull, the cormorant and wild pigeons.
In the list of birds I have mentioned
you will find all that are likely to be
objected to and likely to be described as
vermin in so far as you can describe a
Agriculture Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
bird as vermin, and I suppose you can.
That is one of our difficulties, that there is
no general statutory definition of vermin.
Whether an animal or a creature is to be
classed as vermin or not seems to depend
on habit rather than on anything else.
For instance, the fox may come in as
being an noxious beast, but when you
consider the otter I am not so sure about
whether you regard him as vermin. The
weasel certainly is vermin, but what about
the ferret or our friend the red squirrel?
He is scarcely vermin, but he is not
entitled to the protection of the game
laws. The grey squirrel brings us into a
different category. I was about to mention him specially, but for the moment
I thought we might deal with the matter
by limiting ourselves to protection of
birds other than those I have mentioned
as excepted under the Wild Birds Protection Act. I do not think on any
consideration that it goes far enough.
We have got to do something about game
as well as the wild birds.
There is also the other Act which I
have mentioned-the Destruction of
Rats and Mice Act. I think I have
already said what I want to say about it.
County councils have power to meet the
expenses, if any, by raising money out
of their poor rate. That is not quite the
form of recoupment, so to speak, brought
about in the Bill before us.
The other Bill I was thinking of was
the Destructive Imported Animals Act,
1933, the one we passed chiefly in connection with the, musk rat. In that Act
power was given to the Ministry to include other noxious creatures under certain conditions. Notice had to be given
before it was done there is no word
about notice in this Bill-and also any
Order made has to be laid before Parliament for the statutory period. If either
House of Parliament object the Order
falls to the ground. That method preserves what I might describe as Parliamentary control over the action of the
Ministry.
There is no indication of such Parliamentary control in the Bill before us.
Consequently, it seems to me that we
shall have to deal at a later stage in some
way or other with sub-clause (3) which,
as I say, is the one thing which to my
mind obstructs the. Bill. There are
various methods of doing it. One could
.
introduce a Clause like the one I have
referred to in the Destructive Imported
Animals Bill requiring the order to be
laid before Parliament. One cannot
leave out the thing altogether and I am
not sure that that would not be the best
course, but one hesitates to suggest that
because there are certain quadrupeds-I
am thinking about the rabbit-for which
it might be desirable to do something.
There are, of course, rats and mice, but
I think they are sufficiently dealt with
by the Destruction Act of 1919. I do
not think it necessary to say anything
about them, although it might be we
ought to introduce into this Bill a subclause saying there, is nothing in this Bill
to be regarded as derogatory to the
powers conferred in that Act.
There is, as the House will remember,
a section in the Act of 1919 saying that
the powers given there are not to be regarded as a derogation of any powers
given to any Government Department or
local authority. It might be thought that
we should leave that alone and confine our
attention to birds other than those
excepted and such four-footed animals as
are not covered by the game laws.
There are those two possible courses we
ought to take, and it is for the consideration of those two possible courses that we
ought not to unduly rush this Bill. As regards the destruction of foxes I agree
that they are a great nuisance, but they
have one merit and I should have thought
the Ministry of Agriculture would have
recognised it. Where you have a multitude of foxes you do not have a multitude
of rabbits. I think it is agreed in England that so long as a fox can get rabbits
he will eat nothing else. He prefers a
rabbit warren to a hen run.
There are other things which do mischief. I imagine geese and goats if they
escape from proper custody could do as
much damage to crops as anything else.
The goose, and, perhaps, the goat each
serve a useful purpose which the fox does
not, Nobody would suggest that the
Ministry would be likely to class either
a goose or a goat as vermin. The fox
does destroy certain things, and there
have been cases of the destruction of
lambs. There have been more suspected
cases than established ones, but there is
a general feeling in the country that the
fox does destroy lambs. I have heard
the same complaint in England.

I hope the House will pass this Bill,
but that it will keep it here long enough
to enable us to consider what is to be
done in regard to sub-clause (3) of Clause
2. For the purpose of that consideration
I do not think we ought to meet either
to-morrow or even next week. I do not
oppose the Second Reading of the Bill.
I
Sir EMERSON HERDMAN: I can
safely say that I have been at the death
of more foxes than anybody else in Northern Ireland. For the past fifty years,
with the exception of the time I was serving during the last war and now when I
cannot get about, I have spent my winters.
in pursuit of foxes. I agree most heartily
with the hon. and gallant Member in the
lower House who stated that a fox hunt
was the greatest enemy of the fox.
notice in the Press that the Minister of
Agriculture in England made the same
statement in the British House of Commons last week. The suggestion that
there should be a pack of fox hounds is
not such a preposterous statement as one
might imagine, but I think it was not
right that he should have omitted you
from that hunt. If you could not have
accepted the position, the hon. Senator
for Fermanagh would, I think, have
gladly taken your place. He would look
a very fine figure wearing a scarlet coat
and sitting on a horse.
I need not tell you anything about the
habits of the fox. As you well know, he
lies snugly underground during the day
and marauds and makes love by night.
In countries where they have a fox hunt
the animals are kept within bounds. Not
only are the numbers kept down, but the
hunt pays for any destruction they do in
their neighbourhood. There are no fox
hunts in Northern Ireland. The great
trouble about foxes is the fact that they
go to ground. In countries where there
are fox hunts expense is occasioned by
stopping up all holes, so that when they
come back from the night's depredations
they have to lie in open ground. This
enables the hunt to seek them out. Every
known hole and crevice in the surrounding
country has to be closed, otherwise they
can, after the hounds have got them on
the run, get away, especially in mountainous districts.
This season we have killed 1 brace of
foxes, but if we had been able to stop up
the holes we would have killed no fewer
than ten brace. I wonder the Minister of
Agriculture did not think of this matter
himself, seeing he is a keen fox hunter.
A small grant to hunts, hunting foxes in
Northern Ireland would be an excellent
way of helping to get rid of these
animals.
Mr. MCALLISTER: There is one
defect in this Bill, and that is caused by
the way in which it is framed. It is not
made compulsory on county councils to
put this Bill into operation. When the
measure is passed one county council may
operate it, while an adjoining county
council can refuse to do so.
The Bill, as mentioned by Senator
Leslie, is going to give the Government
very drastic powers in so far as bringing
in rules and regulations is concerned. I
am not casting any reflection on the Civil
Service I have the greatest respect for
its members and find them competent and
anxious to act as civil servants-but its
members are human, and we should not
delegate powers to them which should be
carried through by Parliament. Subclause (3) of Clause 2 states:
If at any time the Ministry is satisfied with respect to any species of
quadruped other than foxes, or with
respect to any species of bird, that, for
the purpose of preventing damage to
livestock, crops or agricultural land, it
is desirable that the said species should
be included in a scheme for any county,
the Ministry may make an order
accordingly, and the scheme shall have
effect in relation to the said species.
That includes every species of quadruped
-domestic animals as well as foxes, rats
and such things. Between this and the
Committee stage of the Bill the Minister
should consider the wording of that subclause and try to make it more practical
and more applicable to the terms of the
Bill. It is very loosely framed, and if
interpreted in its present form would be
a very drastic measure. I have not had
an opportunity of considering the Bill,
but I would ask the Leader of the House
to consider the points raised before the
Committee stage is taken.
Mr. BARCLAY: I understand that
this invasion of foxes in Northern Ireland
has come from over the Border. What
can we do to keep them on their own side

Major BOYLE: I would like to support the Bill in every way. At the same
time I am sorry that it is permissive and
not compulsory. If the county councils.
were compelled to adopt a scheme and
attack the fox where he breeds, which is
the simplest way of getting at him.
it would be an effective check on foxes.
I would ask the Government to ask
county councils to attack the destruction
of foxes in that manner. I would also
have liked to have seen our friend the
rat included in the Bill. The best way
to kill rats is to get at them where they
breed, which are the refuse dumps. I
understand a couple of rats will produce
one thousand rats in a year.
Major DOBBS: I am quite prepared
to support the Bill, but I would have
liked to have seen a very much stronger
line taken. It should be obligatory on
the county councils to promote schemes
for the destruction of vermin. I do not
like the title of the Bill, because it
emphasises the destruction done by foxes.
There is a certain amount of destruction
done by foxes in certain parts of the
country, but far greater damage is
caused by rats. That being so I should
like this Bill to be called not a fox destruction bill but a fox and vermin destruction bill. For every fox in this
country there are something like ten
thousand rats. The number of rats has
increased enormously in the last few years
due largely to the increased acreage that
has been tilled. Consequently, there is
a tremendous increase in rat food, grain
and so on. Another point is that many
people do not realise the size of some of
these rats. A rat caught a few days
ago near where I live was as big as a
rabbit. I was speaking to an American
doctor and he told me that his camp
was overrun by rats. He said he had
never seen rats of such a size; they were
as big as cats. I should like this Bill
to enforce the destruction of rats, and so
save the food of the country both in the
stook and in the barn.

Mr. ROBB: With regard to the question of compulsion, I think I made it quite
clear that this Bill has been introduced at
the request of the county councils. If
they have requested to bring in a Bill to
give them this power, presumably they are
going to use it. As to the point Senator
McAllister made with regard to the Civil
Service, it is not the Civil Service which
will administer this Bill but the county
councils. They are the people who will
frame. schemes which will be worked
through the county committees of agriculture. It is quite true that schemes
must be approved by the Ministry, but
the types of vermin to be destroyed will
be suggested by the county councils, and
the Ministry will approve because they
will be paying half the cost.
With reference to the point made by
Senator Sir Emerson Herdman, there is
nothing in the Bill to prevent grants
being made to hunts. It is a matter for
the county council. If rats are such a
plague as has been stated there already
exists power under the Rats and Mice
Act, 1919, to deal with them. It was
not contemplated that rats should be
dealt with in this Bill because there is
ample power to carry out any schemes
under the other Act. Furthermore, I
am informed--although I do not know
how far it is true-that farmers prefer
to deal with rats themselves rather than
have a Government rat catcher on their
premises. The point made by Senator
Leslie with regard to sub-clause (3) of
Clause 2 can be dealt with in committee.

Agriculture (Destruction of Vermin)
Bill.
Order of the Day for the House to be
put into Committee read.
Resolved: "That this House do now
resolve itself into Committee on the said
Bill." (Mr. Robb.)
In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair.]
CLAUSE 1 (Expenses of schemes for
destruction of vermin).
(1) The county council of any county
may, if they think fit, resolve to raise
money to be applied towards the expenses of carrying out a scheme under
this Act for the destruction of vermin
in their county.
Major DOBBS: I beg to move to leave
out sub-section (1) and insert the following sub-section:
(1) The county council of any county
shall prepare and carry out a scheme
for the destruction of the foxes, rats
and vermin in their county: they may
for this purpose, if they think fit, resolve
to raise money to be applied towards
the expenses of carrying out such
scheme under this Act.
I am sure that I am voicing the
thoughts of everyone here when I say
how very glad we are to see the Minister
of Agriculture present to help us in our
deliberation. This Bill, as it stands, is a
very lame Bill and a very half-hearted
one, as I and several hon. Members
pointed out on the Second Reading.
This sub-section is, to my mind, hopelessly weak. The Minister of Agriculture
issued a Christmas appeal for a strenuous
production of more food. Well, that is
splendid as far as it goes, but he does not
take the slightest trouble to ensure that
that food, when it is grown, shall be saved
from destruction by vermin. He has
a real opportunity to do so if he will take.
hold of this Bill and make it a real Bill,
not the poor measure it is at present. If
he supports my Amendment, it will at
any rate show the farmers that he is
trying to help them. He should make
the Bill a live. Bill and make an effort
to exterminate everything that destroys
our food, whether that food is live stock
or crops.
The Leader of the House, when explaining this measure, said that the scheme
at the outset will be confined to foxes.
Why to foxes? The title of the Bill is
"Destruction of foxes and other species
of vermin." If he had left it at foxes
that would be all right. I would have
nothing to say to bringing in other
species of vermin. Why does he not
make it quite clear that rats and pigeons
should be destroyed? Why should not
the scheme start with them just as much
as foxes? They do far more damage to
our food than foxes do. I want to see
foxes, rats and pigeons destroyed, and I
think that would be done if my Amendment is accepted. It would go a long
way towards it at any rate.
This question is most important to
farmers. Farmers have seen rats on their
holdings increase ten-fold in the last two
years. I am sure ten-fold is a very conservative estimate. A farmer cannot go
far in exterminating rats, because if he
destroys all the rats in his fields, in a
Agriculture Bill-
Committee
29 DECEMBER 1942
very short time those fields will be invaded by the rats from the fields of his
neighbouring farmers. It is not a question for an individual, but for every
effort that can be brought about by the
Minister of Agriculture. There is another
point. The hon. and gallant Senator
(Major Boyle) pointed out on the Second
Reading that you want to clear refuse
dumps of rats. When you are clearing
refuse dumps, you want to clear all derelict ground and quarries which are
breeding places for rats.
I want the word "may" in this subclause deleted, and the word "shall" inserted in its place. The Ministry of
Agriculture and the county councils must
face up to the question of the destruction
of rats. I shall be told that there is the
Rats and Mice (Destruction) Act, 1919,
but I do not know of one single rat or
one single mouse having been destroyed
in a rural district in County Antrim
owing to the passing of that Act,
although it has been in force for more
than 20 years.
Pigeons also do a tremendous amount
of damage. For many years they have
visited us in the late autumn, but in the
past the numbers were small.
Last year,
however, they came in hundreds of thousands. If one farmer kills a large
number-I do not see how he can do so
at the present time owing to the fact
that he is allowed only 25 cartridgesthe remainder leave his land and go to
holdings where, perhaps, they are not
disturbed. In this way very little is
done to reduce the enormous number in
the country. We look to the Ministry
of Agriculture to rid the country of
pigeons. I ask that this Amendment be
passed so that by the destruction of
vermin-rats, pigeons and foxes-food
may be protected. I beg to move the
Amendment standing in my name.
The MINISTER OF AGRICULTURE
(Lord Glentoran): The hon. Senator in
his very able speech fell into the same
mistake as did hon. Members in another
place. I do not think he has clearly
before his mind the fact that this is
purely an enabling Bill; that it is a
Measure which has been demanded by
the farmers for the past three or four
years. This is a matter which we tried
to get the county councils to take up,
but until we came to the present arrangement we could not get them to agree. The
area controlled by the County Council of
Antrim suffered particularly heavy depredations by foxes, and that council
agreed to take action, but others would
not. We were able to get agreement on
only one question-foxes. That was all
that was asked for, but now the hon.
Senator comes to the House and wants
us to add rats and pigeons. At no meeting of farmers which I attended was a
demand ever made for a large number of
rat killers to be sent out to take charge
of farms or to prosecute farmers if they
did not do what certain people thought
they should.
I am glad that the hon. Senator raised
this question, because it is one of great
importance. Only yesterday in papers I
received from England I was reading
similar complaints that rats have increased one hundred fold. That must be
so, because there are stacks of oats in
nearly every field. We have had a very
open winter, and there has been an influx of rats into the country.
The Ulster farmers-it is not for me
to disagree with them-say the best
people to deal with the killing of rats
are the farmers, and that they are well
able to do so. It would be very difficult
for the Government or myself to ask the
county councils to increase the rates in
order to pay for a large number of people
to go round farmers' houses killing rats.
At no farmers' meeting I ever attended
was it suggested that people should be
sent to kill rats. It would be a very
difficult thing to do. From the speeches
delivered in another place one would
think that in England the rats are killed
on farms free of charge to the farmer. The
agricultural war committees have devised
schemes, but they insist upon the farmer
paying 1s for each acre freed from rats.
If I asked the farmers in County Antrim
to do that they would chase me off their
farms.
The hon. Senator mentioned the Act
of 1919. The relevant section of that Act
is very clear. It says-
Any person who shall fail to take
such steps as may from time to time be
necessary and reasonably practicable for
the destruction of rats and mice on or
in any land of which he is the occupier,
or for preventing such land from becoming infested with rats or mice,

a fine not exceeding five pounds, or,
where he has been served with a notice
under this Act requiring him to take
such steps,
not exceeding twenty
pounds.

Mr. LESLIE: Like my hon. and
gallant Friend, I am very pleased to see
Agriculture Bill---
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
the noble Lord the Minister of Agriculture here this morning. I was very pleased
that he came so early, so as to join in our
prayers. I am not sure that that is not
a record so far as Ministers who do not
sit in this House are concerned.
The MINISTER OF AGRICULTURE
(Lord Glentoran): I may need it.
Mr. LESLIE: I cannot agree with
that. I do not care very much about the
amendment itself, the point being that
it is an attempt to get away from what
I must describe as the hopeless generalisation which has been introduced into this
unfortunate sub-clause (3) of Clause 2.
If sub-clause (3) had been differently
arranged I think that possibly we might
have avoided trouble, but it is drawn in
the most general terms possible. The
Clause says: "If the Minister is satisfied
in respect of any species of quadruped
other than foxes, or with respect to
any species of bird." No quadruped and
no bird is without that distinction. I
am not going quite so far as the hon.
Senator in front of me and suggesting that
the Bill would be construed to include
domestic animals, but it comes very near
to that. The domestic animal, after all,
is an animal, but I thought the general
tenor of the Bill was sufficient to kill that.
Therefore I have not said anything in the
amendment about domestic animals.
The Amendment is directed, first of
all, to getting down, more or less, to
brass tacks, and getting away from
generalisation, and particularly to keeping this Bill to the Ministry of Agriculture's proper business. The Ministry of
Agriculture, I may remind the Committee, has no power whatever under the
Rats and Mice (Destruction) Act. The
noble Lord has got the Act in front of
him and he can satisfy himself as to that.
This Committee seems to have thought
that the administration of the Rats and
Mice (Destruction) Act in Northern
Ireland was part of the functions of the
Ministry of Agriculture. It is not.
Therefore I am not saying anything in
my Amendment about rats. I do not want
to say more than I need say about birds,
because the protection of wild birds is
again the function of the Ministry of
Home Affairs and not of the Ministry of
Agriculture. I am obliged to say something about game, partly because hares
are the function of the Ministry of Home
Affairs; partly because hares and deer
are animals; and also because the word
"birds" includes wild birds and game
birds.
I do not think sub-clause (3) was ever
intended to include game at all, but, unfortunately, I am afraid it has done it.
Therefore I think we must get away from
it, and the simplest plan I could think of
was by putting in a proviso which makes
it clear that any Order shall not interfere with any of the game laws or any
protection afforded under the Wild Birds
Act. That means that the Ministry's
function will be confined to dealing with
those wild birds which are excepted from
the general provisions of the Wild Birds
Act. Those birds, I reminded the House
on the Second Reading, include the rook,
the wood pigeon, the magpie, the
starling, the sparrow hawk, the Irish
jay, the bullfinch, and the cormorant.
Those birds are entirely out of the
protection of the Wild Birds Act, and,
therefore, there is no reason why the
Ministry of Agriculture should not deal
with them.
The sub-clause does not tell us who is
going to start a scheme-whether the
Minister will start it off his own bat, or
whether it will be put up to him by the
county councils. That is left in the air,
but it does not matter. Rats will come
under the Bill if that sub-clause goes
through as it is now, but they are not,
under the present law, the function of
the Ministry of Agriculture; they belong,
as I say, to the Ministry of Home Affairs,
but rats, being quadrupeds, it would be
possible for a scheme to be made under
this sub-clause. However, there has not
been any yet. There are only five, or
perhaps six, quadrupeds to which that
sub-clause could relate. The only ones
I can think of are, first of all, the
badger-although he has some advocates
-then the mouse, then, I am afraid, the
otter, and the rat and the rabbit. That
is all the possible quadrupeds to which
a scheme could apply, apart from the fox.
As it is, I think we are bound to limit
the possible effect of that sub-clause, and
the shortest way I can think of is as I
have done. I should not have had to
put that amendment down if the subclause had been drawn in words which
were not sufficiently wide to run counter
to all existing legislation. When you get
an Act of Parliament dealing with the
Agriculture Bill—
Committee
29 DECEMBER 1942
same subject matter as previous Acts of
Parliament there is always a doubt as to
whether the later Act is intended and
shall be construed as overriding the
earlier one or not. When there is a
doubt care is generally taken in the Bill
to make it clear what the intention is,
but in this case, although the intention
was not to include either game or wild
birds, the words used are so wide as to
bring them in, and to prevent that
happening is the intention of this proviso.
""
Lord GLENTORAN: I regret that I
cannot accept this amendment. At the
same time, I think I can say something
to clarify the position in regard to the
matter the right hon. and learned Senator
complains of. The words in the Bill,
"for the purpose of preventing damage,
strictly limit my power to destroying
only those vermin that do damage. I
can assure the right hon. and learned
Senator that there is nothing in this Bill
that would enable me to destroy birds,
even if I so desired, and there is nothing
I desire less than to interfere in any
way with the Wild Birds Protection Act,
which is an admirable Act in every way.
I would remind the Committee that even
game under the Wild Birds Protection
Act have to be dealt with by me for
causing the destruction of crops.
Last summer we had to deal with
hares, and a short time ago I had to
order pheasants in a certain part of
County Antrim to be destroyed because
they were ruining the crops on a man's
land. I have had to step in and have
these birds destroyed because they were
causing damage to crops. As Minister
of Agriculture that is the only condition
on which I can destroy anything-that
it is doing damage to the food of the
nation. I hope the right hon. and
learned Senator will agree that the words
in the Bill limit my powers in this
respect, and that he will accept my
assurance that I have no intention of
going beyond that power.
Mr. LESLIE: The noble Lord spoke
about an Order made about some
pheasants and so on. I think he was

shooting season were made, as in England,
as a war measure for special reasons. The
other point I will deal with later.

Mr. LESLIE: I am still left in the.

so very wide. The question of whether
damage is being done is to be, settled by
whom? By the Minister? He has got
to be satisfied. We are not told who is
to satisfy him, or how long it will take
to satisfy him. I am not particularly
keen on this particular form of words,
and the Minister has told us that he does
not mean what is said in the Bill. That
is what it comes to. I think the Committee will probably be satisfied not to
insert these words, but I think they ought
to say what they really think. The position is not clear. The Bill says one.
thing, and does not mean what it says.
It may be thought that the next amendment standing in my name will be, sufficient, but I am not sure of that in the
circumstances. In view of the very wide.
term, "any species of bird," the Committee ought to say whether they are
satisfied to let it go through or whether

words.

THE SENATE

anything previously done thereunder or

This is an amendment with some
difference to the previous one, and I must
go the whole way with it. The point
raised is whether the country is going to
be governed by Parliament or to be
governed by departments. All I am
asking for here is the insertion of
a very simple clause that any Order made
shall be laid before each House of
Parliament. In doing that I am following the course taken in the Destruction of
Imported Animals Act, which is under
the control of the Ministry of Agriculture. That was an Act which might
almost have been made to serve all purposes under this Bill. However, it does
not. The Destruction of Imported
Animals Act had two provisions. In the
first place, in regard to musk-rats, any
Order made had to be laid before Parliament. There was a second case of a new
imported animal which made its appearance in Northern Ireland, and steps could
be taken to have it destroyed by making
an Order, but this was not to come into
effect unless it had been approved of by
either House. That was a stronger form.
All that I am asking in this case is that
any Order made shall be laid before
Parliament, and that gives either House
the opportunity during a limited period.
to draw attention to that Order and take
such steps as Parliament might see fit.
I
That is a mild check, but it is a mild
check which can be converted into a
strong check on the activities of a department. It means that a department cannot do something outside the ordinary
law unless Parliament supports it.
must press this Amendment. I think I
have explained it fully. It is a perfectly
ordinary clause, and I think it ought to
go in.
You have a precedent in the
Destruction of Imported Animals Act
before you.
Lord GLENTORAN: In refusing to

The point is that we are pressed to get

You can kill foxes in the early spring,
but once it gets past spring it is an impossibility to eradicate them. Therefore, it
is most essential and pressing to meet the
wishes of the farmers all over the country
and get the Bill through at once, so that

There are a hundred ways you can do it.

TITLE (A Bill to provide for the

Major DOBBS: I beg to move that
after the word "foxes" the word "rats"
be inserted. I cannot find any justification for calling foxes vermin. Stoats,
polecats, badgers, rats and mice appear
to be vermin. It is only a small point,
and I am not pressing it. By this
Amendment I want to emphasise that
rats are really of more importance than
foxes. Even if foxes are vermin, we do
not want to single them out under this
Bill-we. want all vermin destroyed.
Mr. LESLIE: As regards the question of whether a fox is vermin or not,
I told the House on the Second Reading
that there is no statutory definition of
"vermin" anywhere. The only way you
can decide if a beast is vermin or not is
on the basis of whether he has noxious
habits. A fox, from the agricultural
point of view, is a noxious animal, and
therefore is vermin. He is much more
difficult to decide about than my friend the
otter or the badger, but I think the fox
is vermin. I think the popular idea of
vermin is a bird or a beast which at death
is fastened to a nail outside a keeper's
cottage. Anything you find there is
vermin. Certainly you would not find a
fox there.
Mr. SPEAKER: Does the hon. and
gallant Member wish to press the. Amendment?
Major DOBBS: I do not.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Title agreed to.
